HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS: Bermuda Takes theRisk;
From Tourist Paradise to Haven for Insurance Business

BYLINE: By JOSEPH B. TREASTER 

DATELINE: HAMILTON, Bermuda

 BODY:
The construction project in the middle of town tells the tale of how things have been going lately on this lovely island of pastel bungalows and perpetual spring. The grand old Bermudiana Hotel and its luxuriant gardens have been leveled; in their place two big insurance buildings are rising.
With relentless competition and changing tastes, Bermuda's tourist business has been fading. But with no corporate taxes and light regulation, the island has quietly developed a booming insurance business, making Bermuda the third leg with New York and London in the world's central insurance triangle. 
 In the low-rising, vaguely dated village of Hamilton, underwriters, actuaries and brokers are the new princes. The resident lawyers and accountants depend heavily on insurance business, and the construction trades have seldom been busier. Not far from the Bermudiana site, an even bigger block of insurance offices is just being completed. At night, tables at Little Venice and the pricier Cafe Lido are filled with insurance executives and their clients.
"We'd be lost without the insurance people," said Tullio Lombardi, an assistant manager at the beach-front Cafe Lido.
And a lot of insurance people would be lost without Bermuda.
Much as Liberia and Panama have provided flags of convenience for the world's maritime fleets, Bermuda has blossomed as a convenient extension of the American insurance industry. A self-governing British colony, it has generally managed to steer clear of money laundering, drug smuggling and most of the fly-by-night financial scams that have tarnished other so-called tax havens.
And it has attracted a cadre of insurance technicians from the United States and Europe that, as Government business recruiters like to point out, adds to the ease of setting up shop here.
Bermuda is the land of megadeals, where corporate policies are written that often kick in only after losses hit more than $100 million. For the buyers and sellers in this highflying world, Bermuda's oversight is a model of restraint. Here, the issue is financial strength. The details of day-to-day business are between the insurers and their customers.
With that philosophy, Bermuda has become a place where new twists in coverage can be worked out in a few days -- or at worst -- a few weeks, rather than the months, if not years it takes in the United States. Its laissez-faire supervision contrasts sharply with the thickets of regulation that have grown up in all 50 states with the goal -- rarely fully realized -- of protecting the smallest policyholder rather than oiling the wheels of commerce.
Bermuda's overall success has recently inspired regulators in the United States to begin easing up. Half a dozen states have agreed to let corporate buyers and sellers negotiate the terms and prices of high-level coverage just as all coverage is handled here. And similar legislation is being considered in New York and a dozen other states.
The regulatory and tax advantages of Bermuda -- just an hour and a half by air from New York -- made it a natural for solving the industry's problems. When heavy losses caused even Lloyd's of London to back away from offering high-ticket, high-risk coverage against product liability lawsuits and the ravages of hurricanes and earthquakes, Americans with deep pockets and a sense of opportunity turned to Bermuda to set up companies that would consider almost any proposition.
Bermuda's main customers are America's corporate giants, whose transactions here mean less money for insurers in the United States. Yet the big American insurers are not complaining. Indeed, they helped create Bermuda's insurance business. Some have profited by setting up their own subsidiaries here; many others have become buyers of catastrophe reinsurance, which at first was available almost nowhere else and now helps them diversify.
Still others have been delighted to have Bermuda sharing the market for stratospheric coverages so they are not faced with either taking on more risk than they can comfortably handle or disappointing corporate clients.
So, out of self-interest, American insurers have quietly accepted the rise of Bermuda. "The other insurers need Bermuda as much as Bermuda needs them," said Sharda Cherwoo, an insurance specialist at Ernest & Young, the international accounting firm.
Bermudian companies pioneered financial coverage that pays off on fluctuations of foreign currency rates and commodities prices, often packaged with protection against, say, plant fires and lawsuits. And the Bermudian titans -- Ace Ltd. and X. L. Capital Ltd. -- have begun extending their reach. They have turned the tables on their elders, buying up older -- and sometimes bigger -- companies in the United States and prowling among the Lloyd's syndicates that once tried to use their influence to crush the island upstarts.
In the slimmed-down, customized world of Bermudian insurance, there are fewer protections for corporate customers. For example, most Bermudian policies require that disputes be worked out in binding arbitration, rather than in the courts. Also in the United States, insurers are required to contribute to a so-called guarantee fund in each state that provides money to pay claims if an insurance company collapses. In Bermuda, there is no guarantee fund.
But Bermuda has no shortage of customers. For many of its clients, the ability to work out new coverage quickly to solve an immediate insurance problem and to take on enormous risk outweighs concern about a forum for resolving disputes that may never materialize. For big corporations with policies in the tens of millions of dollars, American guarantee funds are of little value anyway because they are often worth only a few hundred thousand dollars, little help if an insurance company fails to pay off a multimillion loss.
Moreover, oversight does not end with the regulators. Many of the Bermudian companies are publicly traded in the United States and subject to the reporting requirements of the stock exchanges and of the Securities and Exchange Commission.
The rating agencies periodically examine the books of all major insurers, including the Bermudians, and publish their findings on financial strength, which provides a powerful measure of their ability to pay claims. "A company with a low rating is just not going to get any business," said Barbara Stewart, an insurance consultant in Atlanta.
The foundations for Bermuda's insurance industry were laid shortly after World War II. That was when American corporations realized they could save money with a paper device known as a captive insurance company, which enables a corporation to effectively sell itself insurance and deduct the premiums from its taxable income. And for tax and regulatory reasons as well as Bermuda's reputation for probity, many American corporations began registering their captives here. Today, there are roughly 1,400 captives in Bermuda, more than anywhere else in the world.
But it took a few decades before the first big mainstream Bermudian insurance companies were created. They began in the mid-1980's with New York money, knowledge and technical skill. When traditional insurers cut back on high-end coverage, Robert Clements, a senior executive at the Marsh & McLennan Companies, the world's largest insurance broker, persuaded 34 corporations, including I.B.M., Ford Motor and Royal Dutch/Shell, to go offshore to Bermuda and start their own insurance company. That was how Ace was started. Six months later, Mr. Clements brought together other corporations to form Exel Ltd., now X. L. Capital. It is Ace and X. L. that are building the new offices that are going up on the grounds of the Bermudiana Hotel.
As a prerequisite for the new companies, Mr. Clements roughed out a new kind of liability policy that carved a middle path between the most restrictive and most liberal policies.
In the early 1990's, big hurricane and earthquake losses convinced major insurance companies that they should be buying a lot more reinsurance. But traditional reinsurers, who provide backup coverage to the front-line insurers, did not want to put more chips into a game that was looking increasingly dangerous. So Mr. Clements dusted off his old blueprints and started Midocean Reinsurance Ltd. to sell nothing but coverage for other insurers against the worst calamities. Eventually, eight catastrophe reinsurance companies were created.
Others' disasters were a timely salve for Bermuda's economy. For decades, Bermuda has been like a big country club for the East Coast golf and tennis set. It also has been a favorite of honeymooners. But Bermuda's popularity is waning. Visitors in recent years have complained that the island is horribly expensive -- a couple of sandwiches and drinks can easily run $40. While peace and quiet were once big selling points for Bermuda, visitors today often complaint that there is not much to do.
The economic balance tipped in favor of insurance and other international business three years ago and since then the gap has been widening. In the last fiscal year, insurance and other international businesses pumped $757.6 million into Bermuda compared with $472.3 million from tourism.
Partly because of the stagnation of tourism, Bermudians last fall voted out the United Bermuda Party, which governed for 35 years. The new Prime Minister, Jennifer Smith of the Progressive Labor Party, is in a delicate political position. She must encourage the growth of insurance, but she must not be seen to be favoring it over tourism -- even though there is some overlap -- because even now, tourism provides more jobs. By her count 2,400 people work in insurance and nearly 6,000 have jobs in hotels, restaurants and other businesses in the tourist trade.
As practiced in Bermuda, insurance is a fast-paced business. The companies have small staffs. There is a lot of give and take and a blurring of ranks that often make the companies seem more like research centers than like hierarchical insurance concerns. Ties, and sometimes even shoes, are optional at some companies.
While the insurers would like to do more local hiring, most of their highest-paying jobs go to fairly young expatriates, attracted by money, a balmy climate and the opportunity to work on big, important transactions. After work on Fridays, underwriters, actuaries and brokers crowd into the Robin Hood, a pub on Richmond Road, to let off steam.
They bring their energy to what was once a pretty sleepy place.
"They've got a certain amount of sassiness and arrogance coupled with real entrepreneurial spirit," said Bill McGannon, who, for years, was in charge of the insurance budget of the Nova Chemical Corporation in western Canada. "They don't come up with a lot of reasons they can't give you what you need. They want to do a deal."

HEADLINE: Park Place To Buy Casinos From Starwood

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
In a deal that will reshape the gambling business in Atlantic City and Las Vegas, Nev., Park Place Entertainment, a recent spinoff from Hilton Hotels, agreed yesterday to acquire the Caesars casino operations of Starwood Hotels and Resorts for $3 billion in cash.
The deal will create a serious rival in Atlantic City to Donald J. Trump, where Park Place will have three casinos with nearly as much gambling space as Mr. Trump's, while casinos owned by Park Place Entertainment in Las Vegas will dominate three of the four corners at the Strip and Flamingo Boulevard, the most valuable gambling real estate site on earth. 
 Shares of Park Place jumped nearly 25 percent yesterday, closing at $11.375, up $2.25, while Starwood fell 68.75 cents, to $34.3125.
Analysts said that Park Place's cash flow, or earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, would be about $1.3 billion, nearly twice that of the next largest gambling company, Harrah's Entertainment.
For Starwood, the world's largest hotel company, the cash payment will allow it to reduce its $8 billion of debt by more than one-third, lowering interest charges enough to increase earnings by about 7 cents a share in the first year, said Jim Gallagher, a Starwood spokesman. "This will bring us much closer to our goal of being able to issue investment-grade debt," he said.
He said that the price "might represent a small loss" for Starwood on the price it paid plus improvements to the casino properties, which it acquired 17 months ago.
Others estimated the loss at more than $500 million based on the purchase price and investments Starwood made.
Arthur M. Goldberg, Park Place's chief executive, said: "By acquiring the Caesars brand name and related customer data base, we plan to heavily cross-market between our destination resorts. This will boost revenues and expand the markets where we operate."
He projected savings of $50 million a year in operating costs, a figure that two analysts said was probably low given Mr. Goldberg's record of cutting costs in previous deals by as much as twice what he promised.
The deal to sell the casinos comes less than four months after Park Place was created by spinning off Hilton's gambling properties, including the Flamingo in Las Vegas and the Bally casinos in Atlantic City and Las Vegas.
The deal is a triumph for two casino executives known for making smart deals who joined forces in December 1996 when Hilton Hotels acquired Bally Entertainment. One is Mr. Goldberg, who was a New Jersey trucking company owner before 1990, when he took over Bally Manufacturing, a debt-ridden maker of slot machines and operator of casinos and health clubs. The other is Stephen F. Bollenbach, the chief executive of Hilton and chairman of Park Place Entertainment.
For nearly all of 1997, the two men tried to buy the casino and hotel properties of ITT Inc., the conglomerate run by Rand Araskog. But Hilton dropped out of the bidding in December 1997, with Mr. Bollenbach saying that the price ITT wanted was too high. Starwood was the winning bidder.
"This deal leaves them gratified that they were right and that this was the wrong acquisition for Starwood at the wrong price," said William H. Moore, an analyst at Forum Capital Markets in Old Greenwich, Conn. "This deal shows that they were justified that they walked away when the price was too high, especially since the guy who paid too much has to come back and sell to them. And Steve Bollenbach likes to be right."
Another analyst, Jason Ader of Bear, Stearns, said the deal "could be a positive for the Atlantic City market because you will have a greater concentration within fewer companies and are less likely to have foolish marketing decisions" such as big coin giveaways to lure slot machine gamblers.
"That's bad for the value gambler, good for the investors," Mr. Ader said of the expected tight rein on giveaways to customers.
It cost 11 percent more to gamble at Bally Park Place than next door at Caesars, which was more willing to extend complimentaries like free drinks for blackjack players and private jets to fly in high rollers, a 1990 study by National Economic Research Associates showed.
Now Bally's Park Place, an extension of its gambling area called Wild West and Caesars will be owned by the same company. Park Place Entertainment also owns the Atlantic City Hilton, formerly the Golden Nugget, at the south end of the Boardwalk.
These three Atlantic City properties will have 27.6 percent of the gambling floor space, compared with 29.9 percent for the three Trump casinos, records of the New Jersey Casino Control Commission show.
On the Las Vegas Strip, Park Place will acquire Caesars Palace. It already owns three casinos across the Strip from Caesars: the Flamingo Hilton, Bally's Las Vegas and Paris, which is scheduled to open in the fall.
After the deal closes, the company will have 15 percent of the casino space on the Strip and 18 percent of the hotel rooms, said Adam Brecht, a spokesman for PricewaterhouseCoopers.
The deal was a loss for Mirage Resorts Inc., which owns casinos on each side of Caesars Palace in Las Vegas that its chief executive, Steve Wynn, wants to connect with a monorail.
Park Place is also acquiring Caesars casinos in Lake Tahoe, Nev., riverboat casinos in Mississippi and Indiana, slot machines at Dover Downs Race Track in Delaware and interests in various gambling operations from Canada to South Africa.
Park Place did not buy Starwood's Desert Inn, which, with its own golf course on about 35 acres, is one of the large tracts of developable land left along the Strip in Las Vegas.

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE EUROPEANS;
In Uniting Over Kosovo, A New Sense of Identity

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN 

DATELINE: BERLIN, April 27

 BODY:
A month of war in Europe has already brought deep changes to the continent, awakening a new sense of European patriotism, rekindling tensions over "American imperialism" and reviving the sagging fortunes of some leaders.
Raised after World War II to reject military violence but also to insure that the barbarism of Hitler and Stalin never be repeated, a generation of Europeans has been abruptly forced by events in Kosovo to a painful choice between these principles. 
 In general, they have come down in favor of using military power to try to stop the "ethnic cleansing" of Kosovo and have increasingly portrayed the fate of the province as a decisive test of Europe's commitment to a continent freed in the 21st century of the mass deportations and killings that marked this one.
Ludger Volmer, Germany's Deputy Foreign Minister and a member of the once pacifist Green Party, said: "We have to fight for our Europe where boundaries no longer matter, against old notions of a Europe of ethnic identity and race-based violence. An integrated Europe with an open wound in the Balkans is unimaginable."
Despite the unconvincing results of more than four weeks of NATO bombing and the failure of past appeals to rally to a European rather than a national ideal, such ideas have had a surprising resonance as image after image of desperate refugees hounded from Kosovo have filled the news media.
A majority of people in Britain, France, Germany and Italy support the military campaign. A poll last week in France showed 70 percent in favor of the NATO intervention. Support for Gerhard Schroder, the German Chancellor, has surged as his chief preoccupation has become war rather than unemployment.
Indeed, such is the popular support that the British and French Governments have gone further than President Clinton in suggesting the use of ground forces, although the allies' summit meeting in Washington over the weekend suggested that support for a land war is limited.
"We have to be ready to die for Pristina," Kosovo's capital, "because to die for Pristina is to die for the future of Europe," said Andre Glucksmann, a leading French philosopher. "Americans have died for Europe twice. Now we have to make sacrifices to show this continent will no longer tolerate policies that recall Stalin."
Further, Europeans have been looking to their own future. Many see the conflict as a decisive moment for the politics of the continent -- one that could give Europe the political and military identity it has lacked.
Images of stunned deportees with unseeing eyes have stirred terrible memories. In a Europe no longer divided by the cold war, the images have prompted a determination to act, and to establish a continent-wide respect for human rights that will serve as the basis for the Europe of the next century.
But this bold new determination to salvage Europe's pride after a wounding century is by no means shared by everyone.
With an American general directing the NATO military campaign, deep concern or outright anger over European dependency on Washington and over what some see as America's control over the post-cold-war world has surfaced.
Deploring Europe's premature decision to cash in the "peace dividend" and cut military spending, Lamberto Dini, the Italian Foreign Minister, said, "The United States cannot be the gendarmes of the world, nor the mercenary soldiers for countries that do not intend to assume military responsibility."
In a distinctly anti-American tone, some French intellectuals have portrayed the war in Kosovo as the latest attempt by Washington to satisfy the appetites of its military-industrial complex, undermine Europe and bolster the unilateral power that the United States has enjoyed since the Soviet Union collapsed.
"The undeclared war in the Balkans is not a war against the Serbs," Guy Declos, a French sociologist, wrote in the French daily Le Monde. "It is a war against Europe."
Of course such anti-Americanism is not new in France, and up to now, it appears that a crisis of European-American relations has been averted.
A rupture in relations with Russia has also been avoided, and Western governments now seem determined to back a Russian-led peace bid because President Boris N. Yeltsin's influence in Belgrade is considerable. Whatever the outcome of this diplomacy, concern is strong, particularly in Germany, that Moscow not be alienated.
After Europe's profound failure in Bosnia, a war the European Union initially volunteered to stop and much later saw halted by a "Pax Americana," the desire to assert a political and military identity with a success in Kosovo is strong.
And with the arrival this year of the common European currency, the euro, the continent has been casting around for some new catalyst for the integration that has been missing.
"Kosovo could be our military euro, creating a political and defense identity for the European Union in the same way as the euro is the expression of economic and financial integration," said Ulrich Beck, a professor of politics at Munich University.
But the attainment of a "military euro" is evidently a long way off. A month of bombing has failed to bring any of the results NATO had sought, and the longer the war continues, the sharper the tensions in Europe are likely to become.
Mr. Volmer, the German Deputy Foreign Minister, said he thought his Green Party, the junior partner in the governing coalition, would be unable to accept the dispatch of ground troops if NATO moved in that direction to break the impasse. "This could bring down the German Government," he said.
The Greens are to hold a congress on May 13 at which they are likely to vote on this issue. The tensions in the party reflect the way the conversion of Mr. Volmer and of the Foreign Minister, Joschka Fischer, to supporting military force against Yugoslavia has by no means been followed by all members of the party.
Similar tensions may emerge in Italy's coalition Government, where the former Communists are very uneasy about ground forces, even as the British and French Governments push the view that a long war fought only with air power may risk losing public support.
Certainly a war in which the only victims are civilians does not appear likely to command widespread support indefinitely in a continent where the idea of "body bags" is more acceptable as a price of war than it is in the United States.

HEADLINE: A Supermarket as a Spur for Change;
New Harlem Pathmark Promises Competition and Convenience

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN 

 BODY:
Like many Manhattan residents, Olga Hayes is used to doing her major grocery shopping outside the borough -- in her case, at the Pathmark on Bruckner Boulevard in the Bronx -- and picking up a few things between trips at her local food stores.
Lately, however, Ms. Hayes, who lives on East 114th Street in East Harlem, has noticed that the Associated market two blocks from her home is looking nicer. The items she wants are placed at eye level instead of out of reach, and she no longer spots canned goods on display after the expiration date has passed. 
 "The quality of the fruits and vegetables is not great," Ms. Hayes said as she left the market one night last week with enough soda and juice to get her family through the weekend. "But they've changed a lot of things in there."
It is probably no coincidence that this transformation is occurring just as something else has happened to Ms. Hayes's neighborhood: A $15 million Pathmark supermarket, about eight times the size of the independently owned Associated that Ms. Hayes patronizes, opened April 15 on 125th Street and Lexington Avenue.
Built with private and public money by two black community groups, it is the first chain supermarket to open in Harlem in three decades and the first major commercial development completed in the neighborhood since the riots of the late 1960's. On Friday, Gov. George E. Pataki and other dignitaries will gather on the rooftop of the Pathmark to welcome the store.
In most communities, the opening of a grocery store would not be something to make a fuss over, much less invite the Governor to. But this Pathmark in this community is a very big deal -- because Harlem has been without a chain supermarket for so long, and because this one was delayed so long, in part by a bitter fight put up by owners of independent markets.
For many people in the neighborhood, who have often had to pay more for inferior quality or travel long distances to have the kind of choices that residents of other neighborhoods take for granted, the Pathmark became more than just a grocery store.
"We've been waiting a long time for a nice supermarket in Harlem," said Dorothy Reece, a retired clerical worker who lives at 135th Street and Park Avenue. "In Harlem, we always have to wait for everything, so we're used to that."
One of thousands of Harlem residents who have already been sizing up the new 24-hour Pathmark, Ms. Reece said she was so dissatisfied with the meat available at her local markets that she often used to ask her daughter to shop for her in the Bronx.
Ms. Reece predicted that the Pathmark would force the independent markets to work hard to compete. Even though the broccoli at her local Associated and CTown was cheaper -- $1.19 a bunch compared with $1.50 -- Pathmark's was more appealing, "so I don't mind spending the extra change," Ms. Reece said.
But several shoppers said they were disappointed by Pathmark's prices. "For me, it's a beautiful place just to look," said Josefa Perez, who lives on 115th Street but does not plan to return to the new store until the prices have dropped.
Harvey Gutman, a senior vice president of Pathmark, said he was unaware of these complaints. But he added: "To the extent that we're not properly priced on a particular item, that's something we'll be correcting."
The new Pathmark, one of three in Manhattan, occupies a section of East 125th Street that is disfigured by tattoo parlors, boarded-up windows, and roll-down gates covered with graffiti. Near the store's entrance, men with shopping carts full of bottles and cans recently lined up in front of the recycling machines. Not far away, a small crowd of people with shopping carts crammed with bags of groceries waited for gypsy cabs.
For people accustomed to the cramped aisles that are typical of Manhattan markets, the new Pathmark (which is about the same size as its sister store on the Lower East Side) is almost breathtaking in its spaciousness. It has 25 aisles that are more than wide enough for two-way shopping-cart traffic and it offers a large array of products for Hispanic shoppers: an aisle full of packaged or canned food, a special section of personal-care products, and a sizeable selection of root vegetables like yuca.
There is a deli counter, a separate seafood section and enough varieties of cheese to satisfy Charleen Jones, a nurse at North General Hospital. Ms. Jones said she used to order cheese through the mail and go downtown to find Stouffer's frozen products for her son or hot ketchup to spice up her meals.
Carolyn Boyd, a secretary from Central Harlem, said she was used to dragging her two young sons from store to store to get her shopping done. "I'm just going crazy in here," Ms. Boyd said. "Everything I need in a store is right here." The market also contains a Chase Manhattan Bank branch, a pharmacy and rooftop parking for 125 cars.
For decades, most big supermarket chains had stayed away from the inner city. The buying power of residents was assumed to be minimal, and the likelihood of shoplifting and other types of crime seemed all too real. But Pathmark has operated in tough New York neighborhoods since 1965, when it opened the Bruckner Boulevard market. Then Pathmark started opening stores in partnership with community groups -- first in the Bedford-Stuyvesant section of Brooklyn in 1979 and in Newark's Central Ward in 1990. The success of both markets convinced the company that there was money to be made in underserved communities.
Today, about two dozen of Pathmark's 133 supermarkets are in urban locations, Mr. Gutman said. But none has taken as long to open as the one in East Harlem.
It was to keep local families from spending their food dollars in other neighborhoods in New York -- or even in Yonkers or Paramus -- that Alice Kornegay, a community advocate in East Harlem who died in 1996, dreamed of converting the city-owned parking lot on 125th Street into a supermarket.
In 1991, the city requested proposals for building a market. When it became clear that the project could not be developed solely with private dollars, the city asked Local Initiatives Support Corporation, a national nonprofit organization known as LISC that assists local development corporations, to help find a community group with the wherewithal to get the market built and lease it to Pathmark. Profits would be plowed back into the community.
LISC, an investor in the project, identified the development arm of the Abyssinian Baptist Church in Central Harlem, a group that had created hundreds of housing units, as the best choice to team up with Ms. Kornegay's organization, the Community Association of East Harlem Triangle. The partnership was blessed by Community Board 11, which represents East Harlem, after it was agreed that most of the 200 jobs generated by the market would go to local residents.
Proponents of the market had anticipated opposition from the independent markets in East Harlem, many of which are owned by immigrants from the Dominican Republic, but they were caught off guard by the vehemence of the campaign that was mounted against them.
As the market owners saw it, they had been willing to fill a need after the large chains left the inner city. Now they were being accused of running shoddy, overpriced stores, and they felt that the city was treating them unfairly by helping a mammoth competitor come in and drive them out of business. (The Fairway market that opened in a former meat-packing plant on the West Side Highway and 133d Street in 1995 did not inspire a similar protest because it is a privately developed store that caters as much to Upper West Siders as to Harlem residents, and it is not in an area saturated with medium-sized markets.)
As supporters of the Pathmark saw it, the owners of the independent markets were seeking to protect their own economic interests by raising unjustified fears that the supermarket would put 200 neighborhood bodegas out of business and by falsely claiming that the chain itself was being subsidized. In fact, Pathmark, which was acquired in March by Royal Ahold, a Dutch food retailer, is paying an annual rent of $1.5 million -- above the market rate -- because it expects the store to be profitable, Mr. Gutman said.
Because it involved a transfer of city land, the project required the approval of the Manhattan Borough Board, which is made up of City Council members, the heads of the community boards and the Borough President. In April 1995, the Pathmark squeaked through by a single vote of the board only after Guillermo Linares -- a City Council member from from a heavily Dominican district in Washington Heights and a close friend of Alfredo Rodriguez, the leader of the independent market owners -- took a political risk and decided at the last minute to support a project that even opponents conceded was supported by most members of the community.
But the project still needed the endorsement of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani. The Mayor, who had campaigned against ethnic politics, now found himself under pressure from the markets and Hispanic politicians. Mr. Giuliani insisted that the city be given a 49 percent ownership in the market until it could be transferred to a Hispanic group. No Hispanic partner has yet been selected, Deputy Mayor Rudy Washington said recently.
Had the Pathmark deal collapsed, said Charles A. Gargano, the state Commissioner of Economic Development, "it would have been a major setback for economic development plans in Harlem." Instead, the store may attract future projects, including a 10-screen United Artists multiplex that has been proposed for a site across the street.
But the independent markets did not come away empty-handed.
Chase Manhattan, a major investor in the Pathmark, set up a program that has given out $12.1 million in loans to mid-sized markets, including six in East Harlem, bank officials said. One of the beneficiaries was Mr. Rodriguez, who said he got a $700,000 loan for a store he owns in Westbury, L.I.
After the Pathmark was approved, Mr. Rodriguez sold one market on 120th Street and Third Avenue, but he continues to operate a second store on 116th Street, near Lexington Avenue, where one of his customers, Ms. Hayes, has noticed some improvement. "We lost," he said, "but we gained respect."
Mr. Rodriguez's store on 120th Street was purchased by Efrain Martinez, who said he had spent $150,000 on improvements, including new shelves and flooring. "I'm going to try not to go out of business," Mr. Martinez said.

HEADLINE: DuPont's Profit Down 4.2% in Quarter

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WILMINGTON, Del., April 27

 BODY:
The DuPont Company, the nation's biggest chemicals company, said today that its first-quarter profit fell 4.2 percent, less than expected, helped by a rebound in demand from Asia and lower costs of raw materials.
DuPont said first-quarter operating profit fell to $749 million, or 66 cents a diluted share, compared with $782 million, or 68 cents a share, in the quarter a year earlier. Analysts expected the company to earn 60 cents a share, according to the First Call Corporation. Sales rose 1.6 percent, to $6.3 billion from $6.2 billion. 
 DuPont had a charge in the latest quarter of $121 million from its acquisition of Hoechst A.G.'s paints unit and $35 million in income from its interest in Conoco Inc., which DuPont plans to sell later this year, making net income $663 million, or 58 cents a share.
The company had a $145 million charge in the period a year earlier related to a revision in the purchase price for Protein Technologies International and $269 million in income from discontinued operations, making net income $906 million, or 79 cents a share.
In the most recent quarter, shipments to Asia, which contributes about 11 percent to DuPont's annual sales, increased 6 percent. Struggling Asian economies had dragged down profit the last few quarters, and DuPont shares rose after the company said 1999 earnings could be slightly stronger than previously expected.
Shares of DuPont, based in Wilmington, rose $2.375, to $69.125.

HEADLINE: Too Scared to Save Social Security

 BODY:

To the Editor:
An April 23 front-page article asserts that a plan developed by Representatives E. Clay Shaw Jr. and Bill Archer has been "certified by Social Security actuaries as sufficient to keep the system solvent for at least 75 years." This statement is misleading. Social Security's actuaries did calculate that the plan would keep the system solvent, if the stock market produces 7 percent real returns on average over the next 75 years. 
 The actuaries did not project that this would be the rate of return in the market over this period; they just applied the historic rate of return in their calculations. The actuaries project profit growth to be less than half as rapid in the next 75 years as in the last 75 years. Current stock prices are at record highs in relation to earnings. No economist has yet shown how the stock market can match its historic rate of return under these circumstances.
 
DEAN BAKER
Washington, April 24, 1999
 
The writer is a senior research fellow, Century Foundation.

HEADLINE: City Gardens Endure

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Re "City Rejects $2 Million Offer for Gardens" (news article, April 23): As a professor of environmental psychology at the CUNY Graduate Center in the 1970's, I conducted the first extensive study of community gardens in New York City. Of the 10 community garden sites we studied, nine are still operating today, more than 20 years later. When compared with nearby city parks and playgrounds, the gardens were consistently rated by residents as safer, more heavily used and more beautiful. Since then, the number of gardens throughout the city has sharply increased. 
 I would characterize community gardens today as the new American commons. To sell them off is like selling pieces of Central Park for their economic development value.
 
MARK FRANCIS
Davis, Calif., April 26, 1999
 
The writer is a professor of landscape architecture, University of California at Davis.

HEADLINE: METRO NEWS BRIEFS: NEW JERSEY;
New Jersey to Use Fund To Help Keep Port Lines

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
A New Jersey task force that holds the purse strings to a harbor-dredging fund yesterday approved spending $100 million to keep the state's two biggest shipping lines in New York Harbor.
The spending was approved by the Dredging Facilitation Task Force, a body that was created in 1996 to administer a $300 million bond fund approved by state voters to finance dredging, cleanup and economic development projects in the harbor, said Frank McDonough, executive director of the task force. 
 The expenditure was proposed by Gov. Christine Todd Whitman to allow the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey to save $100 million it would have spent on dredging and to pass the savings on to the shipping lines in the form of lower leasing fees.
Sea-Land Service and Maersk Lines have threatened to relocate from ports in Elizabeth and Newark, respectively, to Baltimore, Halifax, Nova Scotia, or Norfolk, Va., which could send ripples through the local shipping industry, which officials say accounts for 170,000 jobs and $20 billion a year in economic activity.
John Boyd, a Maersk spokesman, said the company was "still deliberating." A Sea-Land employee said there was no word from the company yesterday on the possible move.

HEADLINE: In America;
Unmasking Poverty

BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT 

 BODY:
The cities have not been in the news much lately. Crime is down, unemployment is down and the ghettos are not burning down. What's to cover?
Compared with Monica and impeachment, the war in the Balkans, high school massacres and Hillary's quest for fulfillment, the status of daily life in places like Rochester, N.Y., or Flint, Mich., is -- to most people -- not worth thinking about. 
 But now comes Andrew Cuomo to awaken us from our urban snooze.
Despite a booming national economy and the creation of millions of jobs, many American cities still face a bewildering array of very serious problems. In an important new report called "Now Is the Time: Places Left Behind in the New Economy," Mr. Cuomo, the Secretary of Housing and Urban Development, argues that the current economic environment "offers the best opportunity in a generation to effectively address these problems."
Feel-good stories about the economy are waved in our faces like pompoms, making it easy to miss the astonishing fact (to cite just one example) that the poverty rate for the city of Miami is 42.8 percent.
That should be unacceptable, and a big story. But it's not even part of the national consciousness.
Most cities are doing reasonably well. But Mr. Cuomo's report, released yesterday in Washington, says that one in three central cities are struggling with persistently high poverty rates, one in five are experiencing steady population losses and one in six are faced with chronically high unemployment rates.
The problems are pervasive. According to the report, "The comparatively high unemployment areas, most of which are small or mid-sized cities, are found throughout the nation, from farm cities to former industrial giants, from timber towns to former mining centers: Yuma, Arizona, saw its unemployment rate fall modestly, from 19 percent in 1992 (pre-recovery) to 18.8 percent in 1998; Madera, California's rate fell more than 5 percent over the last six years but remains high at 18.3 percent; North Chicago, Illinois, saw a modest drop from 10.7 percent in 1992 to 10.3 percent last year; and unemployment in Yakima, Washington, has declined from 13.7 percent to 10.2 percent, still more than twice the national average in 1998."
The jobless rate in Madera, Calif., or Yakima, Wash., is not high on the list of most Americans' concerns. They are out of sight and far out of mind. And even in big cities like New York and Chicago, the ugly pockets of poverty and joblessness lie hidden and neglected in the shadows that surround the glittering economic successes.
"This economy is in some ways deceptive," said Mr. Cuomo. "It is so strong that it has generated almost an economic euphoria. But when you look at the numbers underneath, you see a widespread group of cities and counties which are lagging behind."
Because most of the country is doing well, and because of the residential and employment patterns that have emerged in the last few decades, most Americans never see the areas of extreme economic distress, and don't encounter their fellow citizens who are struggling.
Mr. Cuomo said that as he traveled the country talking about these problems, the reaction he frequently got was surprise. "You have to expose it because people don't see it anymore," he said. "They're surprised that it still exists."
The report lists what it describes as the economic successes of the Clinton Administration, including the creation of 18 million jobs, the lowest peacetime unemployment rates in decades, decreases in poverty rates among blacks and Hispanics, and a record rate of home ownership.
But despite those successes, the report says in its conclusion, "The great project of our nation -- extending opportunity to all -- remains incomplete."
The problems of America's cities are not limited to the cities. They have bled into the suburbs and they are evident in many areas of chronic economic distress, including Appalachia, the Mississippi Delta region and many of the nation's Indian reservations -- places Mr. Cuomo refers to as "economic flatliners."
If those problems are not forcefully addressed when the national economy is strong and budget surpluses are huge, said Mr. Cuomo, then when?

HEADLINE: To U.S., With Friendship and a (Semi)Fast Ball

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: TOKYO, April 28

 BODY:
Prime Minister Keizo Obuchi prepared today for a meeting with President Clinton, working on his economic policy and on his pitching arm.
Mr. Obuchi, who is to leave on Thursday for the first official visit by a Japanese Prime Minister to the United States in 12 years, is expected to throw out the first ball at a Cubs game at Wrigley Field in Chicago on Saturday. So Mr. Obuchi, 61, who said he last threw a pitch about 50 years ago, spent part of the afternoon in his backyard with a baseball glove and a former pro pitcher. 
 "I'm really worried whether the ball will reach home plate or not," Mr. Obuchi said.
Judging from the practice session, which Mr. Obuchi good-naturedly allowed reporters to watch, he has good form but not much consistency. All in all, he seems to have a strong chance of getting the ball to home plate -- at least by the third bounce.
Other prime ministers have made working trips to Washington, but this is a more formal official visit and includes stays in Los Angeles on Thursday night and in Chicago on Friday and Saturday nights. Then Mr. Obuchi is to travel to Washington for talks on Monday with President Clinton and a White House dinner that evening.
This is a time of relative harmony in American-Japanese relations, and officials say the main purpose of the summit meeting is to reaffirm and strengthen the relationship rather than to resolve any particular disputes. But the backdrop will be Japan's faltering economy and American insistence that Japan do more to revive it.
The Group of Seven major industrial nations issued a communique on Monday calling on Japan to "implement stimulus measures until growth is restored, using all available tools." But in a meeting today with American reporters, Mr. Obuchi did not seem to take the hint; he suggested that Japan has already taken enough action for now and needs to allow time for results.
"We've adopted numerous measures," he said, pointing to various economic legislation passed in the last year. "I trust that favorable effects will emerge."
Asked specifically whether Japan would consider a supplementary budget to provide a stronger fiscal stimulus if current steps proved inadequate, Mr. Obuchi poured cold water on the idea, saying, "At this stage, we have no intention of drawing up a supplementary budget."
He emphasized that Government spending is only a modest part of the economy and suggested that there may be only so much that he or any official can do to revive the economy, which has been in an on-again-off-again slump since the early 1990's. He noted that the greatest part of the economy is private consumption, and added, "We are somewhat concerned that personal consumption so far has not yet shown a rising curve."
He said there are some areas where the Government can help, like promoting the housing industry and deregulating the economy. But he gave no indication that he was planning any dramatic program.
Mr. Obuchi and Mr. Clinton are also to discuss American military bases on Okinawa, where bases are concentrated and the opposition to them has traditionally been strongest. But the anti-bases movement in Japan has lost steam in the last few years, and public support for the bases and for the military alliance with the United States now seems stronger than ever.
"Our relations with the United States are the very foundation of Japanese foreign policy," Mr. Obuchi said today. "My own perception of Japan-U.S. relations is that they are at their best and most stable since Japan opened its doors to the outside world."
Mr. Obuchi has long been friendly to America, and today he recounted how as a college student in 1963, he visited the United States for the first time, staying in Washington at the Y.M.C.A. The son of a member of Parliament, Mr. Obuchi won a meeting with Robert F. Kennedy, who was then Attorney General, and was given a 1960 Kennedy-for-President tie clip showing a PT-109, the boat John F. Kennedy commanded when fighting Japan in World War II.
Inspired by that example, Mr. Obuchi said, he has used tie clips in his own campaigns in Japan. He said he was thinking about wearing the PT-109 clip on this trip but was afraid he might lose it.
On this trip to Washington, Mr. Obuchi will enjoy fancier accommodation, at Blair House, the official guest residence.
"Back in those days, the Y.M.C.A. was the cheapest place in Washington, $1.50 a night," Mr. Obuchi said. "But this time will be even cheaper. Blair House -- no charge!"

HEADLINE: A Police Chief In Khmer Rouge Resurfaces

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: PHNOM PENH, Cambodia, April 28

 BODY:
Long presumed dead, the chief of the secret police who directed a torture center for the Khmer Rouge has resurfaced, a convert from Communism to Christianity who has been living freely in western Cambodia, The Far Eastern Economic Review reports in an article to be published on Thursday.
The man, known as Duch, told reporters that he was deeply sorry for his actions and willing to face justice, the news magazine, based in Hong Kong, said. 
 Mr. Duch was found by chance by a freelance photographer, Nic Dunlop, who videotaped him. The magazine reported that Mr. Duch, 56, had admitted his identity and confessed to his role in the atrocities committed in the Khmer Rouge years in power, from 1975 to 1979.
Mr. Duch disappeared when the Khmer Rouge were toppled in 1979 and was long presumed dead. But, the magazina said, for at least several years he became an evangelical Christian and worked with international aid organizations that were unaware of his identity.
Mr. Duch's emergence could increase pressure on Prime Minister Hun Sen to put Khmer Rouge leaders on trial on charges of crimes against humanity. The Cambodian Genocide Program, financed by the United States, estimates that 1.7 million Cambodians from a population of 7.9 million were murdered, tortured, starved or worked to death under the Khmer Rouge.
Only one senior Khmer Rouge figure, Ta Mok, a hard-line commander, is in custody awaiting trial. Other surviving leaders have been allowed to live freely in exchange for making peace with the Government. Mr. Duch could be an important witness, able to implicate those above and below him.
Mr. Duch headed the Santebal or internal security organization, and was also responsible for Tuol Sleng, a former high school here that became a prison where at least 14,000 men, women and children were tortured before being killed.
The Khmer Rouge kept methodical records of the tortures. A document from 1978 shows that 17 children whose parents were suspected of treachery were taken to Tuol Sleng. Mr. Duch signed an order that said, "Kill them all."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
DaimlerChrysler Has 23% Rise in Profits

BYLINE: By MICHELLE KREBS 

DATELINE: DETROIT, April 28

 BODY:
Fueled by strong vehicle sales in the United States and healthy heavy-truck sales, DaimlerChrysler A.G. reported today that first-quarter profits increased by 23 percent, exceeding some analysts' forecasts.
Net profits totaled 1.6 billion euros, or $1.75 billion; the equivalent of $1.64 euros, or about $1.79 a share, versus 1.43 euros, or about $1.56 a share, for the corresponding period in 1998. Revenues rose 10 percent, to 35 billion euros, or about $38.28 billion. Profits and revenues were up in every category in which DaimlerChrysler competes. 
 These comparisons, provided by DaimlerChrysler, are based on the combined totals of Daimler-Benz A.G. and the Chrysler Corporation, which merged in November.
DaimlerChrysler is riding the wave of strong United States vehicle sales, which ran 10 percent above a year ago in the first quarter at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of nearly 17 million vehicles.
Chrysler products, such as the new Jeep Grand Cherokee and the Dodge Durango sport utility, have been well received as Chrysler has increased its market share. Profits from the sale of Chrysler vehicles rose 12 percent, to $1.5 billion, in the quarter. More than half of DaimlerChrysler's 2.8 billion euros, or $3.06 billion, in operating profits came from Chrysler, the company said.
"Chrysler is selling what people want and selling what people are willing to pay a premium for," said James Collins, an auto analyst with the London office of Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette. "Chrysler's revenues were up slightly, but its operating margins improved from 9 percent to 9.6 percent. It's clearly a matter of the mix of vehicles Chrysler is selling."
Income from the sale of Mercedes-Benz and Smart vehicles, the second- largest contributor to DaimlerChrysler's operating profits, rose 4 percent. Mercedes is being dragged down by slow sales of the Smart car, a new minicar sold in Europe. Sluggish sales have led the auto maker to cut production by a third.
"Mercedes saw a good quarter, but it was slightly hidden under the continuing problems with the Smart car," Mr. Collins said. "The recent news of Smart sales has been better, but it's difficult to see longer term if the car will be viable. In the meantime, the Smart car is hurting the margins at Mercedes."
Mr. Collins predicts the Mercedes margins will be hurt only temporarily. The expected high profit margins of the new Mercedes top-of-the-line S-Class, introduced in Europe late last year and on sale since March in the United States, have yet to have an impact on DaimlerChrysler's balance sheet.
Strong sales of Freightliner heavy trucks helped profits in the commercial-truck segment increase by 12 percent, to 1.63 billion euros, or $1.78 billion. Profits from the company's aerospace unit more than doubled, and income from financial services operations also rose.
DaimlerChrysler's strong first-quarter results suggest increased revenues for the rest of the year, which are now expected to surpass its initial revenue target of 137 billion euros, or $149.84 billion, DaimlerChrysler's chairman, Jurgen E. Schrempp, said in a statement. Strength in the United States should offset a possible slowdown in sales in Europe during the second half of the year and continued economic problems in Asia, the company said.
The company's stock closed at $99.125, down $2.1875. European auto stocks declined after Volkswagen A.G. reported lower than expected profits, raising concerns about sales on the Continent.
Created by the $36 billion takeover of Chrysler by Daimler-Benz A.G., DaimlerChrysler, now the world's fifth-largest auto maker, also continues cost-cutting that will help increase operating profits at a rate greater than revenues over the year.
In a conference call, DaimlerChrysler executives told auto analysts that the cost-cutting benefits from the merger were minimal in the first quarter but would total $1.4 billion in savings for the full year.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
China Toughens in Negotiations on Trade Group Membership

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: SHANGHAI, April 28

 BODY:
China appears to have toughened its stance in talks aimed at gaining entry to the World Trade Organization, making United States negotiators pay for President Clinton's decision earlier this month to delay a final deal.
In the latest turn in China's long and complex path toward membership in the group that makes the rules for global commerce, Chinese negotiators have accused American officials of making excessive demands. 
 "Some negotiators are reaching out for a yard after taking in an inch," Wu Yi, a State Councillor and senior trade official, said in Beijing. "They are demanding too high conditions that are impractical and unacceptable for China."
China also seems to be trying to use international leverage with European trade officials to put pressure on the United States to agree to a World Trade Organization deal more quickly.
Today, Chinese negotiators met for a third consecutive day in Beijing with negotiators from the European Union, in preparation for a visit by Sir Leon Brittan, the European trade commissioner, who is scheduled to arrive in Beijing on May 5 and is expected to announce an agreement that China should finally be allowed into the world trade body.
Two separate groups of negotiators met about tariffs and on trading services, the New China News Agency reported.
Long Yongtu, China's chief negotiator, was quoted by the agency as saying that China was prepared to make extensive efforts to push forward the negotiations, apparently appealing for international support to counter American demands in its trade talks with China.
Just before Prime Minister Zhu Rongji's trip to the United States early this month, China made a broad array of concessions aimed at overcoming United States opposition for Chinese entry to the trade body, which China has sought for 13 years.
China agreed to import more American beef, citrus products and wheat, and to allow more openness in the telecommunications, financial services and entertainment fields. President Clinton rejected a proposed agreement, though, apparently judging the anti-China mood in Washington to be too strong to accept an accord.
After vigorous protests from United States business leaders that a good deal had been within reach, the Clinton Administration authorized a fresh round of negotiations aimed at preserving the momentum that had been achieved.
With the Americans on the defensive for what is widely seen to have been a lost opportunity during Mr. Zhu's visit, Chinese negotiators seem to want to take advantage of a suddenly strengthened negotiating position.
American officials now say that their latest round of talks with China, held last weekend, focused on financial services, an area where they say the most obstacles remain. They have demanded full rights for foreign banks operating in China to conduct business in local and foreign currencies and to serve both local and foreign customers.
The American negotiators say that China is insisting on too many restrictions on United States banks, insurers and other financial companies that want to enter the country. In response, Chinese officials argue that equalizing market access for Chinese and foreign banks could be disastrous for domestic banks.
"Excessive growth of nonbank financial institutions could lead to a financial crisis," The China Economic Times reported, quoting a position paper from the State Development and Planning Commission. "Foreign-invested firms, supported by their banks, could easily take over sectors of the market and monopolize industries."

HEADLINE: Corporate Conduct Code Is Proposed for Third World Nations

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Corporations around the world will be encouraged to offer employees stock-ownership plans and other profit-sharing incentives under a new code of good corporate conduct that finance ministers from 29 industrial countries are expected to endorse next month.
The code was drawn up by the Paris-based Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development at the request of member governments last year after the financial meltdown in Asia. The organization said the crisis highlighted what it called the need for better standards of business behavior in that region of the world. 
 Introducing the code today, the organization's secretary general, Donald J. Johnston, called it a "cornerstone for attracting foreign investment into the developing world."
He described the code as complementary to the efforts of the International Monetary Fund and World Bank, which are holding their annual spring meetings here, to strengthen the international financial system after the crises in Asia and Latin America.
"The Asian crisis highlighted the weakness in transparency, in regulation and in overall good governance in the corporate sector in a number of countries," Mr. Johnston said.
The code, which will be approved at a meeting in Paris on May 26, comes a few months after the organization banned corporate bribery and after ratification by 12 governments including the United States, Germany, Japan and Britain. Mr. Johnston said France was expected to ratify the code this summer.
This prohibition on bribery, which closely follows existing American law, requires ratifying countries to introduce domestic legislation making it a criminal offense for their corporations to bribe foreign officials to win business contracts.
Although the code will be voluntary, Mr. Johnson said that the World Bank and the monetary fund could insist that governments encourage companies to follow its prescriptions as a condition for loans they offer.
Most of the code's provisions are already accepted practice in the Western industrialized world. The recommendation that corporations offer profit-sharing plans to employees comes in the section dealing with "the role of stakeholders in corporate governance," which calls for "active cooperation" between businesses and all those with a stake in their success, such as workers and suppliers.
Other sections of the code define the rights of shareholders and provide for their equitable treatment. The code also lays down general principles for corporate disclosure of finances and management and defines the responsibilities of a board of directors.
After a meeting of the joint Development Committee of the World Bank and the monetary fund, which deals with poorer nations and those recovering from conflicts, the World Bank president, James D. Wolfensohn, said today that he expected the number of Kosovar refugees to rise from 600,000 at present to 950,000.
Within 30 days, he said, the World Bank and the European Union plan to establish a jointly financed relief operation and to start planning for the refugees' eventual return as well as for the reconstruction of Kosovo.
He also said that the longer the bombing lasts in Yugoslavia, the greater the economic damage to surrounding countries like Macedonia, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Albania and Romania, which are suffering trade and investment losses.

HEADLINE: Orders Up in March For Durable Goods

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Orders for big-ticket goods made in the United States rose in March for the fourth time in five months, a sign that the rebound in manufacturing is real, Government figures showed today.
Orders for products like cars, washing machines and computers rose a stronger-than-expected 2 percent last month, Commerce Department figures showed. Even counting a 3.9 percent decline in February, first-quarter orders rose at the strongest quarterly pace in four years. 
 The gain in orders for durable goods last month suggests the drag on manufacturing from last year's recessions in Asia, Russia and Latin America may be waning.
In March, durable goods orders rose in almost every category surveyed by the Government. Orders for planes and cars rose 3.6 percent, for example, and electrical equipment orders -- including household appliances -- rose 3.1 percent. Industrial machinery orders, which includes personal computers, increased 2.9 percent.
Excluding airplanes, autos and other transportation equipment rose 1.5 percent after falling eight-tenths of a percent in February.
In addition, orders for nonmilitary capital goods, excluding aircraft, rose 4.5 percent last month, the fourth increase in the last five months, suggesting businesses are optimistic enough about the future to invest in new equipment.

HEADLINE: Seoul Journal;
Out at Last, Prisoner 3514 Catches Up on 40 Years

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: SEOUL, South Korea, April 23

 BODY:
For four decades in prison, Woo Yong Gak coolly handled just about every challenge life could throw at him, from the most brutal tortures to endless spells of solitary confinement.
Yet a few things do intimidate Mr. Woo these days in a way that torturers never could. Vending machines, for example. 
 "How do you do this, anyway?" Mr. Woo asked, staring in puzzlement at a hot-drink machine on a Seoul street. "I've never used one of these contraptions."
Mr. Woo, 69, emerged in February after 40 years, 7 months and 13 days in prison, after becoming known in human rights circles as the world's longest-serving political prisoner. After decades simply as Prisoner 3514, he has recovered his name and he has a nice new watch on his wrist and he is even taking lessons in using computers.
"But," he said quickly, "I haven't gotten as far as getting an E-mail address."
Nowhere could a Rip Van Winkle encounter greater changes in four decades than in South Korea, the world's fastest-growing economy in that period. These changes are particularly poignant because they seem to challenge everything that Mr. Woo believed in, everything that carried him through beatings and sickness and the constant threat of anonymous death in the darkness of his prison cell.
Mr. Woo endured because of his faith in the Communist Government of North Korea, and in 1958, when he was arrested, that might have seemed a good bet. North Korea then was booming economically and was regarded by many Koreans as a more legitimate government than the American-backed dictatorship in the South.
Yet now Mr. Woo has been released into a different world, one where South Korea is a rich, industrialized country and a vigorous democracy. North Korea, on the other hand, is so poor that it is suffering a famine, and it remains a tightly run authoritarian nation halfway between a Communist country and an ancient Korean kingdom.
None of this persuades Mr. Woo to change his loyalties. In a long conversation in the offices of a human rights group that battled on his behalf, he comes across as friendly, humorous and polite -- and a dedicated North Korean Communist.
"The real issue isn't economic development," he said stoutly, dismissing South Korea's economic gains. "G.N.P. -- that's just numbers. The fact that the living standard has increased might be an interesting research topic, but it's not everything."
As for politics, Mr. Woo is not particularly impressed by the degree of freedom in the society that imprisoned him for 40 years. For a determined Korean nationalist like Mr. Woo, the crucial issue is whether a country is self-sufficient and can stand on its own. And there he finds North Korea does better.
"We have lost our self-reliance here," he added, referring to South Korea. "Economically, politically and militarily, the level of dependence on the outside has increased."
South Korea, as a vibrant democracy whose President, Kim Dae Jung, is himself a longtime dissident, may seem like an odd place to have held the world's longest-serving political prisoner. But South Korea somehow manages to be deeply authoritarian as well as vigorously democratic, and there is strong public support for repressive laws that punish anyone who praises the enemy in the North.
Minkahyup, a leading human rights group, says South Korea still has more than 200 political prisoners. The longest-serving political prisoner now that Mr. Woo has been released is Sohn Song Mo, 70, who has been imprisoned for 18 years.
"President Kim Dae Jung has been honored with human rights awards and touted as a 'human rights President,' " said Nam Kyu Sun, the secretary general of Minkahyup. "But such things don't make a 'human rights President.' "
The way for Mr. Kim to really earn the title, Mrs. Nam said, would be to ease the National Security Law that punishes Communist sympathizers and to curtail the security organizations that now run around seizing people who praise North Korea.
Although Mr. Woo came to be renowned as a political prisoner, he started his prison career as an ordinary spy. He was commander of a boat that was gathering intelligence on South Korea's coast when he and his comrades were captured in July 1958.
But such intruders were quickly released if they agreed to denounce North Korea and cooperate with the South, and indeed some of Mr. Woo's companions were freed in this way. Even terrorists who blew up a South Korean airliner and attacked the presidential mansion were pardoned after switching sides, and no one doubts that Mr. Woo would have been freed as well if he had not stubbornly clung to his faith in the North.
Day after day, the torturers battered Mr. Woo, trying to force him to sign a statement denouncing North Korea. He lost his teeth to the torture -- now he is outfitted with dentures -- and he says his best times in those decades were the moments when he felt himself fainting from the pain.
"It was such a peaceful feeling," he recalled. "I was so comfortable."
"The most unbearable thing was watching my friends dying of the torture, one by one," he said, adding that 12 of his companions died that way. The worst heartache, he said, was when a childhood friend, Choi Seok Ki, was stomped to death in his prison cell for refusing to renounce North Korea.
When Mr. Woo left on his mission, he had a wife and a 4-year-old son in North Korea, and he says the knowledge that they were safe was an enormous comfort to him.
"The thought that my child would remember me as a father who went missing while fighting for reunification was a source of great strength to me," he recalled.
North Korea and South Korea have talked publicly about exchanging prisoners, so that Mr. Woo could go home and see his family again -- although he has no word on whether they are still alive. But those talks quickly broke down, and so Mr. Woo remains stuck in South Korea.
He is trying to make the most of it by taking his computer classes and trying to acquire skills that might help him get a job in some charitable organization. But he has a lot to catch up on, for until his release he had never done something so simple as ride a subway or make a telephone call.
"Things that are common knowledge to other people are all new to me," he said with a laugh. "I've made some phone calls, now, but I'm still really bad at speaking on the telephone."

LENGTH: 281 words

HEADLINE: A German-Italian Merger

 BODY:
The economic integration of Western Europe, as signified by the common euro currency, was designed in part to help European businesses unify to become world-class competitors that could compete with giant American companies. A merger of Deutsche Telekom and Telecom Italia is unlikely to produce such a company.
The merger, agreed to last week, would create one of the largest telecommunications companies in the world, as measured by market value. But for that company to be an effective competitor it would need to pursue efficiencies under a management dedicated to making money and improving service. Nationalistic interests would need to take a back seat. 
 But those goals seem to have been overlooked in this deal. The merged company is to be managed on the basis of "parity," with neither old company dominating the merged entity and the German Government agreeing not to meddle in management although it will own 45 percent of the stock.
This is a marriage born of desperation, not of love. The Italian company wants to fend off a hostile takeover offer from Olivetti, a much smaller Italian concern. Deutsche Telekom and Telecom Italia -- once state-owned monopolies in their countries -- have been struggling with the deregulated and internationalized telecommunications market. They have entered into a variety of foreign alliances that so far have shown little in the way of results.
It will be difficult for a merged Italian-German business to overcome internecine rivalries and provide superior service at a reasonable cost. If it fails to do so, the result will be a huge company that will gradually see its markets picked off by more nimble competitors.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
A soft-spoken plan for global reform that lacks a big stick.

BYLINE: By Michael M. Weinstein 

 BODY:
TREASURY Secretary Robert E. Rubin's proposal for reforming international financial institutions, announced on the eve of this week's gathering of the world's finance officials in Washington, was greeted mostly with yawns. His ideas were described as modest or cautious -- tinkering in a financially hazardous world that cries out for bold architectural overhaul.
But the problem with Mr. Rubin's proposal, some prominent economists say, lies not with its cautious ideas. Prof. Barry Eichengreen of the University of California at Berkeley argues that modest reforms are what are needed. "The problem with Mr. Rubin's policy announcement," he said, "is too little follow-through." 
 Gary Hufbauer of the Institute for International Economics agrees. "Mr. Rubin failed to outline concrete steps that would translate pleasant words into effective action."
Financial crisis struck Asia hard in the summer of 1997, spread to Russia and Brazil last year and continues to threaten Japan and China. The International Monetary Fund intervened but could not prevent steep recessions in any of the countries. The question that lingers is how to stop currency crises before they spread.
Mr. Rubin would discourage the use of pegged exchange rates -- rates that are temporarily linked to the dollar but easily abandoned during crises. Countries inevitably run out of dollars to prop up their currency, accelerating the flight of investors and precipitating a currency crisis. "At the center of each recent crisis," Mr. Rubin argues, "has been a rigid exchange-rate regime that proved ultimately unsustainable."
To discourage the practice, Mr. Rubin would, with caveats, deny large bailouts to countries that peg their currencies. That could persuade most developing countries to adopt floating rates or currency boards, which bind the value of a currency to the dollar with no easy way to opt out.
Mr. Rubin also proposed that creditors bear more of the cost of bailing out insolvent governments. And he wants developing countries to accept fewer short-term loans, the kind that cause creditors to flee at the first whiff of financial trouble. That prescription might encourage developing countries to adopt measures that the industrialized powers have historically opposed, including taxes on short-term capital loans.
Economists point out that it takes more than hortatory language to achieve Mr. Rubin's goals.
To force creditors to bear some of the cost of bailing out insolvent governments requires rewriting contracts that govern the issuing of their bonds. Those contracts would need to allow a majority of creditors to renegotiate on behalf of all bondholders rather than require unanimous consent. The bond contracts would also need to require creditors that sue delinquent governments to share settlements with all bond holders. Only then could creditors as a group accept lower interest payments rather than driving the debtor government into default.
But, Professor Eichengreen points out, no developing country can rewrite bond contracts on its own. If Ecuador tried to write anti-creditor provisions into its contracts, creditors would take their money elsewhere. There is, however, one country that could proceed with impunity. Professor Eichengreen says that Mr. Rubin could have announced that the United States would take the lead and incorporate majority voting and settlement-sharing provisions into its bond offerings, giving cover for other countries to mimic.
Mr. Hufbauer makes a similar point about Mr. Rubin's call for steering developing countries away from short-term loans. Mr. Rubin could have made headway had he proposed, for example, that the banking authorities in the United States impose higher reserve requirements on banks that make such loans. Mr. Rubin calls instead for international guidelines.
Mr. Hufbauer and Professor Eichengreen are more supportive of Mr. Rubin's decision to avoid the many grandiose ideas that have been proposed to solve the international financial crisis. Some want to create an international "lender of last resort" -- an international central bank that would make loans to distressed governments, just as the Federal Reserve does to distressed banks in the United States. Others call for an international agency, modeled on the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation, to insure foreign loans. The idea would be to reassure skittish investors that their loans were safe so they need not bolt at the first signs of financial stress.
But Professor Eichengreen exposes the flaws of ambitious institution building in a book published earlier this year. International pledges to insolvent governments encourage investors to take unwarranted risks.
Besides, international authorities cannot provide unlimited loans or insurance to individual governments or banks without the right to halt activities that threaten solvency -- an invasion of sovereignty that no proponent of these new institutions has yet to tackle.
