HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS;
In Clinton's Words: 'Speak With a Single Voice'

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from President Clinton's statement on Kosovo and questions and answers yesterday at the White House, as recorded by The New York Times, and a letter he wrote to House Speaker J. Dennis Hastert :
 
Statement by Clinton 
 I just had a good meeting with a large bipartisan delegation from both houses of Congress on Kosovo. It was our fourth meeting since the air strikes began. We spoke about the NATO summit, its unity and determination to achieve our objectives in Kosovo, about the progress of our military campaign and the intensification of economic sanctions, about the humanitarian challenge that we face and the work that we and our allies are doing to meet it.
Just on Monday, some 3,500 Kosovar refugees in trains and buses arrived in Montenegro. Yesterday about 5,000 entered Macedonia. Almost 3,000 arrived in Albania exhausted, hungry, shaken, all by the violence and abuse they experienced on the way.
At one point, 1.8 million ethnic Albanians lived in Kosovo. Nearly one and a half million have been displaced since the start of the crisis. . . .
We are also providing more funds to the U.S. High Commissioner for Refugees and to NGO's to deliver food and supplies to the refugees. Our Defense Department has found a site for the facility it will build in Albania for up to 20,000 refugees. We hope it will begin taking in refugees in about two weeks.
I spoke to members of Congress about all these efforts today. I told them that now is the time to pass the supplemental funding for Kosovo that I requested nine days ago. We needed to maintain our military readiness. Just as important, we need to sustain humanitarian relief and support for the front-line nations that have absorbed the brunt of this emergency.
Let me stress that my request fully funds our military and humanitarian needs in Kosovo. The Congress should resist the temptation to add unrelated expenditures, even important ones, which could delay the process, because that would undermine the very goals that this funding is intended to meet. We must get a Kosovo funding measure passed and to my desk now.
We also talked about other legislative initiatives pending on Kosovo in the Congress. I stressed that the 19 NATO allies are speaking with a single voice. America must continue to speak with a single voice, as well. I told them we would welcome the support of the Congress, so that Mr. Milosevic will have no doubt that we have the determination and the patience to persevere until we prevail.
Each day our military campaign takes a toll on Serbia's machinery of repression. The Serbian leadership has failed to divide us and will not outlast us. The combined military might and moral determination of Europe and North America will endure.
We know what the final outcome will be. The Serbian forces will leave Kosovo. An international security force will deploy to protect all the people there, Serbs as well as Albanians. And the refugees will return with security and self-government.
Q -- Mr. President, do you see any signs that Milosevic is losing his grip, Sir? Any signs at all?
CLINTON -- We have some indications that there are differences of opinion, obviously, developing in Belgrade. And we saw some of it public this week. There are some things that we know that I -- I think I should not comment on.
But the thing I want to tell the American people is we know objectively what damage has been done. We know now we're going to be in a position to fly around the clock at lower altitudes from all directions in better weather. Historically, the weather is better in May than in April, better in June than in May, better in July than in June.
Letter to Hastert --
Dear Mr. Speaker:
 I appreciate the opportunity to continue to consult closely with the Congress regarding events in Kosovo.
The unprecedented unity of the NATO members is reflected in our agreement at the recent summit to continue and intensify the air campaign. Milosevic must not doubt the resolve of the NATO alliance to prevail. I am confident we will do so through use of air power.
However, were I to change my policy with regard to the introduction of ground forces, I can assure you that I would fully consult with the Congress. Indeed, without regard to our differing constitutional views on the use of force, I would ask for Congressional support before introducing U.S. ground forces into Kosovo into a nonpermissive environment. Milosevic can have no doubt about the resolve of the United States to address the security threat to the Balkans and the humanitarian crisis in Kosovo. The refugees must be allowed to go home to a safe and secure environment.
 
Sincerely,
Bill Clinton

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Paper and Chemical Issues Send Dow to One More High

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
Chemical, paper and other stocks sensitive to the business cycle turned in another strong performance yesterday while technology and other longtime market leaders sagged.
The sharply split performance, which resulted in a flat market over all, left analysts struggling to figure the significance of this month's sudden but erratic revival of cyclical stocks. 
 "The market doesn't have a well-organized, broad-based economic theme to support itself any more," said Christine A. Callies, chief investment strategist at Credit Suisse First Boston. "It's confusing. The market goes in several different directions at once."
The Dow Jones industrial average, driven by cyclical stocks, sailed above the 10,900 mark for the first time but swooned late in the session and managed to hang on to only 13.74 points of a gain that had swelled to more than 103 points. Still, the one-tenth of a percent rise, to 10,845.45, was a new high.
The Nasdaq composite index, meanwhile, lost 52.04 points, or 2 percent, to 2,550.37, as software and computer stocks slumped, led by Amazon.com, which fell 12 3/8, to 193 1/2. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 11.89 points, or nine-tenths of a percent, to 1,350.91.
All of the top-performing blue-chip stocks from yesterday stand to benefit from stepped-up global economic growth. Alcoa added 6 5/8, to 62; DuPont rose 4 3/8, to 73 1/2; Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing climbed 3 11/16, to 84 1/2;Exxon increased 3 1/4, to 81 1/2, andAllied Signal was up 3 1/16, to 59 1/16.
The biggest drags on the Dow were I.B.M., down 7, to 205, and American Express, down 5 11/16, to 133 1/16.
"We're seeing continued rotation away from consumer stocks, particularly pharmaceuticals, into industrial companies and oils," said Timothy E. Woolston, a portfolio manager at Boston Advisors. Merck fell 2 13/16, to 70 3/4, and Johnson & Johnson dropped 2 7/16, to 99 1/2.
Vernon Winters, chief investment officer at Mellon Private Asset Management, called America's large-capitalization growth stocks "perhaps the most overpriced asset" in the world. Money should flee them, he said, and "flow to the broader universe of stocks."
Dow Chemical jumped 8 1/2, to 126 1/2, to lead a strong showing by chemical companies. The paper group featured Mead, up 4 3/4, to 43 5/16, with the aid of a recommendation by Morgan Stanley Dean Witter; Bowater rose 5 7/8, to 52, after earnings fell less than expected, and Georgia-Pacific increased 4 1/16, to 94 1/8. Its gain is now above 60 percent for the year.
The most actively traded issue, McKesson HBOC, the biggest drug wholesaler, was one of the biggest casualties. It plunged 31 1/4, to 34 1/2, after restating earnings because of improperly recorded sales.
Aetna joined the long list of big companies that beat Wall Street's expectations this spring and its shares soared 10 3/8, to 92. But America Online, whose report after the close on Tuesday did little better than match forecasts, dropped 10, to 143, still more than eight times its price on Sept. 1.
Nasdaq, which fell for a second consecutive day after five advances, reflected losses in its dominant high-technology sector, with Microsoft, down 1 7/8, to 82 1/8, having the biggest effect. Next were Cisco, which was down 3 5/16, to 111 3/4; Dell, which fell 1 9/16, to 41 5/8, and Amazon.com. Ebay skidded 16 11/16, to 192 9/16, and Yahoo fell 11, to 173 1/2.
The Morgan Stanley cyclical index jumped 2.6 percent, its biggest gain since April 15, and lifted its gain for the month to more than 20 percent.
On the New York Stock Exchange, volume was a moderately heavy 951.8 million shares as 1,753 stocks rose and 1,279 fell. New 52-week highs were made by 82 issues compared with 28 new lows.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Treasury Prices Fall
By Bloomberg News
Bond prices fell yesterday as the dollar dropped against the yen, oil prices surged and investors bought $17.1 billion of new debt securities.
The Treasury sold $15 billion of two-year notes, and the Delphi Automotive Systems Corporation led yesterday's corporate sales by issuing $1.5 billion. The Sprint Corporation plans to sell $3 billion of bonds today, and investors are worried that the new sales will swamp demand.
The price of the benchmark 30-year bond fell 19/32, to 95 8/32. Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 5.58 percent from 5.54 percent. Yields on two-year notes rose to 5.01 percent from 4.99 percent.

HEADLINE: 2 Key Republicans Press Ahead on Social Security, Offering Investment-Account Plan

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Hoping to breathe life into the dying effort to shore up Social Security, two pivotal House Republicans laid out a plan today that they said would assure the retirement system's long-term health without cutting benefits or raising taxes.
The plan would plug the projected hole in Social Security's balance sheet by taking trillions of dollars in Government revenue in coming decades and giving it to workers in the form of tax rebates. The workers would then have to invest that money in the stock and bond markets through individual accounts, with the proceeds going to help finance their retirement benefits. 
 Although the proposal is similar to the approach advocated by most Republicans, it has been shunned by the party's leaders, in part to avoid what they fear would be a losing political fight with Congressional Democrats and President Clinton. As a result, the plan has virtually no chance of passage, and some members of both parties said Social Security was all but dead as a substantive issue until after the 2000 elections.
But the plan's authors, Representatives Bill Archer of Texas, the chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, and E. Clay Shaw Jr. of Florida, the chairman of the Ways and Means Subcommittee on Social Security, said they intended to push ahead anyway, in the hope that they could create momentum for bipartisan negotiations this year.
Insuring that Social Security can weather the retirement of the baby-boom generation and be there for future generations should be a matter of "principle over politics," said Mr. Archer, who, retiring from Congress at the end of this term, is freed of some of the political pressure that is a concern of the other leaders.
Mr. Clinton's aides said he believed that a deal was still possible. The President is to map his next move in a strategy session with his advisers on Thursday, and, while Administration officials said they were not considering any dramatic new proposals, they also said they would look for ways to make a gesture to Mr. Archer and other Republicans. Among the options on the table are technical changes in the way Mr. Clinton's current plan would use the surplus to help Social Security. Also being considered is an effort to convince Republicans that Mr. Clinton's proposal for a new type of individual retirement account outside of Social Security could be part of a package to strengthen the system.
Seeking to nurture the remnants of bipartisan good will on the issue, White House officials gave the Archer-Shaw plan a gentle reception today, saying they objected to some of its components but appreciated Mr. Archer's willingness to tackle a subject that many Republicans have been loath to touch.
"This looks like a serious attempt to engage in a very important debate," said Joe Lockhart, the White House press secretary.
Although many House Democrats attacked the plan, others, including Representative Charles B. Rangel of New York, the senior Democrat on the Ways and Means Committee, muted their criticism. After being briefed on the plan by Mr. Archer and Mr. Shaw this afternoon, some committee Democrats said that although they found it deeply flawed, it could be the basis for negotiation if the Republican leadership was willing to engage on the issue.
Without any changes, Social Security will run short of money needed to pay the full level of promised benefits starting in 2034, after the retirement of the baby-boom generation. Mr. Clinton's approach relies largely on using the projected budget surplus to bolster Social Security's finances and allowing the Government to invest a part of the retirement system's reserves in stocks.
The Archer-Shaw plan uses the same basic tools but in a very different way. Each year all workers would receive from the Federal Government a tax credit equal to two percentage points of the 12.4 percent payroll tax that finances Social Security benefits. Because the payroll tax is currently levied only on the first $72,600 of wages (the amount is adjusted each year for inflation), the maximum annual credit if the plan were now law would be $1,452.
The money would be automatically deposited in a Social Security account in the worker's name. The worker would then choose from among a variety of mutual funds, all of which would be required to hold 60 percent of their assets in stock indexes and 40 percent in high-grade corporate bonds. Any gains in the account would accumulate tax-free.
At retirement, the money in the account would be used to help finance the worker's Social Security benefit. All workers would receive at least as much as promised under the current system, regardless of how well their investments fared. Those workers whose accounts did particularly well would come out ahead.
Democrats said the plan had two major flaws. It relies on budget surpluses to finance the tax credit, and the surplus, they said, is likely to be exhausted sometime in the next several decades. And it creates what Democrats said would be an unwieldy system of more than 100 million individual accounts that would be very expensive to administer.
While the chances of serious bipartisan negotiations this year remain dim, some in both parties said there might be an opportunity now to look at other compromise proposals, including one being developed by Representative Benjamin L. Cardin, Democrat of Maryland.
In a nod to the Republican desire to shift more control to individuals, Mr. Cardin's plan would establish savings accounts within Social Security but would have them supplement the current system rather than replace part of it. And in an effort to appeal to Democrats, his plan would also allow the Government to invest a part of the system's money on Wall Street, but only under restrictions that would limit political meddling in the investment decisions.

HEADLINE: De Niro and Miramax Plan a Film Studio at the Brooklyn Navy Yard

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
The actor Robert De Niro and Miramax Films plan to announce a tentative deal with city officials to build a $150 million Hollywood-style film studio at the Brooklyn Navy Yard, the sprawling complex where tens of thousands of workers once built and launched warships and tankers.
According to executives on both sides of the negotiations, the proposed movie studio would include 12 sound stages in three buildings on a 15-acre site within the Navy Yard and would employ thousands of people. It would be used by Mr. De Niro's TriBeCa Productions, Miramax Films and other companies in the industry, which has overrun New York in recent years, spending $2.6 billion last year alone on crews, equipment, supplies and services. 
 Still, the $150 million plan faces major obstacles, particularly regarding its financing and questions about the long-term viability of such a venture. The project's backers are investing $35 million; they have already raised an additional $35 million from city government sources, and will need another $80 million in financing, people involved in the talks said.
Despite the growth of film and television production in New York, the plan to develop the Navy Yard into film studios has had a tortured history ever since it was first suggested last June. But Mr. De Niro, Harvey Weinstein, co-chairman of Miramax, and another partner in the venture, Steven Roth, chairman of Vornado Realty Trust, are expected to take an important step at the Navy Yard next Monday by signing a nonbinding letter of intent to go ahead with the project.
Even as recently as last week, the negotiations nearly unraveled over a rent dispute between officials at the Navy Yard and the studio group. After frantic meetings over the weekend, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani met with Mr. De Niro and Mr. Weinstein on Monday and put the deal back on track. The meetings were first reported yesterday in The New York Observer.
The studio group, which is led by Mr. De Niro, has been hampered by insufficient financing. A week ago, however, the Union Labor Life Insurance Company, a union-supported pension fund that has invested in New York real estate projects, notified the group and the city that it is considering whether to finance or invest in the project.
"We're taking a hard look at it," said Michael Steed, senior vice president for investments at Union Life in Washington. "It's clear that New York doesn't have enough studios to handle both the television and film production that could take place there. What's unclear to us is exactly what types of studios should be there -- film or television -- and where they should be."
Under the terms of the tentative deal, the city would provide a $25 million, low-interest loan and the city's Brooklyn Navy Yard Development Corporation, which oversees the industrial park there, would contribute up to $10 million in cash.
The rent would rise from almost nothing to market rates over the term of a 50-year lease.
"This is the equivalent of a Universal Studio, where they could shoot a whole movie from start to finish," said one person involved in the negotiations. "If this is a success it could revitalize the entire area."
Another recent participant in the talks has been the Fisher real estate family, a major Manhattan landlord. The Fishers would oversee construction and, possibly, become a partner in the project.
A deal would not only rejuvenate an area that was long a main source of the city's economic muscle, but would also provide growth space for the city's booming film industry. The number of feature films shot in New York City soared to 221 last year, up from 69 in 1993. During the same period, the number of television production shooting days jumped to 7,680, from 3,598. But most of the post-production work is done elsewhere and many companies prefer to shoot interior shots in Toronto or Los Angeles.
There are a number of small sound stages in New York already, including Chelsea Piers, home to "Law and Order" and "Spin City," and Kaufman Astoria Studios in Queens, where "Sesame Street" and the "Cosby Show" are recorded. But a number of proposed projects have had difficulty finding financial support because the stages, which require a large investment, are generally at the mercy of filmmakers who do not want to pay a lot of money for short periods of work.
Union Life had planned to finance Hudson River Studios, a proposed $120 television sound stage atop the St. John's Center, at Houston and West Streets on the West Side of Manhattan. But when the developer ran into delays in acquiring additional financing, Union Life withdrew. Union Life is now considering getting involved again in Hudson River Studios, Mr. Steed said.
Last year, the Shooting Gallery, an independent film production company, announced plans to build a $75 million movie lot in Harrison, N.J. In the end, the company scaled back its plans for 15 sound stages and opened a $5 million production center in lower Manhattan.
"It's a very tough, very fluid business that's hard to keep going," said Hal G. Rosenbluth, president of Kaufman Astoria Studios. "If Harry and Bob can fill the studio with their own work then they're way ahead of the game."
The idea of building a studio at the Navy Yard originated not with Mr. De Niro, but with Cary Dean Hart and Louis Madigan, a movie-set designer and computer consultant, respectively, who proposed building a $160 million complex at the Navy Yard last June, but were unable to raise capital, despite support from the city. Sony Pictures Entertainment Company agreed to manage the studio at one point, but declined to invest in the project.
About six months ago, Mr. De Niro approached city officials about building a studio. Mr. Giuliani suggested that he look at the Navy Yard and talk to Mr. Madigan and his partner.
Mr. Hart and Mr. Madigan declined to comment yesterday.
More recently, Mr. De Niro approached Mr. Weinstein about the project. "I'm told De Niro was having dinner with Harvey Weinstein who got excited about it," one city official said. "The whole thing got serious in the last six weeks."

HEADLINE: Man Charged With Receiving 7 Years of School Pay Illegally

BYLINE: By JULIAN E. BARNES 

 BODY:
For more than seven years, James Hardy collected a paycheck, was given satisfactory evaluations and received cost-of-living raises. But in those seven years he never worked a day at his public school job, the Special Commissioner of Investigation for the New York City schools charged yesterday.
Investigators from Special Commissioner Edward F. Stancik's office arrested Mr. Hardy, 32, of Brooklyn on charges of stealing $193,259 from the school system by continuing to receive pay from an internship program that he left in 1990. 
 From September 1984 until August 1990, Mr. Stancik said, Mr. Hardy was enrolled in New York State's Substitute Vocational Assistant program. The program, which now has 47 participants, trains high school graduates for vocational teaching jobs. These interns draw a salary while getting technical training in a trade or craft and helping classroom teachers.
At the end of the five-and-a-half-year program, the participants must pass a final exam to become certified teachers. Mr. Hardy never passed that test, but he was never dismissed, either. In 1990, his salary was $25,000; by 1997, it had risen to $27,500.
During the course of the scheme, Mr. Stancik said, Mr. Hardy collected his paychecks and submitted forged time sheets as an intern at Van Arsdale High School in Brooklyn. He was found out in September 1997 after the state cut financing for the program at Van Arsdale. The principal, Bruce Billig, then met with Mr. Hardy and fired him after he admitted that he was not working for the Board of Education, according to Mr. Stancik's report.
After he was fired, Mr. Hardy tried to apply for unemployment benefits, but was met by an undercover investigator from Mr. Stancik's office in February 1998. Mr. Hardy was arrested yesterday and is in jail awaiting arraignment, the commissioner's office said. The office had no record of the name of his lawyer.
Mr. Stancik's report singles out the former and current principals at Van Arsdale High School, two former payroll secretaries at the school and two former administrators of the internship program. Mr. Stancik recommended yesterday that the current employees be disciplined and the former employees never again offered jobs.
In interviews yesterday, several of those current and former employees blamed one another or the school district for failing to catch Mr. Hardy. Carla Barnet, the payroll secretary at Van Arsdale until 1993, who now works at Canarsie High School, said that there was no way for her to know Mr. Hardy's time sheets were faked and that the high school administrators or the intern program supervisor should have caught Mr. Hardy.
Dwight Threepersons, the coordinator of the internship program from 1984 to 1990, said human resources officials should have fired Mr. Hardy.
"It was the system that failed, not me," said Mr. Threepersons, who now works for the White Plains public schools.
John Woodman, the principal at Van Arsdale from 1987 to 1993, gave Mr. Hardy six satisfactory evaluations during the time he was doing no work and not supposed to be in the program. "If he was missing for seven years, this was a mystery to me," Mr. Woodman said. "I had no reason to be looking for him."
The alleged theft undermines confidence in the city's public schools, especially its evaluation procedures, Mr. Stancik said.
"No one was paying attention," he said. "It is hard for me to have any confidence that these people are handling their other jobs properly."
A spokeswoman for the Board of Education, Margie Feinberg, said the Schools Chancellor, Dr. Rudy Crew, had not read Mr. Stancik's report yesterday afternoon and had not yet determined if he needed to take any action.

HEADLINE: I.M.F. Agrees to Make Loan, But Not Send Cash, to Russia

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
The International Monetary Fund announced a preliminary agreement tonight to lend Russia $4.5 billion over the next 18 months, the first resumption of loans since the country defaulted on its debts and devalued its currency last summer.
But in a highly unusual element of the deal that was not mentioned in tonight's announcement, the money will never actually be sent to Moscow. Instead, at the insistence of the United States Treasury and some European nations, the loans will essentially be transferred from one of the I.M.F.'s accounts to another -- allowing Russia to avoid default on money it owes the lending agency this year and next. 
 The Western allies feared that if the loan was made in the usual way, with the money transferred directly to Russia's central bank, the cash could disappear in days. That is what happened last July, when the I.M.F. sent $4.8 billion to Russia.
The deal will enable Russia to get through to elections in the year 2000 without a default to the lending agency that would undercut whatever strands of credibility the country still has with private investors.
Negotiations over the loan had sputtered along for months, as the I.M.F. had sought to extract commitments from Russia over its spending policies, reluctance to collect taxes, and failure to reform a collapsing banking system. But the renewed drive by the West to complete the deal this week reflected a wish to avoid another nasty dispute with the Russians at a moment when Moscow is being courted to play the role of mediator in the Kosovo conflict.
"We are trying to make sure that Russia does not feel even more alienated from the West," one senior official involved in the talks said earlier this week.
In a statement released tonight about the loan agreement, Michel Camdessus, the lending agency's managing director, said: "I am pleased that we have agreed with the Russian authorities on an economic program for Russia that I hope to be able to recommend to the executive board in support of a new stand-by arrangement as a new step in the cooperation between the I.M.F. and Russia. There are still a few technical elements to be settled in the next few days."
Mr. Camdessus added: "The program includes many measures in the fiscal, banking, and structural areas that the authorities have undertaken to implement, including through enacting the necessary legislation, as quickly as possible."
He said Russia also had agreed to provide the I.M.F. with an explanation of how earlier loans had been used, a veiled reference to widespread suspicions that past loans had been funneled to benefit some of Russia's wealthy oligarchs or secretly sent abroad.
The decision to lend money to Moscow without actually sending it there reflected some behind-the-scenes pressure from Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin and finance ministers from other European nations, who had argued that any credible new loan program for Russia must include safeguards to prevent a repeat of last summer's embarrassment. In mid-March, Mr. Rubin told a Congressional panel that much of the $4.8 billion pumped into Moscow's foreign exchange reserves at that time "may have been siphoned off improperly."
Mr. Rubin was referring to investigations showing that when the I.M.F. money briefly propped up the value of the ruble, the country's richest oligarchs raced to convert their cash into dollars at a favorable exchange rate. More than $4 billion flowed out of Russia nearly immediately, much of it headed for Swiss bank accounts and other safe havens.
This time, officials say, Mr. Rubin's aides made it clear to the lending agency that the United States would seek to block any effort to resume loans to Russia unless the I.M.F. retained direct control over the money at all times. That means the aid amounts to little more than rescheduling Russia's payments. The country owes the lending agency more than $5 billion over the next 18 months, and nearly $2.5 billion by the end of June. Even with the tentative agreement reached today, Russia's new loans will total less than its repayments.
"The bottom line is that the I.M.F. will get a bit more from Russia than Russia gets from the fund," one senior official involved in the talks said.
Russia's Finance Minister, Mikhail M. Zadornov, who has been in Washington all week holding talks with the I.M.F. and the World Bank, had hinted that an agreement was close earlier today, saying, "We do not have any special problems."
The chairman of Russia's central bank, Viktor S. Gerashchenko, had hoped to borrow about $4 billion.
But the agreement alone will not get Russia the money it has sought for so long. Before the board of the I.M.F. formally approves any accord, the lender is insisting that the Russian Parliament pass several bills to remake Russia's bankruptcy laws, strengthen its tax collection and overhaul its banking system. Last year, the I.M.F. transferred money to Russia before the Parliament acted -- only to discover that the Communist-led body failed to fulfill several commitments made by the Government of President Boris N. Yeltsin.
A senior European official who had been briefed on the negotiations said today that "the way the agreement is now set, not a dollar will actually enter Russia, so the risk is very small."
The lending agency's board made another demand: a report by Price Waterhouse on the operations of Fimaco, an offshore company based in Britain's Channel Islands, where the Russian central bank was secretly sending a portion of its foreign reserves. The I.M.F. was never informed by Russian officials that they were moving some of the the reserves -- presumably including some of the I.M.F. loan -- outside of Russian territory.
To this day, Russia has yet to provide a convincing explanation of why the money was sent to the offshore haven, exactly who had control over it, and who may have benefited from management fees or investment proceeds.

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: DEATH AT NOONTIME;
Inside an Accident of War: Sirens. Blast. Grief.

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: SURDULICA, Serbia, April 28

 BODY:
"We grew up together," said Momir Andjelkovic, his eyes veined and red, staring fixedly at the green, encircling hills. "He was my finest friend."
Mr. Andjelkovic stopped, dropping his clouded eyes to the mounded rubble across narrow Zmaj Jovina Street, where Aleksandar Milic, 37, died on Tuesday. Mr. Milic's wife, Vesna, 35, also died. So did his mother and his two children, Miljana, 15, and Vladimir, 11 -- all of them killed about noon when an errant NATO bomb obliterated their new house and the cellar in which they were sheltering. 
 "We lived together here for 32 years, for good and bad," Mr. Andjelkovic said. "He was a great man, a great friend."
He sobbed once, briefly. "This was a completely new house, three stories, with completely new furniture," he said, still astonished at the jagged pile of refuse where it used to stand. "It had a large basement. Everyone felt safe there. But the whole family died."
Dying with them were at least two cousins: Stanica Rasic, 21, and Dragan Manoluv, 18. Altogether, Yugoslav officials said, 20 civilians, half of them children, died in Surdulica, this small town of 11,000 people 200 miles south of Belgrade, near the Bulgarian border. At least six houses were destroyed and another 20 or so had significant damage.
A short distance from Mr. Milic's house, his father, Vojislav, head bent, sat on a broken bit of wall, cupping an unlit cigarette in his palm.
He was in shock. Two women hovered, arms around his shoulders, squeezing them through his black jacket as if he might otherwise fall over. They urged him in steady whispers to stand up, to eat something, to drink some water.
Mr. Milic rocked silently, oblivious, his head bobbing up and down with the even beat of a human heart.
NATO officials in Brussels said that this was yet another accident of war. At least one laser-guided bomb, they said, fell 200 or 300 yards from its intended target -- an army barracks and military depot previously bombed on April 6.
The barracks is at least 500 yards away. Only after Western journalists saw another large crater a half-mile from Mr. Milic's house did NATO officials concede that they "could not exclude" that another bomb might have gone astray. NATO did not say what might have caused either to do so, though they have said in the past that weather and other factors can throw the weapons off.
The other crater -- a nearly perfect scoop in the earth, 30 feet across and 20 feet deep -- had blasted three houses away on muddy Beogradska Street. The hole, slowly filling with ground water, was scattered with the debris of human life: filthy clothes, exploded sofa cushions, pill boxes, the back of an oven, the cracked circuit board from a television set.
No one died here, officials said, although at least five people were wounded.
"Death can be a blessing, too," said Borica Novkovic. The house of her son, Stanko, 35, was obliterated. He, his wife, Tatjana, and their 8-month-old daughter, Andrijana, were not in the house. "My son is not here," Mrs. Novkovic said, with pride. "Stanko is in the army now."
The noise was indescribable, she said: "The sound was like a huge blow on my head. Everything turned over and rolled down the hill.
"Radica was screaming, screaming, when we came to help her," Mrs. Novkovic added, bending to pull up her coarse woolen stocking.
Radica Rastic's house was closest to the impact. "She was taken from the house all twisted and bent over," Mrs. Novkovic said. "She was shaking and shaking, her hands were pressed tight over her ears."
Perica Jovanovic, a 44-year-old economist at the Narodni Bank, said: "I'll never forget the strange voice of the bomb. When the plane is flying and drops the bomb the noise changes. It's awful. It's like the static on the radio but so loud, and then there is this awful crash and pressure and everything moves and boils up."
Mr. Jovanovic was outside with his two children, Biljana, 13, and Nemanje, 8. "Somehow we stayed alive," he said quietly, with care. "But I think these NATO bastards will come back."
He stopped and glared.
"You should pass through that," he said. "Americans should have this experience."
He softened then, to say: "People think I'm very calm, but I'm not, I'm very upset. Here you have 11,000 people who are crazy, nervous. People died here and what was the aim? There is nothing around here."
He threw up his hands. "We were very brave for 34 days, but we've moved now," he said. "I've moved the family now to a lake in the hills."
About 200 yards from the crater, a half-finished house was badly damaged. Over the door was a handpainted sign: "Kuca na prodaju" -- house for sale -- "Tel: 85-552."
On Zmaj Jovina Street, Slobodan Milosevic, 48, the bearer of a common Serbian name, stood covered with dust in the ruins of his own house, across the street from Mr. Milic's. Photographers had scrambled up to the second floor, easily visible from the street because the entire front of Mr. Milosevic's house had been ripped away.
"This house is a ruin," he said. "To build it I worked four years in Africa -- two in Tunisia and two in Algeria." He laughed sourly. "Now I'll have to go there again."
Mr. Milosevic, a civil engineer, was out visiting relatives with his wife, Sladjana, 45, son Sasa, 25, and daughter Suzana, 22, when the bomb hit his street.
"My first thought when I saw it all was that we were very lucky not to be in the house, because we would have died," he said. "We're alive, so we will build another house."
He said he was sure that NATO had aimed for the houses. "It's impossible for the pilot to make this mistake," he said. "The sky was clear. He couldn't miss. The barracks isn't even that close."
Asked about his neighbor, Mr. Milic, Mr. Milosevic said he worked in a small factory, PES, that made components for Zastava, the huge car and munitions factory in Kragujevac that NATO destroyed. Mr. Milic also owned two small convenience stores in town, and it seemed he was returning home for lunch when the air raid sirens sounded and he and his family and neighbors went down to the basement of his fine new house. "But Momir," he said, "was really his friend."
Mr. Andjelkovic described how he went down to his own basement with his parents and emerged to see what had happened.
"I couldn't understand what was going on," he said. "There was dust everywhere, and the houses existing two minutes before -- right in front of my house -- were just gone. I heard people screaming, and one of the people down there was my uncle. We tried to dig with our hands, I made him a small hole to breathe until first aid could come."
The uncle, Stojanka Petkovic, 65, "was the only one to survive this house." He pointed to the elder Mr. Milic, still sitting frozen on the wall. "He came from somewhere else," Mr. Andjelkovic said. "He lost everyone."
Asked quietly if he thought that keeping foreign troops out of Kosovo was worth this kind of price, Mr. Andjelkovic looked up, stunned. "I don't understand the question," he said. "You shouldn't ask me that now -- we can talk about that in 10 or 15 days."
He shook his head. "We haven't buried these people and you want to ask me that?"

HEADLINE: City Council Sweetens Correction Officers' Pension Plan

BYLINE: By ABBY GOODNOUGH 

 BODY:
In a surprise vote that angered Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, the City Council approved a plan yesterday that would increase retirement benefits for correction officers, a change that the Mayor said could eventually cost the city $100 million a year.
Peter F. Vallone, the Speaker of the City Council, said correction officers deserved the extra retirement money because their jobs were among the most dangerous in the city. He pointed out that the city was already giving supplemental retirement benefits to police officers and firefighters, and said correction officers had been treated unfairly. 
 "This is just a parity bill," Mr. Vallone said yesterday afternoon, after the legislation passed, 45 to 1. "Correction officers have always been treated as stepchildren since I got to this Council, and it's simply unfair."
The plan, which needs approval from the State Legislature, would put stock market profits from the correction officers' pension plan into a separate fund that would supplement their retirement benefits. Until now, such profits have been reinvested in the pension plan itself, increasing its worth.
Aides to Mr. Giuliani complained that if the stock market profits are taken out of the pension plan, a formula that set mandatory levels of financing would mean that the city would have to make up the difference, which is expected to reach $65 million to $100 million a year eventually. Randy L. Levine, the Deputy Mayor for economic development, said he feared that other unions would seek similar changes from the Council.
"This is just a raw political giveaway that sends a very bad signal," Mr. Levine said. "It's really outrageous, because something like this should be done in collective bargaining and the union should be giving us something in exchange."
But Norman Seabrook, president of the 11,000-member union representing correction officers, said the union had requested supplemental retirement benefits in its most recent negotiations with the city, and the city had refused. In any case, Mr. Seabrook said, correction officers should not have to make any sacrifices in labor negotiations because their jobs are so stressful.
"I don't think that anyone in city government has ever worked in a jail with some of the most violent individuals in society," Mr. Seabrook said after the vote yesterday. "I don't think these individuals who enjoy such a cushy life of air-conditioned offices should criticize correction officers, or ask us to give back to anybody."
Mr. Seabrook and Mr. Vallone both pointed out that most of the city's correction officers were black or Hispanic, and said that their union was less politically powerful than the union representing city police officers, most of whom are white. The difference in race and political power, they said, made the city more willing to make concessions to the police union.
"Correction officers have not been able to obtain through collective bargaining what all the other public safety workers have gotten," Mr. Vallone said. "We're talking about people who risk their lives every day."
Mayor Giuliani said yesterday that he would urge Albany to reject the plan, but in the past the State Legislature has pushed similar proposals through. Just last month, the Legislature passed a bill giving 25,000 state correction and security officers the right to retire with a tax-free disability pension of three-quarters pay if they have any kind of heart ailment. Although the bill aroused some opposition, Gov. George E. Pataki has indicated that he will sign it.
Both the state and the city correction officers' unions have sharply stepped up their lobbying and political donations in recent years, becoming major contributors to politicians in Albany and New York City. Mr. Vallone said his decision to support the plan had nothing to do with political support.
Council leaders put the pension legislation on the agenda at the last minute yesterday, one day after the Committee on State and Federal Legislation approved it. The only Council member to vote against it was Sheldon S. Leffler, a Queens Democrat who heads the Public Safety Committee. After the vote, Mr. Seabrook made it clear that the union would not support Mr. Leffler in any future campaign.
"How dare he insult this organization and say that he doesn't care about us?" Mr. Seabrook said. "He can forget about correction officers supporting him."
But Mr. Leffler said that increased retirement benefits should come through collective bargaining, and that the correction officers should give something in return. "They have a tough job and a good case for more compensation," he said. "But this is not the way to do it."

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
That Silence In Albany? Budget Talks

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

DATELINE: ALBANY, April 28

 BODY:
Even for Albany, this is bad.
The state budget will be a month overdue on Friday, a milestone that would have made lawmakers blush a decade ago, but has become ho-hum. What amazes and concerns many legislators, lobbyists and advocates this year is not so much the lateness, as the absence of serious budget negotiations among legislative leaders and Gov. George E. Pataki. The talk of the Capitol is that there is no talk. 
 "I've never seen an April like this, where, four weeks into the fiscal year, there's a complete vacuum," said Andrew Rein, senior research associate at the Citizens Budget Commission, a nonprofit group that closely follows the process. "It's phenomenal and disturbing. There's one major thing they have to get done every year, and nobody's even really talking about it."
The usual barriers stand in the way of progress, from serious disagreement over how much to spend, particularly on education and health care, to Mr. Pataki's ever-chillier relationship with the Legislature. Nor is there evidence that the public much cares about late budgets, making it easier for lawmakers to go past the deadline.
But perhaps the more important brake on budget progress this year is a series of fundamental changes the Governor is pressing in the way budgets are proposed, negotiated and completed, part of a campaign to seize much greater power over the final product and marginalize the Legislature. Those changes have left the Legislature reeling, unsure how to proceed, and desperately searching -- so far unsuccessfully -- for a way to reassert its influence.
The stalemate has implications not just for the budget, the main business of the Legislature, but for nearly every issue taken up here. In Albany, most significant legislation is held hostage through budget negotiations, as factions use the budget as leverage on unrelated matters, and then disposed of in a flurry of deal-making.
But the later the budget goes, the harder it becomes to take care of other business before the Legislature's annual session draws to its scheduled close in June. Not coincidentally, the very late budgets of recent years have been accompanied by a steep decline in the number of bills enacted.
"I've been here a lot of years, and I thought the last few years were bad, but I've never seen such a lack of movement, on the budget or anything else," said Lester M. Shulklapper, who has been a lobbyist in Albany for 25 years.
Of course, experience has shown that when motivated, New York's lawmakers can break the logjams and pass a budget in as little as three weeks. No one here rules out something like that happening soon, but no one expects it, either.
His words drawn out by a protracted sigh, Assemblyman Herman D. Farrell Jr., the normally ebullient Manhattan Democrat who heads the Ways and Means Committee, which drafts spending bills, said, "I think it could run very, very long."
The Democratic Assembly Speaker, Sheldon Silver, and the Republican Senate majority leader, Joseph L. Bruno, have been carping at each other, and at Mr. Pataki, each trying to fix blame somewhere else.
"I talk to the Governor, I talk to Shelly, and I think they talk to each other," Mr. Bruno said. "I don't know." But he conceded that those conversations had been neither frequent nor productive. His assessment of the situation? "It's a cartoon."
Asked whether there was any progress, he said, "No, there really isn't. Not a thing."
Mr. Pataki, a Republican, takes the unusual position that the stalemate has nothing to do with him, but is entirely up to the Legislature to resolve. Asked this week whether there was anything he could do to get the process moving, he said, "Not that I'm aware of."
Mr. Silver and Mr. Pataki have not met face to face since April 6.
New York already has a record of budget tardiness unimaginable in any other state. For 15 straight years, lawmakers have failed to enact a budget by the April 1 deadline, going as late as 126 days in 1997.
This year, Mr. Pataki has proposed a $72.7 billion budget, keeping the spending increase within the rate of inflation by cutting education and health care programs. The Democrats who control the Assembly want to spend $2 billion to $4 billion more, depending on whose figures one believes, and the Senate Republicans are between the two, but closer to the Governor's numbers.
Until last year, budgets were negotiated by the Governor and the two legislative leaders in closed-door meetings, a process derided as secretive and undemocratic.
Mr. Pataki repeatedly prodded the Legislature to do what Congress does: have members of both houses meet in conference committees to hammer out their differences, then pass a budget and send it to him. Last year, the Assembly and Senate finally took him up on the idea, and it was, at first, viewed as a great success. Rather than only two legislators being directly involved in the negotiations, dozens had a hand in it.
But the new approach turned out to be a coup for the Governor. Under the old system, he was obliged to negotiate, and then to abide by the resulting compromises. In the new system, he was freed of both duties.
When the Legislature sent him its budget last year, he vetoed 1,300 spending line items, well more than $1 billion. It was a stinging defeat for the Legislature, particularly the Democrats who control the Assembly, whose pet projects were the bulk of the veto targets.
The legislative leaders say they do not want to return to the old "three men in a room" practice, and if they tried, they would risk not only harsh criticism but possible rebellion among their colleagues. They also want to avoid a repeat of last year's fiasco, but don't know how.
Mr. Pataki gave them a couple of new things to worry about this year.
First, he insisted that in exchange for approving a pay raise for the state's elected officials, that legislators' salaries be withheld as long as the budget is late. Many legislators, including some who voted for the law, complain that it gives the Governor great power to outwait them, and could cause the Legislature to cave in after a time.
Second, Mr. Pataki dramatically changed the way his budget bills were drafted, making it much harder for the Legislature to make the kinds of alterations that were once considered standard. Some legislators say the changes are illegal, though no court challenge has been mounted. Legislative leaders are still debating how to counter the Governor's move.
"The Governor wants to establish the imperial governorship," Mr. Farrell said. "He's created a series of really deep, institutional problems for us, and he's convinced we'll collapse under the pressure. He's decided this is the year to break us."
For now, Mr. Bruno and Mr. Silver are mired in a spat over whose fault it is that they have not yet begun conference committee meetings, and there is ample evidence that neither is in a hurry to start.
"I think anyone making plans for the summer is going to have to reconsider," Mr. Shulklapper said.

HEADLINE: Documents Say Microsoft Considered a Blow to Novell

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF 

 BODY:
Bent on breaking the spirit of the Novell Corporation, its Utah-based archrival in the network software market, the Microsoft Corporation in 1994 considered a recommendation that it lock up sales accounts at both the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints and Brigham Young University, documents filed yesterday in Federal court contend.
In an E-mail message to Microsoft's chairman, William H. Gates, Mike Murray, a high-ranking Microsoft executive, wrote that at a retreat for company executives he had proposed that while the accounts were not Novell's largest, they were important psychologically and emotionally. "The influence of the L.D.S. church in the Utah economy and culture is difficult to appreciate from a distance," Mr. Murray wrote. "I recommend we throw some significant weight at this account." 
 The message was part of 188 pages of evidence filed in Federal court in Salt Lake City by Caldera, a software company that is suing Microsoft for as much as $1.6 billion.
The E-mail message added: "In addition, it would be great for Billg to make time for a trip to Salt Lake City to meet with church leaders. It may be possible to leverage your mother's membership on the KIRO board."
At the time, Mrs. Gates, who has since died, was on the board of KIRO, a Seattle TV station owned by a church subsidiary, Bonneville Communications. In the end. Mr. Gates never visited Salt Lake City for this purpose and his mother never intervened, the company said today.
Caldera contends that Microsoft set out to destroy public demand for DR-DOS, an operating system that competed against Microsoft's MS-DOS. Caldera was formed by Novell's former chairman, Ray Noorda, who filed the lawsuit in July 1996 after acquiring DR-DOS.
Caldera contends that Microsoft integrated MS-DOS with its Windows graphical user interface in part to damage its competitors. Microsoft's own filings contend that DR-DOS was a flawed product and that its customers were provided with inadequate technical support.
The filings are aimed at defining the scope of claims that will be allowed at a trial now scheduled to begin next January. Until now, Microsoft has been successful at keeping much of the pretrial evidence sealed under a broad court order.
Caldera executives said they believe the evidence drawn from more than 1.5 million pages of documents filed in the case demonstrates that Microsoft used misleading product announcements to dampen DR-DOS sales and that it made false and misleading statements about DR-DOS to sow what Caldera called F.U.D. -- for fear, uncertainty and doubt -- in the computer industry.
Caldera also said the evidence showed that Microsoft developed intentional incompatibilities to make it appear to be difficult for DR-DOS to work with Windows and that it used "per-processor" and other exclusionary licensing terms to block DR-DOS in the market.
"These aren't just random E-mails," said Bryan Sparks, chief executive of Caldera. "We think this material is significant, and much of this has never been seen before because it's been under protective order."
But a Microsoft spokesman said all accusations raised by the company had previously been reviewed and rejected by the Justice Department. "This is an attempt by Caldera to rewrite the history of the high-tech industry by pasting together these handpicked excerpts," said Mark Murray, a company spokesman.
He said that in many cases the Caldera document took the E-mail comments of Microsoft executives out of context. For example, one excerpt from an E-mail sent by James Allchin, the Microsoft executive who now heads the company's Windows 2000 effort, states: "If you're going to kill someone there isn't much reason to get all worked up about it and angry -- you just pull the trigger. Any discussions beforehand are a waste of time. We need to smile at Novell while we pull the trigger."
But taken in context, Mr. Murray said, Mr. Allchin was writing an appeal to instruct Microsoft executives to stop "trash talking."
"He was saying we should just compete in a way that is more professional," he said. "He was saying this is hurting us with the customer."
The Caldera document asserts Microsoft integrated MS-DOS and Windows in an effort to damage competitors' operating systems. The document quotes Nathan Myhrvold, Microsoft's chief technology officer, as arguing for integrating the two products for a strategic reason: "We put a bullet in the head of our would-be competitors on DOS, like DRI, Desqview, DOS extenders etc." DRI was Digital Research Inc., the company that developed DR-DOS.
The Caldera accusation is not supported by history, Mr. Murray said: "This is them excerpting a small snippet of a memo and trying to create a narrative that does not reflect the facts of the situation. Even the Department of Justice has acknowledged that Windows 95 is a completely new operating system."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Hedge Funds for Wealthy May Face Tougher Scrutiny
Federal regulators are proposing to further scrutinize the trading practices of investment banks and closely examine the books of private investment funds.
While not calling for direct regulation of the investment pools for the wealthy known as hedge funds, the Clinton Administration plans to introduce legislation soon that will give the Securities and Exchange Commission and the Commodities Futures Trading Commission enhanced powers to examine the dealings of Wall Street trading firms. Page A1. 

Microsoft Is Sued By a Rival
The software company Caldera is suing Microsoft for as much as $1.6 billion, contending that Microsoft set out to destroy public demand for DR-DOS, an operating system that competed against Microsoft's MS-DOS. C4.
 
Aetna Shares Soar on Profit Report
First-quarter earnings at health insurer Aetna rose 15 percent, as it raised premiums on employer health plans and shed unprofitable Medicare business. Shares of Aetna rose $10.125, or 12.4 percent, to $92. The stock has risen more than 50 percent since Aug. 31, when shares hit a 52-week low of $60.1875. C24.
 
Dow Industrials Set Another Record
Stocks sensitive to the business cycle turned in another strong performance, while technology and other longtime market leaders sagged. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 13.74 points, to 10,845.45, a new high. The Nasdaq composite index lost 52.04 points, or 2 percent, to 2,550.37, while the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 11.89 points, to 1,350.91. C13.
 
Sony Reports a Loss for the Quarter
Sony reported a fourth-quarter net loss, and a decline of nearly 20 percent in fiscal-year earnings. Demand for its electronic products fell, as Japan's economy faltered. C5.
 
Britons Consider Missile Venture
British Aerospace acknowledged that it was exploring a deal with French and Italian companies to create a European missile industry to challenge Raytheon. C5.
 
China Toughens Trade Stance
China appears to have toughened its stance in talks aimed at gaining entry to the World Trade Organization. It is using leverage with European officials to press the United States for quicker entry. C5.
 
DaimlerChrysler Profit Rises 23%
Fueled by strong vehicle sales in the United States and healthy heavy-truck sales, DaimlerChrysler reported that first-quarter profits increased by 23 percent, exceeding analysts' forecasts. C5.
 
Rubin's Plan for Global Reform
Although Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin's proposals for reforming international financial institutions have been described as modest or cautious, the problem in the view of some prominent economists is not that they don't go far enough but that Mr. Rubin failed to outline concrete steps that would translate his ideas into action. Michael M. Weinstein: Economic Scene. C2.
 
Katzenberg Testifies in Disney Suit
Jeffrey Katzenberg, the former Walt Disney studio chief, testified in court that his 10 years at the Disney Company were "a lie" because of a dispute with Michael D. Eisner, the Disney chairman, over a huge bonus. C2.
 
Durable Goods Orders Up in March
Orders for big-ticket goods made in the United States rose a stronger-than-expected 2 percent in March. C10.

HEADLINE: More Scrutiny Is Sought for Firms That Runor Lend to Hedge Funds

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN 

 BODY:
Worried that Wall Street will soon forget its brush with financial meltdown six months ago, Federal regulators are proposing to further scrutinize the trading practices of investment banks and closely examine the books of private investment funds.
While not calling for regulators to have direct control over the investment pools for the wealthy known as hedge funds, the Clinton Administration plans to introduce legislation soon that would give the Securities and Exchange Commission and the Commodities Futures Trading Commission enhanced powers to examine the dealings of Wall Street trading firms that lend to the funds. 
 The White House will also seek Congressional backing for rules that require hedge funds, which are traditionally unregulated, to disclose more information about borrowing and trading practices even if that drives some funds out of the country.
The proposals do not seek to directly control how Wall Street firms trade or lend money, but to give regulators a closer look at their books, which would give them more time to intervene to avert a crisis.
The recommendations are found in a long-awaited Government report, the product of an investigation into Wall Street practices that began after the Government-brokered rescue of Long-Term Capital Management, a large hedge fund, in September.
The report falls short of recommending direct control over the financing and activities of hedge funds -- which would have provoked strong opposition within the financial industry -- but the authorities do plan to adopt new powers over investment banks, among the biggest lenders to Long-Term Capital. The measure would make the Wall Street houses subject to some of the same controls commercial banks face.
The recommendations are part of a long study on financial borrowing prepared by the nation's top financial regulators, led by Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin and Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve. The report was obtained by The New York Times and is expected to be released today.
Though Long-Term Capital's near-collapse -- and the turmoil it caused in world financial markets -- happened only six months ago, it is no longer a pressing concern on Wall Street in a booming market. Long-Term Capital was rescued by 14 Wall Street companies, which injected $3.6 billion at the height of the crisis. The fund has since posted solid returns and profits for its new owners.
But the bailout of Long-Term Capital was orchestrated by the Federal Reserve, the nation's central bank, and regulators said at the time that the hedge fund's failure might have prompted a worldwide financial crisis. Officials now argue that the lessons of that brush with disaster would be lost unless they adopted new measures designed to limit the risks that financial companies take.
The regulators called the amount of money that Long-Term Capital borrowed "extraordinary" and complained that many of the best-known banks and securities companies adopted "excessively liberal" lending policies. But they said the problem went well beyond the fund.
"The L.T.C.M. episode illustrates the need for all participants in our financial system, not only hedge funds, to face constraints in the amount of leverage they can assume," the report says. "Some of the same weaknesses that permitted L.T.C.M. to achieve its extraordinary market leverage were evident in other market participants."
The report uses strong language to describe the increasing tendency of financial companies to leverage bets on financial markets with borrowed money. The ratios of borrowed money to capital at the top five investment banks average 27 to 1, according to the report, a bit higher than Long-Term Capital.
The securities companies may not be much better able to withstand a sharp downturn in financial markets than that hedge fund was, the report contends. A collapse of a major Wall Street firm is widely dreaded because it could have ripple effects on financial markets everywhere.
But regulators did not propose legal limits on borrowing. Instead, they expressed hope that more intensive scrutiny of borrowing and trading practices by regulators, the public and industry rivals would prompt financial companies to reduce their risk levels voluntarily.
If they do not, however, the authorities are threatening much sterner measures. These include imposing tight capital requirements on investment banks not unlike those now imposed on commercial banks. The Government is also considering direct regulation of both hedge funds and derivatives dealers, which are now largely free of oversight.
"If these steps don't work to reduce the leverage in the system, then there are other options we can take," said a member of the President's Working Group on Financial Markets, the regulatory committee that studied the Long-Term Capital fallout. "These would be likely to raise a great deal of noise from the industry, and we are hoping that it will not be necessary to take these steps."
The report recommends these steps:
*Bank and securities regulators should insure that financial companies adopt better risk-management practices, saying that the risk-management system "broke down" when companies allowed Long-Term Capital to borrow so much money.
*Banks and brokerage houses should provide a more detailed summary of their lending to individual borrowers so that both regulators and other financial companies could spot excessive risk building up.
*The Federal authorities should take action to insure that other countries, and particular popular offshore financial centers like the Cayman Islands, toughen their regulatory policies in tandem with the strengthened United States rules.
*Congress should speed passage of updated bankruptcy laws. The laws would make it easier to unwind the intertwined financial securities a hedge fund or other big financial company holds, allowing it to collapse without severely disrupting the entire financial system.
Wall Street executives reached yesterday said they had not yet seen the report, and most declined to comment. But the proposals on the whole seem unlikely to prompt a strong protest from banks and brokerage houses. Many traditionally secretive hedge funds seem likely to bristle at the demand for more disclosure. But they are not viewed as a strong industry lobbying group, and many already operate from offshore bases.
"The hedge-fund industry will object to disclosing more than they do now; they won't like it," said Roy Smith, a professor of finance at New York University. "But it's a nuisance they brought on themselves."
As for the proposed requirements on banks and brokerage firms, Mr. Smith said they sounded moderate. "It does not appear to be a major overhaul in the way things work now," he said.
But at least two recommendations seem likely to raise at least some industry and Congressional reaction. The first is the proposal to expand the powers the S.E.C. has over investment banks and their affiliated companies. Investment banks often use affiliated companies to conduct financial transactions, including those that involve instruments known as derivatives. The affiliates are almost completely unregulated.
The S.E.C. will propose that it have more authority to police those affiliated companies just as commercial bank regulators have the power to scrutinize bank subsidiaries. The Commodities Futures Trading Commission also hopes to gain new examination powers over companies it regulates, including firms that specialize in underwriting futures contracts and other derivatives.
Specifically, the two regulatory bodies are demanding more information about trading strategies, risk management practices and record keeping at the affiliated companies. The regulators want the authority to look at trading positions one by one, not only in the aggregated way investment banks usually report them.
With those increased powers, the regulators could then put pressure on the investment banks or derivatives dealers to reduce risk levels.
One sign of a possible struggle over these proposals is noted in the report. In a footnote, the report says that Mr. Greenspan, the Fed chairman, "declines to endorse" the enhanced powers sought by the two regulatory bodies. Mr. Greenspan, it adds, "deferred" to the individual supervisory authorities in allowing the recommendations to go forward.
The second area of contention involves hedge funds. Many hedge funds now face no regulation of any kind, though a few larger ones are subject to light reporting requirements. Under the new proposals, all hedge funds of significant size would have to file quarterly reports on several measures of their risk taking, which would be published for public scrutiny. But they would not have to reveal detailed proprietary trading information the way banks and securities firms do.

HEADLINE: U.S. EASES POLICY ON SOME SANCTIONS

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration announced today that it had decided to ease its sanctions policy to permit the sale of food and medical supplies to Iran, Libya, Sudan and other nations accused of supporting terrorists.
The new policy, which had long been sought by farm-state lawmakers and lobbyists for several large corporations, will make a general exemption for food and medicine whenever trade sanctions are put on countries considered adversaries of the United States. 
 The shift would not directly affect trade sanctions against Cuba, Iraq and North Korea, in part because American companies are already permitted to carry out some trade in food, medicine and other supplies with those countries.
Stuart E. Eizenstat, Undersecretary of State for Business and Economic Affairs, said that President Clinton had approved the policy after a two-year review concluded that the sale of food and medicine "doesn't encourage a nation's military capability or its ability to support terrorism."
In fact, he told reporters, a ban on such sales can backfire by eroding support for American foreign policy, by depriving American companies and farmers of the chance to sell their goods and by harming innocent civilians abroad who are deprived of needed food and medicine.
Joe Lockhart, the White House spokesman, said President Clinton had decided that "food should not be used as a tool of foreign policy, except under the most compelling circumstances."
The policy shift reflects a growing sense, in the corridors of the White House and on Capitol Hill, that sanctions are not an effective way to make foreign policy and punish rogue states.
Business leaders have long argued that economic sanctions rarely produce changes in foreign governments and instead often taints American corporations as unreliable suppliers.
Human rights groups disagree, insisting that economic sanctions have been a factor in the collapse of pariah governments, notably the apartheid Government of South Africa.
Cargill, Farmland Industries and a number of other large agricultural and commodity-trading companies had lobbied intensively for the policy change.
USA Engage, a powerful coalition of more than 670 companies that has sought an easing of American trade sanctions, welcomed the Administration's announcement.
"This is good news for American agriculture, it's good news for U.S. producers of farm equipment and it's an outstanding development for American foreign policy," said Bill Lane, Washington director of Caterpillar Inc. and the coalition's chairman.
Clinton Administration officials insisted that today's move was not meant to be a gesture of friendship toward Iran, but they acknowledged that the action would doubtless encourage improved ties between the United States and the Government of President Mohammad Khatami, who is perceived to be a relative moderate among Iranian leaders.
The policy shift comes only two weeks after President Clinton made strikingly conciliatory comments about Iran during a public appearance in the White House in which he saluted that nation's "enormous geopolitical importance over time" and said that it had "been the subject of quite a lot of abuse from various Western nations."
An American exporter, Niki Trading Company, recently sought a special export license from the Treasury Department to sell millions of tons of food products, mostly grain, for about $500 million, to Iran, a deal that now seems likely to be approved.
Administration officials had seen the deal as a promising line of contact with Iran after two decades of bitter hostility following the 1979 Iranian revolution.
While the State Department labeled Iran last year as the "the most active state sponsor of terrorism," the Administration is hopeful that easing the sanctions might help give Mr. Khatami leverage that could allow him to rein in rival factions of the Government that support anti-American terrorist groups.
Under the policy shift, the sale of food, medicine and other "human necessities" would be included in trade sanctions only if a nation is in armed conflict with the United States or is diverting food and medical supplies for military purposes.
Today's move by the Administration would not affect trade with Cuba, which is limited principally by Congressional sanctions.
Mr. Eizenstat noted that the sale of medicine and medical supplies to Cuba was already allowed under legislation approved in 1992, and the regulated sale of food, pesticides and related items was permitted under a policy shift announced in January.
American companies are allowed to sell food, medicine and other goods to Iraq under the so-called oil-for-food program overseen by the United Nations.
American companies are already permitted to trade food, medicine and other goods to North Korea under a deal brokered by the State Department to end that country's program to develop nuclear weapons.
The United States Government has provided tens of thousands of tons of donated food to North Korea as a result of the widespread famine in that country.
Few of the countries affected by today's decision offer large markets to the United States. With a population of nearly 70 million people and its oil riches, Iran is a notable exception.
The official Iranian news agency reported this week that more than 15 percent of Iran's food was imported last year, worth about $3 billion. It imported about 3.8 million tons of grain in 1998.
Iran had been eager to resume trade with the United States. In August, it lodged an official complaint with the United Nations, asserting that the American sanctions had created a "scarcity of essential goods needed for the improvement of the nutritional and health-care standards of the Iranian people."
The decision to permit trade in food and medicine could also ease tension somewhat with Libya.
A variety of international economic sanctions on Libya were lifted earlier this month when two suspects accused of bombing Pan Am Flight 103 in 1988 were handed over to prosecutors in the Netherlands. Separate American trade sanctions remain in place.
