HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE WHITE HOUSE;
Clinton, Asking Quick Approval of Funds, Talks of a Campaign of Many Months

BYLINE: By JOHN M. BRODER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
President Clinton warned today that NATO's bombardment and economic pressure on Yugoslavia would persist for many more months and called on Congress to quickly pass his request for $6 billion in emergency funds to pay the mounting costs of the conflict.
"Each day, our military campaign takes a toll on Serbia's machinery of repression," Mr. Clinton said in a Rose Garden statement after a meeting on the Balkan conflict with 46 members of Congress. "The Serbian leadership has failed to divide us and will not outlast us. The combined military might and moral determination of Europe and North America will endure." 
 But hours later, the House dealt the White House a sharp rebuke, voting not only to ban use of ground troops without Congressional approval but rejecting a symbolic resolution supporting the air campaign.
The President said that additional air power was arriving in the region and allied pilots were beginning to fly round the clock on missions to destroy Serbian troops who are terrorizing the ethnic Albanian population of Kosovo, a province of Yugoslavia. The Serbs have already driven hundreds of thousands of Kosovo's people out of the province.
Mr. Clinton said that the air campaign, today ending its fifth week, had been hampered by rain and clouds and said the pace would now pick up as the skies clear.
"Historically, the weather is better in May than in April, better in June than in May, better in July than in June," Mr. Clinton said in a clear sign that he intends to prosecute the air war for months. "And I feel very strongly that we should stay with and be very strong in determination to pursue our strategy, as well as the very important decisions we made at the NATO conference to intensify the economic pressure."
Mr. Clinton also said that signs of dissension were beginning to appear in Belgrade, including public criticism by senior officials of Slobodan Milosevic, the Yugoslav President.
Senator Olympia J. Snowe, Republican of Maine, said that Mr. Clinton pleaded for forbearance from lawmakers during their meeting, saying they had to give air power time to work to undermine Mr. Milosevic.
But she said that the Administration's "gradualism and incrementalism" were frustrating many in Congress, who want quicker consideration of the use of ground troops to end the war.
Ms. Snowe and other members of Congress who attended today's session with the President said he repeated his determination not to dispatch ground troops to Kosovo.
"On the contrary," said Senator John Kerry, Democrat of Massachusetts. "He indicated that he intended a stepped-up prosecution of the air war and the blockade of oil supplies."
But other members said that Mr. Clinton's aims -- the removal of Serbian forces from Kosovo and the return of hundreds of thousands of ethnic Albanian refugees -- could not be achieved with air power alone.
Representative Sonny Callahan, an Alabama Republican, said the Administration had been sending confusing signals about the goals of the war, its costs and the Administration's plans for relocating refugees.
"The messages coming out of the White House have a great deal of ambiguity," Mr. Callahan said. "But it's very obvious to me that this is going to be a prolonged and costly endeavor."
Mr. Clinton urged the lawmakers to move quickly on his funding request, which would provide money to replace munitions already used and to build weapons stockpiles for a lengthy air campaign. The Administration's $6 billion package also includes about $1 billion for food and shelter for refugees displaced by the fighting in Kosovo.
The President said that Congress should resist the temptation to attach to the legislation money for unrelated activities, no matter how worthy, because it would slow the bill's progress.
"We must get a Kosovo funding measure passed and to my desk now," the President said.
Draft proposals in the Senate and the House would add $6 billion to $7 billion in Pentagon programs, bringing the emergency spending bill requested by Mr. Clinton to roughly $12 billion to $13 billion.
The Congressional add-ons include projects arguably unconnected to NATO's operations in the Balkans. For example, the House bill would spend hundreds of millions of dollars on construction programs sought by the military to increase soldiers' morale and efficiency, such as refurbishing 50-year-old barracks, building car washes and garages, and paving parking lots at bases in Germany.
Many Republicans are eager to approve them as a show of support for the military, and to bolster their argument that Mr. Clinton has starved the Pentagon. But the emergency money must come from surplus Social Security revenues, which both parties have pledged to keep their hands off.
At least five Republican senators, loath to break their vows of fiscal abstinence, are ready to break with their colleagues instead of supporting a $12 billion emergency bill, according to senior aides. They want the spending limited to what the President has requested. Others want to see even more money added to the bill.
Given the intensity of the internal debate, it will be at least two weeks before any money is approved.
In his White House remarks, Mr. Clinton said that the terror in Kosovo was the product of a "meticulously planned" campaign by Mr. Milosevic's Serbian troops to drive the ethnic Albanian majority out of the province. "A campaign organized by the Government of Belgrade for a specific political purpose -- to maintain its grip over Kosovo by ridding the land of its people," Mr. Clinton said.
Before the expulsions began, ethnic Albanians made up about 90 percent of Kosovo's population of two million.
Mr. Clinton cited continuing reports of atrocities, including one unconfirmed report brought to him by Brian Atwood, the Administration's humanitarian coordinator for the region. Mr. Atwood, who just returned by Albania, said he had been told of one village in Kosovo where most of the men were rounded up, tied together and burned alive.
"It's the kind of story that would be too horrible to believe if it were not so consistent with what so many refugees have been saying," the President said.

HEADLINE: It Dawns on Hartford: Patriots May Never Arrive

BYLINE: By MIKE ALLEN 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, April 28

 BODY:
The idea of luring a major league team like the New England Patriots to this blighted insurance town between the lights of New York and Boston had seemed so grand, so exciting, so downright unlikely, that many Hartford boosters had feared that they would wake up from their blissful dream.
Sure enough, that seemed to be happening this morning, when the breakfast television shows were dominated by shots of Gov. Paul Cellucci of Massachusetts, surrounded by legislative and business leaders, declaring shortly before midnight that his state was putting aside years of bitter negotiations with the Patriots' owner, Robert K. Kraft, and offering a package of subsidies for a new football stadium next to the one at Foxboro, just south of Boston. 
 The fate of the stadium is far from decided; Mr. Kraft, in fact, has taken no public position on his home state's offer. But to many people here, and to executives of the National Football League itself, momentum appeared to be moving rapidly away from Hartford and toward Boston.
In some quarters here, the stadium was even being discussed in the past tense. "We would have loved to have had it," said State Representative Annette W. Carter, a Democrat from Hartford.
The N.F.L. issued a statement saying, "The Massachusetts political leadership's agreement last night regarding a stadium proposal is a step in the right direction towards a Patriots alternative in Foxboro."
Officials in Connecticut are so concerned that the deal is slipping away that they are working on a possible suit against the N.F.L., which helped broker the Massachusetts plan, charging the league had undermined the state's contract with the Patriots.
"We're not going to sit by idly while our legal rights get trampled," said State Senator George C. Jepsen, a Democrat of Stamford who is the majority leader. "We believe that a wide range of people, including taxpayers and the trade unions that would have had all these construction jobs, would be damaged," he said.
The outcome of the stadium tug-of-war is likely to determine the future face of Hartford, which had received a psychic lift from the football frenzy. The loss of the stadium would dash hopes for renewing the city's downtown, since it would sink, or at least stall, an ambitious hotel and convention center complex that was to be built around the stadium.
Losing the deal would also be potentially devastating for Gov. John G. Rowland, a second-term Republican who insisted that he had "bonded" with Mr. Kraft in their months of talks and who has said he would stake his legacy on the outcome of the huge gamble on the Patriots.
For their part, leaders of the Massachusetts effort said that if Mr. Kraft resists their overture, they were prepared to make their case directly to his fellow league owners, three-fourths of whom must approve any move by a team. The proposed Patriots' move is scheduled for a vote by owners in late May.
The recent back-and-forth over the Patriots represents an escalation in the pattern of demanding owners who extract lavish offers from competing cities. Now, it appears, a victorious city cannot rest easily even after a contract has been signed.
The Massachusetts offer came not just with the blessing of the N.F.L., but at its instigation. A league executive first flew into Boston on Jan. 6, three weeks after Connecticut had approved its deal, and the league has been working ever since to promote an alternative to the Hartford move. League executives are concerned because of the disruption to the team's fans and because the Patriots would be leaving the nation's 6th-largest television market for the 27th.
The league, which in recent years has lost teams in Los Angeles and other major markets, has become more aggressive in trying to keep its teams in big cities, knowing that its lucrative contracts with television networks depend on high ratings in those markets.
The Boston-Hartford situation is slightly different in that Patriots games would continue to be televised in Boston. Even so, media analysts said the league could expect an erosion of viewership in Boston if the team were playing two hours away. "Fans try to be loyal at first, but that rarely lasts," said Terri L. Ritenour, a sports analyst for Paul Kagan Associates, in Carmel, Calif.
In Connecticut, today was a day for gallows humor, with legislators and lobbyists chatting about all they could do with the $100 million that was supposed to be spent this year on a stadium for the Patriots.
Critics of the Connecticut stadium deal, who have long feared that Mr. Kraft was using the state's generous offer as a $1 billion bargaining chip to help him extract an acceptable offer from his home state, felt vindicated.
"I hate to be the crepe-hanger at the funeral, but there's nothing worse than being caught trying to buy self-respect," said William E. Curry Jr., a former Connecticut Comptroller and State Senator who ran against Mr. Rowland in 1994.
Mr. Rowland, who typically talks off the cuff, prepared carefully before going before television cameras today, armed with 13 talking points, including No. 11, "Nothing has changed." Even so, he looked a bit rattled as he faced the huge pack of Boston and Connecticut journalists keeping what he calls "the death watch."
"I know that people would like to see this fall apart -- it's not going to fall apart," he said. "If I quote my critics they say this is one of the best plans in the country. Why would he walk away from this?"
Mr. Rowland knew he would be accused of giving away the store when he signed his stadium deal, knew the site was contaminated by coal tar and toxins, and knew he was dealing with a canny owner who was sure to wind up with the upper hand.
But for all the hurdles Mr. Rowland had faced with the happy-go-lucky air he has kept from his days as a fraternity president, his aides say he was blindsided by still another: the clout of the N.F.L.
According to people who have talked privately with Mr. Rowland, he feels betrayed. The N.F.L. Commissioner, Paul Tagliabue, had called to congratulate him the night he signed the deal with Mr. Kraft.
On Thursday, according to Mr. Rowland, Mr. Tagliabue had phoned to assure him that he was "just listening" to the Boston officials -- that it was his responsibility to be sure every avenue had been explored. The very next day, according to people who were in the meetings, Mr. Tagliabue was aggressively lobbying business and political leaders to offer a deal to Mr. Kraft.
In response, Greg Aiello, the N.F.L.'s spokesman, said: "In order to make a recommendation to the owners, he has to be able to explore all alternatives. To develop an alternative you have to be aggressive. That's not inconsistent."
In Massachusetts, meanwhile, business and civic leaders were kicking off a drive to sell sky boxes -- at prices running up to $325,000 a year -- in the stadium they were suddenly hoping to build. Among the leaders of the stadium effort is the Rev. J. Donald Monan, the Chancellor of Boston College, who said the city's leaders had realized that retaining the Patriots "wasn't just a matter of sports."
"This was a sport that was a business which has a tremendous economic impact on the Commonwealth, and we thought that it was a real tragedy for the team to leave the state," he said.
The Patriots issued a statement saying the team was "not involved with any efforts to develop, or discussions pertaining to, a new Massachusetts stadium." Mr. Kraft has until Sunday to exercise one of several clauses in his deal with Connecticut that would allow him to walk away from the deal with no penalty.
The Massachusetts group is proposing a new stadium on the site of a former horse track at Foxboro that is now used for special tailgate parties.
Mr. Kraft would have to pay to build the $250 million stadium, and the state would pay $70 million for related improvements, including roads, parking lots, utilities and landscaping. The offer includes a requirement that the team pay the state $1 million each year for using the improvements. The state also would receive a $400,000 annual fee from some of the stadium's parking lots.
The Connecticut offer is a much better financial deal for Mr. Kraft. The state would pay the entire $375 million cost of the stadium, although he has agreed to invest $70 million in a hotel in the development. The Boston leaders are banking on the fact that he would accept the less favorable terms in exchange for the stability of keeping the same fans.
But N.F.L. officials warned in their statement that "other essential steps must be taken" by the group beyond Tuesday night's offer, "the most immediate being a long-term commitment by the business community to support the stadium project through the purchase of suites and club seats."
The Patriots' lobbyist in Connecticut, Jay Malcynsky, who was a senior adviser to Mr. Rowland's re-election campaign last year, said the Massachusetts deal was similar to ones that Mr. Kraft had rejected in the past, even without the lucrative Connecticut offer in his back pocket.
"You can put a tuxedo on a scarecrow, but it's still a scarecrow," Mr. Malcynsky said. "These are desperate people in desperate times, scrambling to at least proffer the illusion that there's a viable alternative in Boston."

HEADLINE: Federal Panel to Recommend Some Gambling Restrictions

BYLINE: By BRETT PULLEY

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
A Federal commission is preparing to recommend to Congress and the President a moratorium on gambling expansion and more Federal regulation of the giant casinos on Indian reservations.
But after two years of studying the economic and social effects of gambling, the National Gambling Impact Study Commission is likely to issue a report that, as expected, leaves major commercial casino operators free of Federal regulation. 
 In addition to the moratorium, the commission, meeting here for two days this week, is likely to recommend that all forms of Internet gambling be prohibited, that state lotteries curtail advertising and that states that sponsor lotteries be required to contribute some of their revenues to treatment programs for problem gamblers.
Although the nine-member commission, which includes gambling supporters and opponents alike, is not expected to advocate any Federal taxes or regulating of the country's casino industry, it does appear that more Federal oversight of casinos operated by Indian tribes will be recommended.
The commission was created in 1996, and at the time casino operators feared that it would lead to Federal policies that could stifle their businesses and impose taxes and layers of regulation. But after vigorous lobbying by the casino industry, the panel's major recommendations are likely to be aimed mostly at other forms of gambling, and even those recommendations could face tough opposition. Before the recommendations could take effect, they would have to be approved by Congress, state legislatures or tribal governments.
"The battle over the commission and the hearings have educated the American people about the money and power" that the $40 billion-a-year casino industry has, said Representative Frank R. Wolf, the Virginia Republican who sponsored the legislation that led to the commission's formation. Mr. Wolf, an immovable opponent of gambling, said he was pleased, nonetheless, that the commission was "putting the spotlight" on the industry.
"There will be a lot of information and data in the report," he said. "You had to have the commission report to take these guys on."
The commission faces a June 18 deadline for its report, which will be delivered to Congress, the President and tribal governments. Although no drafts of the report have been released, it has become apparent in commission meetings, which are open to the public, that there is some disagreement on the panel. While members agree on issues like raising the minimum age for gambling to 21 and banning Internet gambling, they remain divided over the extent of compulsive gambling in the country and whether the social costs of legalized gambling's growth outweigh the economic benefits.
"Although there is now a great deal of consensus, on some issues that just won't happen," said Kay C. James, the commission chairman, who resigned this week as a dean at Regent University in Virginia.
With some form of gambling now legal in 47 states, commission members are expected to express concern in their report about the speed with which gambling has spread since the last Federal study, in 1976.
One of the most heated debates here today concerned whether a moratorium should be imposed on expansion of gambling, which would include any form of gambling a state or local jurisdiction does not currently have. Some commissioners argued that such a move would give local governments a chance to evaluate the impact of their gambling operations before adding more.
But others commissioners, including J. Terrence Lanni, the chairman and chief executive of the MGM Grand casino in Las Vegas, said that recommending a moratorium was not necessary.
"When people hear the word 'moratorium,' they think, 'That's it. They want to end it,' " Mr. Lanni said.
After a majority of commissioners voted to recommend a moratorium, Mr. Lanni said he might submit a minority report.
Commission members considered expanding regulation of Indian casinos because they have had too much difficulty getting information from tribes about their gambling operations. While the commission members say they have agreed to respect the sovereign status of the tribes, they are taking aim at the National Indian Gaming Commission, a part of the Department of the Interior that oversees tribal gaming.
"There is a concern that there needs to be more Federal funding for regulating," said Robert W. Loescher, an Alaska businessman who is the sole Indian on the panel.
But tribal leaders contend that they are already regulated, and they are likely to oppose any further efforts to do so.
Besides the content of the report, commission members are also wrestling with its tone.
"What's missing in my view is an explicit recognition of the fact that the American people love to gamble," said John W. Wilhelm, president of the Hotel Employees and Restaurant Employees International Union, which represents 75,000 casino employees. "While I don't think it's the only thing to say, I certainly don't think it should be ignored."
The commission has traveled around the country to explore the consequences of gambling's expansion, and it has also financed several studies to help reach its conclusions. One study found that more than 5 million Americans are considered problem or pathological gamblers, and an additional 15 million are at risk. Another study found that an estimated 1.8 million American adults and 1.1 million young people between the ages of 12 and 17 exhibit severe, or "pathological" gambling behavior each year.
But gambling advocates on the panel said the language in the studies on compulsive gambling was misleading and exaggerated the dangers. They felt, for example, that it was unfair to say that all 15 million people who have gambled at least once to relieve uncomfortable feelings should be classified as "at risk."
Opponents of gambling were unhappy that one study found that the economic cost of gambling addictions is about $5 billion a year -- for things like legal fees for divorce, court and jail costs, bankruptcies and lost wages -- much less than gambling opponents have estimated.
Although it does not appear that the commission will recommend action that would severely affect the nation's large casino companies, some members want to make sure it reflects a concern about the role the casino industry plays in society.
"At one time gambling was rare and considered exceptional," said Richard C. Leone, the former New Jersey Treasurer and now president of the Century Fund in New York.
Gambling now is "common and unexceptional," Mr. Leone said. "The point is bring home to people that the change has been dramatic."
Raising the minimum age for all gambling could affect race tracks, which in many states allow 18-year-olds to wager. States with legalized casinos have already set the minimum age at 21, but the commission agreed that stricter enforcement of the age requirements was needed.
The final report is expected to be highly critical of the lottery industry. Some panelists argued in favor of referring to politicians as "addicted to the revenue" that comes from state-sponsored gambling. Ultimately, they agreed to change the language, but they are still expected to call for independent boards to oversee regulation of lotteries. They are also likely to recommend that states stay away from video lotteries.
One study financed by the commission found that about 5 percent of lottery players buy over half of the tickets and that poor people spend a greater portion of their income on lottery tickets than other players.
Although it is expected to be extremely difficult for one country to regulate activity on the worldwide Internet, the commission appears to be unanimous in its belief that action must be taken to stop the hundreds of millions in dollars that are bet with on-line gambling companies. Although the companies are based outside the United States, American legislators have been trying to pass legislation that would make it difficult for them to do business with customers in this country.

HEADLINE: Big Steel's Problems Are Home Grown;
Low-Cost U.S. Mills Play a Bigger Role Than Imports in Eroding Profits

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE 

DATELINE: WEIRTON, W. Va.

 BODY:
It was a scene that summed up so much about this quintessential steel town, here where the Alleghenies roll seamlessly into Appalachia.
Beneath a handwritten sign saying "Buy America or Bye America," Mark Glyptis, a union boss and proud of it, was counseling two laid-off steelworkers at the union hall. Sunday hams are now available, he told them, but yes, unemployment benefits for Weirton Steel's idled workers were running dry. 
 As an awkward silence filled the room, Mr. Glyptis broke it with a vague but supportive message to the glum workers: "You guys are great. Don't give up the fight."
What they are fighting for, Mr. Glyptis says, is nothing less than their jobs, their way of life and the fate of the American steel industry. Weirton is still reeling from events that started last year in lands far away -- Asia, Russia and Latin America -- where economic turmoil and sagging currencies sent cheap steel flooding to American shores, pushing down prices here. For Weirton, this meant dropping its prices, shutting one of the company's two blast furnaces and, even though employees own a healthy chunk of the stock, laying off 1,000 workers -- nearly a quarter of the work force -- just before Christmas.
Now Weirton's workers, along with those of other big, old-line steel companies, have taken their pain to Washington, going by the bus load to promote a measure that would impose quotas on imported steel and roll back imports to pre-1998 levels. The quota bill passed the House by an overwhelming majority in March, and is before the Senate.
Critics deride this legislation as dangerous, illegal and unnecessary. Not only do they say that it would cost American consumers an estimated $6 billion, but it would violate world trade agreements and could lead to a trade war. Two weeks ago, the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, waded into the debate, saying such protectionist efforts were both futile and harmful.
For Big Steel, the plea for protection has come periodically for a quarter-century. But, many say, this cry has an increasingly hollow ring. Rather, these companies are at the wrong end of a number of trends, mostly inevitable, mostly unfavorable. More troubling than imports is the competition posed by lower-cost, more efficient American minimill steel companies, which account for nearly half the steel market.
"We no longer need these giant facilities to make steel," said Robert Crandall, a steel expert at the Brookings Institution. "Minimills are building sheet plants and making life miserable for the big guys. We're talking about a long, drawn out endgame for Big Steel."
Yet it is a measure of the enduring power of Big Steel, which is far from disappearing, that so much political effort is being devoted to saving a shrinking industry. After all, today there are only 160,000 steelworkers, compared with 400,000 as recently as 1980. And, in financial weight, steelmaking pales in comparison with the technology sector: The stock market value of a single Internet company, Amazon.com, is twice that of the entire steel industry.
In places like Weirton, this changing reality carries painful human costs.
 
An Industry Divided Against Itself
Big Steel -- consisting of integrated steel companies, like Weirton -- is dependent on a more than century-old process to turn iron ore, limestone and coal into finished steel. With huge blast furnaces and oxygen furnaces, this is the same process used by Ernest T. Weirton in 1909 when he founded the company and the town.
In reality, though, there are two American steel industries, one growing and the other not; one profitable, the other struggling. Better times are had by steel minimills like the Nucor Corporation in Charlotte, N.C., turning cheap scrap metal into steel with efficient electric-arc furnaces, largely nonunion work forces and lower costs.
Output from minimills has surged in the 1990's, after the development of a technique called thin-slab casting in 1989. The technique enabled minimills to start making the flat-rolled steel that only big integrated mills like Weirton, LTV Group and Bethlehem Steel could produce before. As a result, minimills, which had a fraction of domestic output when founded in the early 1980's, now account for almost 50 percent of domestic steel output.
The sad irony is that the pain felt in Weirton comes just as the appetite for steel -- for the sport utility vehicles, washing machines, shopping malls and office buildings Americans want -- is at an all-time high.
By many measures, the American steel industry is doing quite nicely. Driven by the booming economy, American steel consumption reached a record 138 million tons in 1998, up from 96 million tons in 1987.
This means most American steel mills have been running nearly flat out, at up to 95 percent of operating capacity -- easily outpacing the rest of manufacturing. And that still is not enough to meet demand.
As a result, for the last decade, steel imports have been necessary to fill the gap between what American steelmakers are producing and what Americans consume. Despite the role of foreign steel in last year's short-term price plunge, throughout the 1990's, steel imports accounted for more than 20 percent of the domestic steel market. In fact, American steel companies themselves, including Weirton, buy a quarter of this foreign steel -- mainly raw slabs that become finished steel.
American steelmakers have also made enormous strides in increasing productivity and efficiency.
"What steel crisis?" John Reilly, a steel industry consultant at Nathan Associates in Arlington, Va., asked. "There has been some turmoil in the marketplace. But certainly, there is no crisis in the steel industry. The demise of the steel industry is nowhere on the near horizon."
 
A Future of Doubt For One Steel Town
The mill that stands on Main Street in this town of 22,000 once employed 13,500. Today, it has 4,300 workers, with 780 of them still furloughed.
To Mr. Glyptis, the union boss -- who remembers when President Clinton campaigned in Weirton in 1992 promising to protect American steel from foreign "dumping" -- steelworkers are now being sacrificed on the altar of globalism and open markets.
"Clinton's not welcome here and neither is Gore," said Mr. Glyptis, whose organization, the Independent Steelworkers Union, has removed the President's picture from the union hall, leaving a bare spot on the wall. "I'd run them out of town."
Around town, handwritten signs displayed in store windows say "Let's Buy Steel" and "Don't Let Clinton 'Steel' Our Jobs." At Renee's Cafe, at the bottom of a menu featuring homemade cakes, pies and 50-cent hot dogs, is the postscript "Stand Up for Steel!!!"
With Weirton paying up to $60,000 a year, in a town where a five-bedroom house rents for $350 a month, employees rarely want to leave.
Aaron Bennett, 25, is a third-generation steelworker, laid off and working as a courtroom magistrate to support his wife and four children on a salary of $20,000 a year.
"We did everything we needed to do to make our mill the best," Mr. Bennett said. "But this has a lot to do with economics and world affairs."
"Thank You Sen. Rockefeller, Sen. Byrd," read signs outside the union hall, referring to the state's two Democratic United States Senators, John D. Rockefeller 4th and Robert C. Byrd. They are spearheading the Senate quota bill -- following Richard A. Gephardt, minority leader from Missouri, who led the House fight.
Senator Byrd, chairman of the House Appropriations Committee, also tucked a long-shot rider onto a pending hurricane relief bill to provide $1 billion in loan guarantees for steelmakers. In Washington, this rider is known as the Weirton Bill.
Either measure could give some much-needed relief to Weirton Steel. But even at the best of times, profits are razor thin. In 1998, the company lost $6.1 million on revenue of $1.25 billion, and more losses are expected this year. Weirton's stock is now at $2 a share.
The company is so mired in debt from a recent modernization program that nearly every dollar it earns is devoted to interest payments to its debt holders. And like many steelmakers, Weirton cannot take advantage of low interest rates to reduce its borrowing costs because there is virtually no market for steel-industry bonds.
Weirton's situation is especially poignant considering that in 1984, with its former parent company, National Steel, threatening to close the mill, Weirton was bought by its employees for $386 million in one of America's largest employee stock ownership plans. Today, employees own 24 percent, having sold off chunks in the public markets to raise money.
"Our whole community is on pins and needles," Dean Harris, Weirton's Mayor and a steelworker, said. "If I lose my job, where do I go? How do we take care of the dreams of our children?"
 
Imports Take A Growing Toll
Although Big Steel has railed against imports for years, last year's surge was particularly significant. Over two months, the price of flat-rolled steel fell to $250 a ton from more than $320. "When prices get down to the $250-a-ton level, the whole industry is losing money," said Richard K. Riederer, Weirton's chief executive.
Still, Big Steel suffered much more than did minimills.
In the 1998 fourth quarter, when the foreign pricing pressures were most severe, the nation's 11 biggest integrated-steel companies were able to sell twice as much steel as the 14 largest minimills -- $5.9 billion in combined revenue, compared with the minimills' $2.6 billion.
Despite greater sales, the big mills' higher production costs meant they earned less than minimills, which recorded operating profits of $199 million for the period, compared with $138 million for big mills.
Since then, steel prices have recovered somewhat, rising last month to around $290 a ton. This came after the Commerce Department imposed anti-dumping tariffs against Brazil and Japan and negotiated a trade agreement.
Despite these events, Weirton is in a bind.
The company's cash cow is a tin mill accounting for 40 percent of its revenue. Though company executives take comfort that tin is rarely imported and is not made by minimills, the market for tin -- mainly for food cans -- has stopped growing.
And the bulk of Weirton's output, around 60 percent of revenue, comes from rolled steel, a commodity subject to the vagaries of global pricing.
Weirton is dabbling in new businesses, forming a steel-trading E-commerce Web site, entering joint ventures with overseas mills and inventing new specialty products like steel roofing shingles. But these early efforts have yet to pay off.
"It's not that we haven't done things right," Mark Roach, a Weirton steelworker, said. "We've spent money on modernization. We just can't compete with slave wages. Until recently, we were the largest employer in the state. It's kind of embarrassing to say now Wal-Mart is."
 
Can Outside Help Forestall the End?
For big integrated mills, it was not supposed to be this way. Over the last decade, to compete globally, the American steel industry pumped $50 billion into a huge modernization that paid off handsomely -- though with a human cost.
Twenty years ago, it took 10 man-hours to produce a ton of steel; today, that is down to under 4. Steel is being produced with fewer than 2 man-hours a ton at minimills, and, remarkably, even at Weirton, too.
With fewer jobs, most workers left at places like Weirton are in their mid-40's.
"We used to have an influx of young bodies," Mr. Harris, Weirton's Mayor, said. "We'd have 20, 25 guys in the hot mill. Now it's 8. You can walk for long periods in the mill and not see anyone."
Yet, even as Weirton and other steel companies lobby in Washington for protection against these trends, some say it may not matter.
"The handwriting is on the wall for the big integrated steel mills," said Daniel T. Griswold, a trade expert at the Cato Institute. "There is nothing that Congress can do about it."
Even Mr. Bennett, the laid-off steelworker, hedged his optimism: "Even if they resolve this thing fast and they call everyone back, it doesn't mean it's over. If I leave this magistrate's job, should I go back? But there really is nothing else around here."

An article in Business Day on Thursday about the effect of minimills on the United States steel industry misstated the surname of the founder of Weirton Steel. He was Ernest T. Weir, not Weirton.

Graph: "Scrap Metal, Scrapping Workers"
Although demand for steel is at a record high, increased competition from imports and the rise of minimills using recycled materials have led to much greater efficiency, sharply cutting the need for steelworkers. Graph tracks tons of raw steel produced and tons of total consumption, from 1979 through 1998. It also tracks the time it takes one worker to produce one ton of steel and the total number of steel industry employees, over the same period. (Sources: Steel Manufacturers Association; American Iron and Steel Institute; Bureau of Labor Statistics)(pg. C1)

SECTION: Section E; Part 2; Page 39; Column 1; Leisure/Weekend Desk 

LENGTH: 1126 words

HEADLINE: ANTIQUES;
Eames Chairs Are Bending The Market

BYLINE: By Wendy Moonan 

 BODY:
In 1928 Charles Eames, an architecture student, was kicked out of Washington University. Then he went on to have one of the most distinguished careers in design.
Eames's legacy is being celebrated in an exhibition that has been touring Europe and that opens at the Library of Congress in Washington on May 20. It will remain there through Sept. 14; in October it moves to the Cooper-Hewitt National Design Museum in Manhattan. The show, "The Work of Charles and Ray Eames: A Legacy of Invention," also honors the contributions of Eames's wife, Ray, who worked with him. The accompanying book, published by Harry N. Abrams, costs $49.50. 
 The exhibition is part of a renewed interest among auction houses, galleries and museums in Eames and in 20th-century design in general.
"Twentieth-century design is stronger than ever," said Mark McDonald, owner of the Gansevoort Gallery in Manhattan. "As the century comes to a close, mid-century things are now antiques." He continued: "Americans were at the forefront of design in the postwar period. There was a concentration of talent -- people like Florence Knoll, the Eameses, George Nelson, Noguchi -- and a market fueled by all the G.I.'s coming home from the war."
Richard Wright, a Chicago dealer, said, "Americans are finally realizing Eames was one of the most important designers of the 20th century." He added, "It's sad that European museums were ahead of us in collecting Eames furniture." He was referring to the Vitra Design Museum in Germany, which has a large Eames collection and is the co-sponsor of the traveling exhibition.
Wlodek Malowanczyk, who owns Collage 20th-Century Classics, a Dallas gallery, said: "Even Dallas is catching up. My customers won't buy the expensive things yet, but they will buy collectibles like Eames's 1945 DCM Chair, with metal legs and a plywood back and seat."
After his dismissal from Washington University in St. Louis, involving his advocacy of the ideas of Frank Lloyd Wright, Eames (1907-78) traveled to Europe and designed a church in Arkansas that caught the eye of the Finnish architect Eliel Saarinen, then president of the Cranbrook Academy in Michigan. He offered Eames a fellowship at the academy. At Cranbrook, Eames met other groundbreaking modernists like the artist Harry Bertoia and fell in love with Ray Kaiser, an art student from California who would become his second wife.
In 1939 Eames went to work in Saarinen's architectural office. The following year, while Eames was working as a resident instructor at Cranbrook with Saarinen's son, Eero, the two collaborated on a prototype of a modern chair that won first prize at the Museum of Modern Art's Organic Design in Home Furnishings competition. Nicknamed the Conversation Armchair, it had a plywood shell, foam rubber cushioning, custom upholstery and four tapering angled wood dowel legs. It was the first time a molded plywood shell was shaped into three-dimensional curves. And it made a lot of news.
In 1941 Eames married Kaiser, and they moved to Los Angeles "to try to mass produce molded plywood with complex curves," said Eames Demetrios, Eames's grandson.
He has created a Web site, www.eamesoffice.com, and opened the Eames Office Gallery in Santa Monica, Calif., to educate people about the Eames philosophy. "It's not about chairs," he said. "It's a way of looking at the world."
During World War II the United States Navy commissioned Eames to develop plywood stretchers and leg splints for wounded American soldiers. After the war the Eameses adapted their experience to the design of furniture. In 1948 they designed the Eiffel Tower Chair, which had a fiberglass shell with metal rod leg supports. It won the second prize at the Museum of Modern Art's 1948 International Competition for Low-Cost Furniture Design. The Eameses went on to create chairs, storage units, tables and folding screens, most of which were manufactured by Herman Miller in Michigan.
Over the next two months work by Eames is expected to show up at several auctions and at a fair devoted to 20th-century decorative arts, beginning tomorrow with David Rago's 20th-century modern auction in Lambertville, N.J. (Information: 609-397-9374.)
Meanwhile, dealers from as far away as Italy are expected to participate in the Los Angeles Modernism Show at the Santa Monica Civic Auditorium on May 8 and 9.
"L.A. is driving the market right now," said John Meany, a San Francisco dealer who will be there. "Since the movie colony embraced modernism and started buying and restoring modern houses, mid-century modern has gotten very trendy. Five or six years ago it was Tokyo that was driving the market."
Los Angeles Modern Auctions, or LAMA, which specializes in Eames, will also be at the Los Angeles show, along with Mr. Malowanczyk. In addition, LAMA is conducting its own auction on May 16 in Beverly Hills. Highlights include the Eames Conversation Armchair and three rare pieces that the architect R. M. Schindler designed in 1934 for the Van Patten residence in Los Angeles.
On May 23 the John Toomey Gallery in Oak Park, Ill., in conjunction with Mr. Wright, the Chicago dealer, and the Treadway Gallery of Cincinnati is having four separate 20th-century sales in Oak Park. For one, Mr. Wright assembled 150 Eames pieces for an all-Eames sale.
Sotheby's 20th-century decorative arts sale in Manhattan is on June 10; Christie's is on June 11, at its new home in Rockefeller Center, another icon of modernism.
Mr. McDonald of the Gansevoort normally sells to American architects and designers like Lee Mindel, Michael Formica and Thad Hayes, but on Sunday he was in the Netherlands at the first Amsterdam Arts and Design Fair, which focused on design after 1880. He was one of three American dealers selling American furniture. Peter Loughery of LAMA sold some Eames leg splints at the fair.
"The icons go for very high prices," he added. "In Europe many can't afford to spend thousands on a side chair."
But the number of collectors is increasing everywhere.
"There was a time when I recognized everyone who bid at my sales in L.A.," Mr. Loughery said, "Now it's maybe 20 percent."
The Lambertville auction, also organized by John Sollo, will offer several pieces by the craftsman George Nakashima, including an end table with a beautifully figured Buckeye burlwood top, a rare Makassar ebony console table with a trestle base, and a spectacular walnut music stand.
The market for mid-century modern pieces today "is a lot like the stock market," Mr. McDonald said. "The business runs the way the economy runs. People have money to spend, and they are optimistic about the economy. Good things come out because people think they will get good prices for them."

SECTION: Section E; Part 1; Page 22; Column 5; Movies, Performing Arts/Weekend Desk

LENGTH: 2248 words

HEADLINE: MOVIE GUIDE

 BODY:

Here is a selective listing by critics of The Times of new or noteworthy movies and film series playing this weekend in New York City. * denotes a highly recommended film or series. Ratings and running times are in parentheses. An index of reviews of films opening today appears on page 11.
 
Now Playing
 
 * "ANALYZE THIS," starring Robert De Niro and Billy Crystal. Directed by Harold Ramis (R, 110 minutes). The title, concept and teaming of these co-stars are all funny before the lights go down, so think of this comedy as an offer you can't refuse. Cast as a mobster whose confidence is shaken, Mr. De Niro is hilarious simply by playing his role halfway straight, in classic goodfella style. His mobster finds just the right comic foil in Billy Crystal, as the psychiatrist who utters the immortal, "When I got into family therapy, this wasn't the family I had in mind." There's a lot more where that came from in this breezy comic treat (Janet Maslin). 

* "CENTRAL STATION," starring Fernanda Montenegro. Directed by Walter Salles (R, 115 minutes). In this quiet, luminous Brazilian gem, a joyless older woman crosses paths with an orphaned boy. There's plenty of room for sentimentality here, but the wonder of Mr. Salles's film is all in the telling. As these characters develop a fractious bond, then embark on a road trip that takes them vividly to Brazil's hinterlands in search of the boy's father, Mr. Salles brings great tenderness and surprise to the events that punctuate their journey. Ms. Montenegro's tough bravura performance was one of last year's very best (Maslin).
 
 * "COOKIE'S FORTUNE," starring Charles S. Dutton, Glenn Close, Patricia Neal and Julianne Moore. Directed by Robert Altman (PG-13, 118 minutes). What a treat. The sweet assurance and guerrilla wit of Mr. Altman's vintage ensemble films make a copacetic return with a Southern small-town black comedy. Seamlessly made and completely enveloping, it tells of the machinations set off when a wealthy old woman (Ms. Neal) outrages her diabolically genteel niece (Ms. Close) by committing suicide. As the old woman's dearest friend and the niece's unwitting nemesis, Mr. Dutton is rock solid and appealing. The entire cast works on a single wavelength as Mr. Altman makes the most of a well-woven screenplay by Anne Rapp (Maslin).
 
 * "THE DREAMLIFE OF ANGELS," starring Elodie Bouchez and Natacha Regnier. Directed by Erick Zonca (R, 113 minutes; in French with English subtitles). In this very special French film about two freewheeling young women, their turbulent friendship is frayed by sexual passion and economic realities. The weaker and more head-turning of the women (Ms. Regnier) falls for the advances of a rich nightclub owner, while her fiery friend, played with unforgettable immediacy by Ms. Bouchez, rages against what she views as imminent disaster. In the process, Mr. Zonca shows astonishing acuity in observing how the women's diverging values affect every aspect of their lives. A rare and wrenching drama (Maslin).
 
 * "EDTV," starring Matthew McConaughey, Ellen DeGeneres, Woody Harrelson, Jenna Elfman and Rob Reiner. Directed by Ron Howard (PG-13, 120 minutes). In this good-humored, deceptively easygoing satire, Mr. Howard holds up a fun-house mirror to a world ruled by voyeurism and specious fame. The subject matter is familiar by now, but this film about a Trumanesque nobody as 24-hour-a-day television star has a shrewd way of drawing the audience into its web. Permeated by all the crassness and absurdity inherent in celebrity-mad culture, and by our collective obsession with snoopy trivia, it features a fine ensemble cast and a dependably jokey screenplay by Lowell Ganz and Babaloo Mandel. It helps that most of the principals, in a cast that also includes Elizabeth Hurley, have played this game for real and act their roles with a comic vengeance (Maslin).
 
 * "ELECTION," starring Matthew Broderick and Reese Witherspoon. Directed by Alexander Payne (R, 105 minutes). From the acerbically funny director of "Citizen Ruth" comes this caustic satire of the American electoral process in a high school setting. Its leading candidate, Tracy Flick, played tartly by Ms. Witherspoon, has a mother who sought tactical advice for Tracy from Connie Chung and Elizabeth Dole. Mr. Broderick plays the hapless good-guy teacher who thinks this wicked little front-runner needs to be stopped. And hunky newcomer Chris Klein plays Tracy's reluctant opponent in a style best described as early Keanu. With a lovely ear for hypocritical conversation, the screenplay specializes in keeping all the characters a few beats off of what they actually mean (Maslin).
 
 "EXISTENZ," starring Jennifer Jason Leigh and Jude Law. Directed by David Cronenberg (R, 90 minutes). Not for Mr. Cronenberg the sleek futuristic look of other films that play virtual reality games. This one has the insinuatingly ghoulish hallmarks of other Cronenberg fever dreams (like "The Fly," "Crash" and "Naked Lunch"). And it uses them to weirdly enveloping effect as a renowned game designer (Ms. Leigh) and her wary companion (Mr. Law) try to play the most dangerous game she has yet created. This sci-fi scheme is disturbingly effective as it plays its own mind games with the viewer, complete with a sharp final twist. The title is pronounced "eggs-is-tense" (Maslin).
 
"GO," starring Sarah Polley, Katie Holmes, Timothy Olyphant, Taye Diggs, Scott Wolf and Jay Mohr. Directed by Doug Liman (R, 100 minutes). This three-episode demimondaine comedy borrows truckloads of inspiration from "Pulp Fiction" but still has some flash and personality of its own. Mr. Liman, who shot the film as well as directed it, uses a supermarket as ground zero and sends a sullen cashier (Ms. Polley) out to make a drug deal. Then he embellishes this situation fully enough to send his film down the rabbit hole into neo-Tarantino adventures, some of which (guys go to Las Vegas, then get into trouble with strippers, gunplay, car theft and a brake-screeching chase) are a lot more tired than others. The ensemble cast shows cool comic flair (Maslin).
 
 * "HIDEOUS KINKY," starring Kate Winslet. Directed by Gillies MacKinnon (R, 99 minutes). Ms. Winslet knew best when she took on this offbeat, richly atmospheric adventure in the wake of "Titanic." The leading role in Mr. MacKinnon's vividly colorful film offers her a field day. She plays a young hippie mother who brings her reluctant English daughters to Marrakesh, circa 1972. The film, about the mother's willfulness, the girls' reaction and the sheer headiness of their surroundings, belongs to the striking sub-genre in which it is baby-boom adults, not their children, who most need to come of age (Maslin).
 
 "LIFE," starring Eddie Murphy and Martin Lawrence. Directed by Ted Demme (R, 105 minutes). Though it's hard to miss the basic unfunniness of seeing two black men sentenced to life at a Mississippi State Prison, this mild-mannered comedy is more likable than it sounds. If it awkwardly straddles the line between period piece (it begins during Prohibition) and comedy, it also keeps the stars kidding their way from youth to curmudgeonhood. Mr. Murphy in particular gives a more substantial performance than might be expected here. Stick around for closing out-takes that show (for instance) a cell phone ringing during a scene set in 1932 (Maslin).
 
 * "LOCK, STOCK AND TWO SMOKING BARRELS," starring Jason Flemyng, Dexter Fletcher, Vinnie Jones and Nick Moran. Directed by Guy Ritchie (R, 106 minutes). Not since "The Usual Suspects" has there been such an oversupply of cool macho schemers in a single film. And they're served up with a quick, tart economy that bespeaks the filmmaker's background making videos and commercials, actually an advantage for a change. There's not much beneath the surface here; what's most entertaining is Mr. Ritchie's droll, hard-boiled storytelling and the rogue's gallery of a cast that he has assembled. All the film need do is juggle assorted miscreants and four separate gangs who, figuratively speaking, can't shoot straight (Maslin).
 
 "THE MATRIX," starring Keanu Reeves and Laurence Fishburne. Directed by Andy and Larry Wachowski (R, 120 minutes). The time-traveling story steeped in virtual reality will be all but incomprehensible to nontechnofreaks. And the film borrows unapologetically from other sci-fi extravaganzas, with a big debt to "The X-Files" in the paranoia department. But the storytelling is intricately bizarre and the special effects are indeed special, notably when it comes to midair martial arts with a gravity-defying gusto. And Mr. Reeves shows that you can look like a Prada model while behaving like an action hero. One of the main stars here is Bill Pope, an inventive cinematographer. He and the Wachowskis provide a couch-potato epiphany: while the characters seem to be performing marvelous feats, they're often lying stock-still in the grip of virtual reality (Maslin).
 
 "PUSHING TIN," starring John Cusack, Billy Bob Thornton, Angelina Jolie and Cate Blanchett. Directed by Mike Newell (R, 115 minutes). This story of air-traffic controllers betrays its origins as a magazine article, since it has more interesting data than built-in drama. But with a cast like this, and with the director of "Four Weddings and a Funeral" at the helm, the tacked-on soapy plot here has plenty of sparks. Mr. Cusack and Mr. Thornton, both tersely good as professional rivals, become even more competitive once Ms. Jolie sashays into view as the latter's show-stopping biker-chick wife. Whatever possessed Ms. Blanchett to play Mr. Cusack's wife, a heavily accented Long Island housewife who talks about casseroles in the oven, she brings off the role with good humor and unexpected grace (Maslin).
 
 * "SHAKESPEARE IN LOVE" (the Oscar winner for best film, best original screenplay, best art direction, best costumes and best comedy score). Starring Joseph Fiennes, Gwyneth Paltrow (the best-actress winner), Geoffrey Rush, Ben Affleck and Judi Dench (best supporting actress). Directed by John Madden (R, 113 minutes). A witty, sexy and merrily literate delight, with an exhilaratingly clever premise that only gets better as the film unfolds. The screenplay, originating as Marc Norman's brainstorm and turned by Tom Stoppard into razor-sharp dialogue reminiscent of his "Rosencrantz and Guildenstern Are Dead," dares to imagine whatever it likes about the link between Shakespeare's artistic passions and his mad yearning for a certain aristocratic beauty. Meanwhile, this tirelessly inventive comedy envisions an Elizabethan theater fraught with the same backbiting and conniving we enjoy today and has great fun presenting the creation of "Romeo and Juliet," glitches and all. Mr. Fiennes and Ms. Paltrow make a fine, tempestuous duo, and she gives the first great, fully realized starring performance of her career (Maslin).
 
 "SLC PUNK!," starring Matthew Lillard and Michael Goorjian. Directed by James Merendino (R, 97 minutes). Maybe only real experience as an alienated teen-ager in Salt Lake City in 1985 could have led a filmmaker to such a (perhaps deservedly) overlooked time and place. But Mr. Merendino supplies enough absurdist humor to make sense of the setting. His film follows Stevo (Mr. Lillard), the kind of anarchist who likes making rules, and whose rebel streak is about as authentic as his blue hair. As the rare punk who may be headed for Harvard Law School without even trying, Stevo is glib but entertaining. For all the film's appealing outlandishness, it's finally as mainstream as its hero proves to be (Maslin).
 
 * "A WALK ON THE MOON," starring Diane Lane, Viggo Mortensen, Liev Schreiber, Tovah Feldshuh and Anna Paquin. Directed by Tony Goldwyn (R, 107 minutes). This evocative 1969 period piece, set on the brink of Woodstock in a Catskills holiday camp, is as seductive as the handsome, itinerant hippie who turns its heroine's life upside down. Thoughtfully conceived and movingly acted, it sweeps a housewife and mother named Pearl Kantrowitz (Ms. Lane) into a torrid extramarital romance and then weighs the consequences. With a tenderness that's unusual for a contemporary American film, it tells an enveloping tale enhanced by artfully selected Woodstock-era music. Mr. Mortensen may strain credulity as the foxiest blouse salesman in the Catskills, but he certainly does wonders for the movie (Maslin).
 
Film Series
 
 "TOUT TRUFFAUT." Cinematic treats in profusion await visitors to Film Forum, where a nine-week, 21-film series devoted to Francois Truffaut (1932-1984) is in progress. This major retrospective offers a double bill this weekend that mingles the comic and the melancholy. Through Monday, the attraction is a double bill of "Shoot the Piano Player" (1960) and "The Story of Adele H." (1975). In the former, Truffaut's follow-up to "The 400 Blows" and an often satirical homage to Hollywood's gangster films, Charles Aznavour stars as a burned-out pianist who becomes entangled with the underworld. In the latter, Isabelle Adjani delivers a wrenching portrayal of the daughter of Victor Hugo and her obsessive love for an English officer. The series continues until June 24 at Film Forum, 209 West Houston Street, South Village. Admission is $8.50 per program; $4.50 for members. Information: (212) 727-8110 (Lawrence Van Gelder).

SECTION: Section E; Part 1; Page 11; Column 1; Movies, Performing Arts/Weekend Desk 

LENGTH: 1367 words

HEADLINE: AT THE MOVIES;
'Limbo' Means What It Says

BYLINE: By James Sterngold 

DATELINE: HOLLYWOOD, April 29

 BODY:
It is a season of certainty in Hollywood, at least as far as story lines in popular movies are concerned. There is often a very pronounced difference between the good guys and the bad guys, and movies tend to end on a note of clarity. There is a winner and a loser, a resolution. It is no accident that many movies now are based on comic books, which tend to have a clear conception of good and right. 
 John Sayles (above) attacks that approach in his latest movie, "Limbo." In fact, few films have ever been as ambiguous as "Limbo," Mr. Sayles's 12th movie, which will play at the Cannes Film Festival in May and is to open in theaters in the United States on June 4. Suffice it to say that the word "cliffhanger" hardly captures the uncertain note on which the film abruptly ends, on a lonely, wooded island off the Alaskan coast.
"That's why it's called 'Limbo,' " said Mr. Sayles, who wrote, directed and edited the picture. "One of the things about an ending to a movie is it should send you back to the movie itself, and it should make sense. This movie is about risks, emotional and physical, and so I want it to end focused on that, not what happens to them ultimately. People will fill in the end for themselves."
The movie is about a group of good-hearted misfits in Juneau, Alaska, who slowly work their way into each other's lives. David Strathairn plays a fisherman who no longer fishes because of an accident years earlier in which two of his friends were killed; Mary Elizabeth Mastrantino plays a lounge singer who stumbles into one bad relationship after another before falling for him, and Vanessa Martinez plays her daughter, who is afraid of drawing close to anyone because of her mother's tempestuous love life.
Little by little, they begin to pull together when they find themselves at a physical risk that mirrors the emotional risks they are taking in trying to build a family.
Mr. Sayles said the movie's tone was a throwback to the 1960's and 70's, when ambiguity was more usual. "I don't think it's as common now to end a movie on a question as it was in the 60's and 70's," he said. "Those were the days of the counter-culture and challenging authority. Heroes were flawed."
At the least, Mr. Sayles added, films with open endings make audiences think. "What you're hoping for is you don't want someone to consume your movie like a fast-food meal and then forget about it," he said. "You don't want them to walk away thinking about other movies, but about their own lives. This may do that."
 
 Creating Effects
 George Lucas's "Star Wars: Episode I -- The Phantom Menace" is expected to be a major hit when it is released in May in a business sorely hurting for blockbuster-size profits. But the film also marks a coming of age for computerized special effects.
When Mr. Lucas produced his first three "Star Wars" pictures, he all but invented the modern special-effects industry. He made innovative uses of computers and models of space ships, aliens and the like to create realistic images of faraway worlds and far-out characters. He eventually organized this expertise into a company, Industrial Light and Magic, which was a pioneer in the special-effects world and which rode the crest of Hollywood's huge special-effects boom.
Although "The Phantom Menace" is perhaps the most sophisticated and one of the most costly special-effects pictures ever made, it comes as Hollywood's ardor for the computer has cooled, said Jim Morris (above), president of Lucas Digital, the parent company of Industrial Light and Magic. He said this was partly because of the costs, and partly because audiences were no longer thrilled by what had become run-of-the-mill effects, like brilliant explosions. As a result, the industry has been in something of a recession.
"There's been a real cooling at the studios in greenlighting these pictures, and some of the infrastructure built up is collapsing under its own weight because there's not enough work going around," Mr. Morris said. He said his company had been kept on an even keel because of its work on "The Phantom Menace," as well as "The Mummy," "Wild, Wild West" and a large number of television commercials.
Mr. Morris said roughly half the new "Star Wars" movie's $120 million budget was used to produce special effects, an unusually high figure. He added that the picture had more computerized effects than almost any previous live-action film, with about 2,000 special-effects shots, compared with 200 or so in a regular special-effects movie.
In making "The Phantom Menace," Industrial Light and Magic geared up considerably, from about 650 employees a couple of years ago to about 1,000 now. In addition, it had to make huge investments in computers and software.
Mr. Morris said that when the original "Star Wars" pictures were made, about 90 percent of the costs of creating special effects was labor, and 10 percent computers. Today, he said, the ratio is about half and half because of the growing expense of computer stations and software.
Mr. Morris also said that in the old days, the Industrial Light crews often had to put in grinding hours and seven-day weeks to complete their work Now, he said, the company has learned to pace itself better.
"They're doing their best work ever because they're not coming in in a haze of exhaustion," Mr. Morris said. "We avoided the last-minute crunch." And so now, he said, several weeks before the movie opens, many of the staff members have already left for well-deserved vacations.
 
 A Hip 'Tarzan'
"Tarzan" is one of the most-filmed subjects in Hollywood history, with Disney estimating there have been 47 adaptations of the Edgar Rice Burroughs stories, second only to the number of adaptations of "Dracula." But it is a safe bet this famed ape man has never before been a skateboarder.
The Walt Disney Company's big animated feature film this summer will be "Tarzan," and as in its previous efforts, the company is both trying to bring to life a classic story known to many and to make it new, so it will be the foundation for everything from merchandise sales to theme park rides.
The resulting movie is long on outright adventure, with the hero, Tarzan (above), adept at shooting through the jungle because he slides along trees as if on a skateboard. The directors, Kevin Lima and Chris Buck, both veterans of several Disney animated movies, used a new computer technique, called Deep Canvas, to enhance the sense of motion and excitement and to give depth to the jungle background. But their goal, also in keeping with the unwritten rules of this genre, was to make the movie something of a psychodrama, a record of a youth's wrestling with his identity in a world in which he does not quite fit. Indeed, one of the bigger changes from many previous Disney films is that the personal struggle at its heart is more adolescent.
The plot follows the boy who is raised by gorillas and struggles to be one of them, until he catches his first glimpse of humans. Tarzan, with Tony Goldwyn's voice, ultimately feels he has to choose between the jungle, a world he knows but in which he remains something of an outsider, and civilization, a world he does not know but to which he feels drawn, even though he remains an outsider.
"It's really the classic struggle most kids feel of wanting to be in control of their worlds," Mr. Buck said. "He's going through an early college-age kind of struggle."
And there is a Jane, with the voice of Minnie Driver. But the attraction is innocent, the directors said, not sexual. As in other Disney animated movies, there is music, provided by Phil Collins, but it is used differently. "This is the first Disney movie for a long time where the characters don't sing themselves," Mr. Lima said. "The songs are the voices of their emotions."
Glenn Close provides the voice of the gorilla who raises Tarzan; Rosie O'Donnell is his gorilla sidekick, and Lance Henriksen of "Millennium" is the voice of the gorilla patriarch. They are the friends who counsel Tarzan, helping him make his difficult decision, and making him probably the first skatehoarder best known for his emotions and thinking.

HEADLINE: FILM REVIEW;
For Vietnamese, a New Apocalypse

BYLINE: By STEPHEN HOLDEN 

 BODY:
"Three Seasons" is a wistful, rain-streaked elegy for a Vietnamese culture that is being swept away on the flood-tide of globalization. Too tender-hearted and lyrical to deliver a political indictment, this touching film, which was chosen best dramatic feature at the Sundance Film Festival in January, is a dreamy cinematic poem whose interwoven voices offer different variations of the same sad story.
In that story, the gentle, common folk of Saigon are being left behind as an emerging, hard-edged international culture symbolized by Coca-Cola signs, luxury hotels and the peddling of plastic lotus blossoms on the streets takes over the city. One of many symbols of bald commercialization is a popular hangout once frequented by American servicemen that is now vulgarly named the Apocalypse Now Bar. 
 "Three Seasons" is the first feature film by Tony Bui, a 26-year-old director who escaped from Vietnam with his parents when he was 2 years old and grew up in California. After returning to his native country for a visit when he was 19, he became fluent in Vietnamese and went back to make a short film, "Yellow Lotus." Filmed in Saigon with a cast of Vietnamese actors, "Three Seasons" portrays the Vietnamese capital as a city of dissolving pastel colors, and the diffused palette reinforces the film's romanticized vision of a worldmelting away under warm tropical rains.
All four of the film's principal characters are viewed as innocent, struggling victims of economic forces beyond their control. The opening scenes introduce us to Kien An (Nguyen Ngoc Hiep), the latest recruit in an all-female work force hired to pick white lotus blossoms on the temple estate of a reclusive boss. After gathering their crop, the women are bused to Saigon, where they sell baskets of flowers on the streets. Business is good until one day Kien An suddenly finds herself competing with vendors of plastic, artificially scented imitations of the real thing.
Summoned by her mysterious employer, who has heard her singing a work song that transports him back to his childhood, Kien An discovers an embittered poet dying of leprosy. This once handsome man whose face is decomposing is the film's most jarring symbol of a traditional culture being pushed into the shadows.
Another strand of the story follows Hai (Don Duong), a cyclo driver who falls in love with Lan (Zoe Bui), an imperious Vietnamese prostitute. Hai assiduously courts her by offering her free rides from the luxury hotels in which she plies her trade. Lan scornfully accepts his transportation until the day he reaps a windfall from winning a taxi race and purchases her professional time. Beneath her hard, high-fashion exterior, he discovers a frightened, alienated woman awash in shame and confusion.
Finally, there is Woody (Nguyen Huu Duoc), a wide-eyed street boy who sells trinkets out of a suitcase. At the Apocalypse Now Bar, the boy meets James Hager (Harvey Keitel), an American soldier who has returned to Vietnam to search for his grown-up daughter. When Hager leaves for a moment, the boy nods off and has his suitcase stolen. Woody spends most of the rest of the movie trying to retrieve the source of his meager livelihood in a drenching rainstorm.
"Three Seasons" doesn't pretend that its stories or characters (who all cross paths before the movie is over) are realistic.
Each is a symbolic thread in a larger fable of people searching for their roots. The movie is so languidly paced that it occasionally turns vague and meandering.
And its characters, especially the adorable, saucer-eyed Woody and the sweet, self-abasing Hai, are sentimentalized to the brink of saintliness. So much misty sadness and soft-focus guilt swirls around that you almost wish for a purgative eruption of anger.
But such an eruption would violate the movie's sweetly uplifting agenda. Determined not to revisit the Vietnam War and to open up old wounds, "Three Seasons" preaches reconciliation, forgiveness, reverence for tradition and the salvaging of all that was best about the past.
 
"Three Seasons is rated PG-13. (Parents strongly cautioned). It includes sexual situations.
 
THREE SEASONS
Directed by Tony Bui; written (in Vietnamese, with English subtitles) by Mr. Bui, based on a story by Mr. Bui and Timothy Linh Bui; director of photography, Lisa Rinzler; edited by Keith Reamer; music by Richard Horowitz, with Vietnamese songs by Vy Nhat Tao; production designer, Wing Lee; produced by Jason Kliot, Joana Vicente and Mr. Bui; released by October Filmscoei. Running time: 110 minutes. This film is rated PG-13.
 
WITH: Don Duong (Hai), Nguyen Ngoc Hiep (Kien An), Tran Manh Cuong (Teacher Dao), Harvey Keitel (James Hager), Zoe Bui (Lan) and Nguyen Huu Duoc (Woody).

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Brazil Pushes Ahead on Bank Inquiry

BYLINE: By SIMON ROMERO 

DATELINE: SAO PAULO, Brazil, April 29

 BODY:
Brazilian lawmakers pressed forward today with an inquiry into allegations of inside information leaks at the country's central bank.
Lawmakers called Francisco Lopes, a Harvard-educated economist and former central bank president who is at the center of the inquiry, to testify before a panel of senators for a third time. 
 Mr. Lopes is accused of engineering, perhaps illegally, a bailout of two banks caught off guard by the central bank's decision to devalue the currency in January. He is also suspected of receiving payment in an offshore account for services at a consulting firm he once owned while employed as a central bank director in 1996.
These accusations have not been substantiated. But Mr. Lopes has refused twice before to testify and was detained by the police briefly earlier this week for his defiance. It was not clear today whether he would agree to testify this time.
Even with the uncertainty over Mr. Lopes, Brazil's financial markets are beginning to shrug off the effects of the inquiry, a sign that it is losing momentum. Less preoccupied with the potential for new allegations to emerge, the country's currency, the real, strengthened today to a four-month high of 1.65 to the dollar, before weakening slightly. On Wednesday, the central bank made another in a series of interest rate cuts because of growing confidence in the economy. And in recent days Brazilian banks have successfully sold bonds abroad to foreign investors for the first time since Russia's economic meltdown last summer suppressed demand for emerging-market debt securities.
"For us it appears this investigation is nearing the end," said Carlos Castanho, the managing director of Credit Suisse First Boston Garantia, an investment bank that is one of the largest traders of Brazilian bonds.
The investigation may still hurt the credibility of President Fernando Henrique Cardoso's administration, and Pedro Malan, Mr. Cardoso's Finance Minister, may be called to testify. Mr. Malan may be criticized for his role in the affair because, in Brazil, the president of the central bank is subordinate to the finance minister. Another high-ranking official, the Budget Minister, Pedro Parente, could also be called to testify since he was deputy finance minister in mid-January when Mr. Lopes bailed out the two institutions, Banco Marka and Banco Fonte Cindam.
But it is unlikely that the scandal will spread to other members of the Government's elite bureaucracy. The current central bank president, Arminio Fraga, whose work has been praised by investors, is considered largely untouchable because at the time of the events in question he was managing a hedge fund in New York for George Soros, the multibillionaire investor.

HEADLINE: Almost a Microsoft Witness, But Definitely a Hot Potato

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR 

 BODY:
He was personally asked to be a witness in the Microsoft antitrust trial by the software giant's chairman, William H. Gates, but he was quickly dropped from the company's witness list after he was questioned by a Government lawyer last fall.
 Yet judging from his deposition testimony released this week, Michael L. Dertouzos had a lot to say -- and he would have been a most unusual witness, blunt and direct, with views not tailored to either side. 
 The 62-year-old director of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology's laboratory for computer science at first refused to be a witness for Microsoft when the company's lawyers reached him. But last July, Mr. Dertouzos said, Mr. Gates called him and he agreed to testify but only on one narrow issue: that in the interests of simplicity, the distinction between the Internet browser and the operating system would become "increasingly blurred in the future."
Coming from a leading computer scientist, this was music to the ears of the Microsoft legal team. It seemed to echo a central defense argument -- namely, that Microsoft's Windows operating system and its Internet Explorer browser are one seamlessly integrated product and not two products bundled together to stifle competition.
Yet as his deposition testimony makes clear, Mr. Dertouzos did not have a legal agenda in mind. In his books and speeches, he has advocated the need to simplify the use of computers if information technology is to truly pay off, making the economy more productive and people's lives richer.
To do that, he explained, a person ought to be able to easily retrieve information from a desktop PC or from some distant place through the Internet by using a few simple commands -- "a steering wheel, a gas pedal and a brake, so to speak."
Besides speaking his mind on only one issue, Mr. Dertouzos informed Microsoft that he would be an unusual witness in other respects as well. He would take no pay, whereas fees of more than $1,000 an hour are common for expert trial witnesses.
Most witnesses at the Microsoft trial prepared for days or weeks, reviewing documents and being coached by lawyers, but not Mr. Dertouzos. "I would like to behave and be treated as a judge's witness rather than as Microsoft's witness in the European tradition of being neutral," he said. "And I did not want to be prepared in any way by Microsoft attorneys."
He was willing to be a trial witness despite what would inevitably be questioned as a possible conflict of interest. He told the Justice Department lawyer at his deposition that nearly a year earlier, in November 1997, Mr. Gates had pledged to donate $20 million to M.I.T. to build a new computer science building.
The news of the Gates donation, which was publicly announced only a few weeks ago, would certainly have been used at the trial by the Justice Department lawyers to question Mr. Dertouzos's independence.
But the computer scientist answered any doubts about his independence as his testimony proceeded. He volunteered that he and Mr. Gates see the future of the information age differently. A key disagreement between the two men, Mr. Dertouzos said, was "my statement that the new world of information, if left to its own devices, would increase the gap between rich and poor." He added, "Mr. Gates felt it would shrink it."
When asked whether the browser was now considered part of the operating system or a separate application, Mr. Dertouzos replied, "Historically and today, it is the case that browsers are treated as applications, yes."
With that, the hopes of the Microsoft legal team fell, as did Mr. Dertouzos's value as a defense witness. Legal cases tend to be decided by facts, rooted in the here and now. No matter what Mr. Dertouzos may think about the future course of computing, he said that the browser was currently treated as a separate product, as the Justice Department contends, and not part of the operating system.
That single quotation from the Dertouzos deposition was featured in the Microsoft trial and presented as evidence by the Government.
Go a little further into his testimony, though, and Mr. Dertouzos emphasizes the difficulty of drawing clean dividing lines, given the malleability of software, rendered in the digital code of 1's and 0's. His goal of simplicity, he testified, could be accomplished in many ways -- giving the operating system the functionality of a browser, giving a browser the functionality of an operating system, or placing most of these capabilities centrally in a network, which can be tapped into with simple Internet devices.
"There are a lot of possibilities there," he said. "Software design is still an extremely primitive activity based primarily on art and craft. So depending on the designer's inspiration, mind-set and approach, one of these approaches might turn out to be best."
