HEADLINE: Initial Jobless Claims Are Down; Purchases of New Homes Climb

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
More Americans are buying new homes and claims for jobless benefits are falling, Government reports showed today.
New single-family home sales rose 2.1 percent in March, reversing a 2 percent decline in February, as sales in the West posted their biggest monthly gain in four years, Commerce Department figures showed. 
 First-time claims for state unemployment benefits fell a larger-than-expected 20,000 last week, to a seasonally adjusted 294,000 after decreasing 5,000 the previous week, the Labor Department reported. Analysts expected claims to fall to 310,000 last week.
A 24 percent rise in March new-home sales in Western states proved to be more than enough to offset declines in other regions of the country. The overall gain for the month translated into an annual rate of 909,000 units, higher than last year's record sales total of 886,000 homes.
"I've been doing this since 1972, and this is one of the best periods," said Roy Hooper, a Phoenix real estate broker and relocation director at the Cendant Corporation's Century 21 Real Estate Corporation. "You have a lot of new people coming in, and they want new homes."
While most analysts do not see housing setting another record this year, builders say they are confident business will improve in the months ahead.
The inventory of new homes for sale at the current sales pace rose to a 4.1-month supply in March from a 4-month supply in February, the Government report also showed. And the median price of new homes fell 1.5 percent in March to $153,000 from $155,400 in February.

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Japan's Nutcracker Suite

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:
For the past decade whenever anyone told me Japan was finally opening its economy and lowering its trade barriers, I always thought of that story of the prisoners who were in jail together for so many years that they didn't need to actually tell each other their jokes anymore. They would just say "Joke number 3," and everyone would laugh. In my case, the simple phrase "Japan is really opening up its economy this time" always had a similar laugh-inducing effect.
Well, laugh no more. After visiting Tokyo a few weeks ago, I left convinced that Japan has indeed begun its third great modernization -- something Prime Minister Keizo Obuchi will be touting on his visit to the U.S. beginning today. The first modern opening of Japan began when Commodore Perry broke down the doors and arrived in 1853, triggering the Meiji restoration. The second was Douglas MacArthur's imposition of democracy on Japan after World War II, and the third is being driven now by the invisible hands of globalization: the Internet and global capital flows are breaking the shell around Japan like a nutcracker. 
 Let's be honest: For the past 50 years Japan was just the most successful Communist country in history. It was only a matter of time before the forces that brought down the Berlin wall and the Soviet Union felled the wall around Japan as well.
It's hard to talk to anyone in Tokyo these days without hearing the "Nutcracker" suite in the background. A friend at the Japanese Foreign Ministry began a conversation like this: "My son informed us the other day that he wants to be a fund manager, but he doesn't want to work for a Japanese bank. He wants to be with an international bank. The Japanese institutions that young people of my generation would automatically like to join, like the major banks, are crumbling. That is new. My wife couldn't understand my son. So he said to her, "If you want to understand what I want to do, watch this film." He gave her a video of the movie "Other People's Money," about a New York financier who takes over a dying company in New England. . . . My wife is still worried."
Another Japanese friend who works in executive recruiting informed me that she always had a hard time recruiting Japanese to work for U.S. companies because these companies, unlike those in Japan, practiced "the three K's -- Kitsui, the Japanese word for 'very demanding'; Kaiko, Japanese for 'firing people' when they did not perform, and Kyoso, Japanese for 'competition.' " This friend now tells her clients: "Even if you think you are signing up for life with a Japanese company today, you could wake up in the morning and find yourself working for a foreign-owned company very soon, so you might as well join one now and learn to adapt to it."
So I've got good news and bad news and good news. The good news is that Japan is really changing. The bad news is that the Japanese Government is still using its savings to prop up inefficient companies. "The good news, though," said a foreign banker in Tokyo, "is that the state is now running out of money, and that guarantees that the free market is going to increasingly decide where capital and labor should go, and that will bring about the new Japan. But it will take 10 years."
Indeed, many Japanese companies, strapped for cash, are now seeking foreign partners, and those partners are bringing foreign business practices. The French auto maker Renault just came in and took a chunk of Nissan, while GE Capital, AIG, Merrill Lynch and Goldman, Sachs are all buying up cash-starved Japanese businesses and restructuring them.
While the transformation taking place in Japan today is going to make it a formidable competitor over time, it will be a wrenching transition. Sure, Japan's economy was a rigged game in many ways, but it provided enormous social stability and cohesion. There is much to admire in Japanese society, and it is not clear what is going to go and what is going to stay in this transition.
Stephanie Strom, a Tokyo reporter for The Times, wrote a moving article the other day about a Japanese manufacturer who stabbed his brother -- who was also his business partner -- to death after their once-high-flying welding company went bust. It went bust after the walls around Japan started to fall and the company had to deal with more open, global competition. Ironically, their bankrupt company had specialized in making metal doors. 
http://www.nytimes.com

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Potential Mayor of Baltimore Has City Guessing

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: BALTIMORE, April 27

 BODY:
State lawmakers shortened a city residency requirement to allow him to run. Prominent civic, business and church leaders formed a draft committee to encourage him to run. Three former mayors have implored him to run.
Supporters of Kweisi Mfume are so convinced he is the only choice to succeed Kurt L. Schmoke, who is stepping down after three terms as Mayor of Baltimore, that they predict dire consequences for the city if anyone else from what they view as a lackluster field prevails in the November election. 
 But as much as they want a candidate of Mr. Mfume's stature -- a five-term Democratic Congressman from west Baltimore who left the House in 1995 to become president and chief executive of the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People -- they can only cross their fingers and hold their breath.
As the city approaches its first mayoral election campaign without an incumbent in 28 years, Mr. Mfume says he has not decided whether to run for mayor or remain at the N.A.A.C.P., which is based here. The uncertainty has kept the city's political forces on edge, would-be candidates on the sidelines and officials of the civil rights organization in the dark. Even Julian Bond, the association's chairman, says he has no idea what Mr. Mfume will do.
"Everybody wants this on their timetable," Mr. Mfume said in an interview on Monday, during which he expressed ambivalence over a decision that he is promising by mid-May. "But at the end of the day, it's my call, it's what I'm comfortable with. In my heart of hearts, I have to choose the best thing I can do to help the greatest number of people."
"You know," he added, "I go back and forth between my head and heart, back and forth, day and night. What I ought to do. What I should do. People think I have made up my mind about this. That's not the case at all."
Baltimore is one of those places that have always strained to maintain big-league stature among American cities. Mr. Schmoke, a former Rhodes scholar and assistant United States attorney, has helped. So has a vast urban redevelopment effort over the last 20 years, creating a vibrant downtown, stabilizing neighborhoods and stemming a slow but steady migration to the suburbs.
Chronic problems remain, though, including a large number of homicides (about 300 a year), underachieving public schools and an abundance of crumbling low-income housing.
Mr. Schmoke, a Democrat in an overwhelmingly Democratic city, has endured much of the recent criticism for the problems' persistence. The complaints only heightened interest in Mr. Mfume as a problem solver once the Mayor announced in December that he would not seek a fourth term.
While other Democrats, including Mr. Mfume's second cousin, Lawrence A. Bell 3d, the City Council president, say they are running for mayor no matter what he decides, none can match Mr. Mfume's credentials. A former member of the City Council, Mr. Mfume, now 50, went on to gain national prominence as a Congressman and chairman of the Congressional Black Caucus before being chosen to lead the nation's oldest civil rights organization.
At the outset, Mr. Mfume played down any interest in becoming mayor. He had two years remaining on a five-year contract at the N.A.A.C.P., where he had inherited an organization facing discord, debt and a struggle to make a case for its own relevance. He has since restored financial stability, brought in new leaders and expanded membership, especially among those of college age. But he contends that the association needs more time to re-establish its once-weighty role in the nation's social conscience.
"That's the dichotomy I face," he said. "I don't feel that what I've started here is finished. The foundation is stable, but now it's up to the association to put up the superstructure."
Only recently has Mr. Mfume begun sounding more like a prospective candidate. As members of the City Council discussed increasing the mayor's salary of $95,000 as an added inducement -- Mr. Mfume earns about $220,000 with the N.A.A.C.P. -- he said in a speech last week that if anything ultimately persuaded him to run, it would be his love for the city and public service, not money.
"I don't need to be enticed to be mayor of this city," he told his audience, in what was regarded as the strongest indication yet that he was giving serious consideration to a run.
The speech came shortly after Gov. Parris N. Glendening signed a measure that reduces the city's residency requirement for political candidates to six months prior to election, from one year. The state initiative allows the Baltimore City Council to restore the one-year requirement, and on Monday night Councilman Norman A. Handy, with strong support from Mr. Bell, introduced a measure that would do so. But that bill is not expected to gather enough votes.
Although Mr. Mfume moved last month from a suburban home into the city, he said in the interview that his change of residence had nothing to do with the new law or the mayoral race. And to those trying to read him for signals, he urged caution for now, saying, "I have been trying to run away from this for four months in a dignified, respectful fashion."
"And I appreciate people expressing support," he added. "But I am not a messiah."
With Mr. Mfume on the sidelines, the campaign leading to the September primary sputters on. Mr. Bell is regarded by many as the front-runner in an Mfume-less Democratic field, whose winner is all but assured of victory in the general election.
While Mr. Bell has announced plans to run, he has not yet filed with the city's election board, as candidates must by July 6. Five other Democrats have filed, including former Councilman Carl Stokes. But none of the campaigns have taken off, largely because Mr. Mfume's decision will have such a strong bearing on the field.
Mr. Schmoke, among others, predicted in an interview that if Mr. Mfume decided to stay out, other candidates might emerge, perhaps even William Donald Schaefer, who served a record four terms as Mayor before serving two as Governor and, last year, winning a four-year term as State Comptroller at the age of 77. Mr. Schaefer, who has been joined by former Mayors Clarence H. Burns and Thomas D'Alesandro 3d in urging Mr. Mfume to run, has offered no public sign that he is seriously interested in returning to City Hall.
As for Mr. Mfume, he said he began to consider the race in earnest last Friday, sounding out his five sons, ages 25 to 30. In a perfect reflection of his own ambivalence, two urged caution, he said, while two encouraged him to run and one expressed uncertainty.
In the weeks ahead, Mr. Mfume said, he intends to "pray on the issue" and discuss it further with his family before meeting with the N.A.A.C.P. board next month in Miami.
After that, he said, he will announce his decision.
Mr. Bond, the association's chairman, who lives in Washington, says he has been following developments only through published reports. Those developments include a draft movement that he called "certainly impressive." More than 250 city leaders, including 11 City Council members and a handful of state lawmakers, endorsed Mr. Mfume's candidacy in full-page newspaper advertisements last week.
"We would hate to lose him," said Mr. Bond, who expects to meet privately with Mr. Mfume next week. "But the institution is bigger than any individual chairman or C.E.O. It would take a search, but we would find a replacement."

HEADLINE: Two Convicted In Spying Case

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: YOUNGSTOWN, Ohio, April 29

 BODY:
Two Taiwan executives who paid an employee of the Avery Dennison Corporation to pilfer corporate secrets from the United States adhesives maker were convicted of economic espionage on Wednesday in the first case tried under a United States law designed to combat trade-secret thefts.
Jurors in Federal court in Youngstown deliberated for about 18 hours over three days before finding P. Y. Yang, chief executive of the Taiwan-based Four Pillars Ltd., and his daughter, Hwei Chang Yang, guilty of economic espionage. 
 The pair were acquitted of mail fraud charges. Four Pillars itself was also convicted on the espionage charges. The Yangs were accused of paying Ten Hong Lee, a senior research engineer at Avery Dennison, $160,000 over eight years to obtain adhesive formulas and innovations developed by the company, which is based in Pasadena, Calif. Four Pillars and Avery Dennison compete in the Asian adhesive-label market.
The Yangs each face a maximum penalty of 10 years in prison and $250,000 in fines for violating the espionage law. Four Pillars could be fined up to $5 million. United States District Judge Peter Economus has not set a date for the Yangs' sentencing. He allowed the Yangs to remain free on bond. Avery Dennison's shares rose $3.0625 today, to $67.125, on the Big Board.

HEADLINE: Greenspan's Advice for Emerging Nations

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
The chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, urged central banks in developing nations today to manage their foreign exchange reserves more efficiently to help prevent financial crises.
In a speech at the World Bank, Mr. Greenspan suggested central banks in emerging economies would be well advised to follow a basic rule of thumb: hold a sufficient amount of reserves to survive for at least one year without additional borrowing. 
 "The Asian financial crises have reinforced the basic lesson that emerging-market economies should pay particular attention to how they manage their foreign exchange reserves," Mr. Greenspan said.
He also urged developing nations to beef up their disclosure policies and provide financial markets with more detailed information on their reserve positions.
Mr. Greenspan recalled the examples of South Korea and Thailand, where the Asian financial crisis started in 1997. The lack of detailed information on their reserves had "contributed to misperceptions by market participants of resources available to the authorities" to defend their exchange rates and, thus, encouraged speculative attacks against their currencies, he said.

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
Jockeys Sign For a Dollar And a Dream

BYLINE: By HARVEY ARATON 

DATELINE: LOUISVILLE, Ky.

 BODY:
THE rain left town and the sun came out and it finally felt like spring, like Kentucky Derby week at Churchill Downs. But for a couple of hours yesterday, the race became secondary for about half the jockeys in the 20-horse field. The potential winnings, from tomorrow's 125th running of the most famous horse race there is, mattered less than a few jars stuffed with one dollar bills.
The jars sat on tables set up under the grandstand, out by the paddock, affixed with yellow stickers asking autograph seekers for a suggested donation of $1. At a time when people attach gross inflationary value to almost anything with an athlete's signature scribbled on it, this was a bargain in the name of benevolence. 
 The cash would go straight into the Disabled Jockeys Fund, a collection that doesn't require much explanation, only a heart. The Jockeys Guild stages a number of promotional events around the country to help stock the fund, which was begun in the early 1990's and supports those who have lost livelihoods and limbs in the matter of a horse's misstep.
"There are about 2,700 jockeys registered nationwide, and there were 2,500 reported injuries last year," said John Giovanni, the Guild's national manager and secretary. "Most of the jockeys know it's not a matter of if, it is a matter of when."
Then the only question is how bad it will be.
"You want to complain when you fall off your horse and you wind up losing the hearing in your right ear, like I did, but who do you complain to when you see people who never got up again to walk?" said Kent Desormeaux, last year's Derby and Preakness winner aboard Real Quiet, who tomorrow will ride the touted filly Excellent Meeting for the trainer Bob Baffert, unless she runs today in the Kentucky Oaks.
Desormeaux's time came in December 1992, riding Judge Hammer at Hollywood Park. He fell in the lead, which made him an obstacle to the field. The last horse to pass kicked him in the head. He awoke 30 hours later, would get to see the son his wife was soon to give birth to and would reclaim his career and come within a nose of a Triple Crown in the Belmont Stakes last June.
The 51 disabled jockeys currently under the care of the fund weren't as lucky. They lost their careers, their ability to walk. A jockey named Linda Hughes was recently confined to a wheelchair after a fall at Calder Race Course in Florida. She has a daughter to raise, a $100,000 on-track insurance policy for her medical costs and a weekly $200 disability check. The money goes fast, and the unforeseen needs pile up, and that is when a hand reaches into the fund and then out to help.
Stephanie Young, a disabled jockey who lives in Louisville, had a baby recently and bought a van, but couldn't afford the $18,000 it cost to make it disabled-ready. The money came out of the Disabled Jockeys Fund, as did a new furnace this past winter for another former jockey in the middle of an Idaho blizzard. "Part of it is basic life needs," Giovanni said. " But we don't just want our disabled jockeys to survive. We want them to think: 'I can get out. I can travel.' "
I remember going to a pre-race chapel once at the Daytona 500, looking around at all the famous Nascar drivers, with their wives next to them, kids on their laps, and thinking that only a strong sense of community, of trust, could make bearable the occasional late-night dread. Professionalism does not allow race car drivers or jockeys to dwell on danger, but it is always there. The Disabled Jockeys Fund, Desormeaux said, is as much an insurance policy as it is philanthropy.
After winning his first Derby in 1993, aboard Sea Hero, Jerry Bailey stopped a sponsor who was awarding him a brand-new car in mid-sentence to say he was donating it to the Disabled Jockeys Fund. Bailey, Desormeaux, Pat Day, Shane Sellers -- the most recognizable riding names in the sport -- signed yesterday for cash, not for profit. A dollar wasn't much to ask, especially in the context of what contemporary sports owners and other athletes too often demand.
Some fans dropped a dollar or two in the jars. Many more did not. "People are people," Desormeaux said. "Some care, some don't."
After last year's close Triple Crown call, Desormeaux was kept at his post long after the others had gone. He signed every last program and card, until there were no more. His jar was three-quarters full when it was removed, its contents dumped into a large bag. Richer for his effort, Desormeaux walked off, in the midafternoon sun.

HEADLINE: Residential Real Estate;
After 7 Years, East Side Condominium Is Selling Quickly

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
The Gotham, a 239-apartment building at 170 East 87th Street, at Third Avenue, was designed as a condominium. But its opening in 1992 was buffeted by the ups and downs of Manhattan's housing market, and for the last seven years it has been used mostly as a rental building.
Now a real estate partnership headed by the sons of one of the original developers has bought the 151 unsold apartments and put most of them back on the market for sale. 
 In February, Arthur W. Zeckendorf and his brother, William Lie Zeckendorf, along with their partners, Starwood Capital Group and the Whitehall Street Real Estate Fund, which is managed by Goldman, Sachs & Company, bought the units, 100 of them unoccupied, for more than $71 million in cash. They range from 650-square-foot studios to 2,000-square-foot four-bedroom apartments, and they can be combined. Since sales reopened two weeks ago, 50 units have been sold at an average price of $500 per square foot. The partners paid $279 a square foot for the apartments, Arthur Zeckendorf said.
The $500-a-square-foot sales price is significantly higher than the $350 per square foot the units were offered for almost a decade ago. But it is consistent with the price of existing Upper East Side condominiums and lower than that of new ones, which are selling for an average of $800 to $900 a square foot, brokers said.
The seller in the Gotham deal, which also included 50,000 square feet of retail space, was the original lender, Fuji Bank, which held a loan of $90 million on the 27-story building, and Tobishima Properties of Manhattan. Tobishima developed the $200 million tower with partners, including Arthur and William Lie Zeckendorf's father, William Zeckendorf Jr., but ultimately bought out their interests after the Gotham opened in 1992 and had sold 88 units before the market soured and buyers vanished.
Since then, the unsold apartments have been rented, with rare exceptions. Last year -- with the economic crisis in Asia, the building's loan due to mature in 1999 and the condominium market improving -- Tobishima and Fuji decided to stop renewing leases and sell all 151 unsold units as a group, rather than sell them to individual buyers.
"The bank was ready to exit the project," said Darcy A. Stacom, an executive director at Cushman & Wakefield, the national brokerage company that handled the sale for the seller. As one of the last large blocks of units to come onto the market, the sale "drew heated bidding from investors hungry for new product," she said, adding that the new owners had outbid 12 other investors.
William Lie Zeckendorf said that buying the units "in a well-located building we knew gave us the ability to get out of the gate quickly and offer product at a price well below the cost of new construction."
Arthur Zeckendorf added, "It also offers instantaneous feedback to buyers who can move into the units within 20 days." The brothers are co-presidents of Zeckendorf Realty, a real estate services company that had managed the Gotham and still does. The company is also the project's sales agent.
Timing is also on the side of the sales campaign, since demand outstrips supply for condominiums and co-ops in Manhattan in general and on the Upper East Side in particular, brokers said. There are "multiple offers for one property, bidding wars and overbidding," said Joanne Douglas, a vice president at the Corcoran Group, a Manhattan brokerage. She added that the Gotham, which "has nice-sized apartments at competitive prices, should do well."
Prices at the Gotham, which have been raised an average of 5.5 percent since sales began two weeks ago, range from $237,000 for a studio to $1.4 million for a four-bedroom apartment. The average price for a 1,400-square-foot, two-bedroom is $675,000 and is $880,000 for a 1,706-square-foot three-bedroom.
Arthur Zeckendorf said the apartments at the Gotham, many with terraces or balconies and some on two levels, were being marketed to young single professionals and couples, empty-nesters and families. He said the remaining 51 units would be put on the market in the next nine months as their leases expired.
Among the amenities in the building are indoor and outdoor children's play areas, a fitness center and an indoor pool. Monthly maintenance charges are $349 to $1,692 and real estate taxes, thanks to a city abatement that is seven years into its 10-year life, are $134 to $1,161, a month. When the abatement ends in 2003, monthly taxes will rise to $189 to $1,627.
Doreen and Hernando Courtright, who have two children, are moving from their three-bedroom rental across the street to a four-bedroom at the Gotham, created by combining two units. "Last year, we looked in East Side neighborhoods in the 70's and 80's, but we thought prices were prohibitive," Mrs. Courtright said. "Then we saw that the units at the Gotham were available and the price was in our ball park."

LENGTH: 1176 words

HEADLINE: Wage Growth In '99 Below Expectations

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
Despite declining unemployment and a shortage of workers in some industries, wage and benefit increases slowed markedly in the first three months of the year, the Government reported today.
The Government's broadest gauge of compensation, the employment cost index, rose just four-tenths of 1 percent in the first quarter, the Labor Department said. The quarterly increase -- the smallest since the index was created in 1982 -- was half what economists had been predicting, and showed that compensation growth has now slowed for two consecutive quarters even as joblessness has declined to a 29-year low of 4.2 percent. 
 The slowing was driven to some degree by a steep decline in bonuses paid to Wall Street executives following last fall's financial market turmoil and a decline in commissions earned by real estate agents as the housing market cooled.
A number of industries, including construction and retailing, showed accelerating wage gains, indicating that tight labor markets are benefiting some workers. And as they have since 1996, workers are still enjoying wage gains after accounting for inflation, a turnabout from long stretches in both the 1980's and 1990's when wages were stagnant or falling in inflation-adjusted terms.
But the overall slowing trend in compensation costs was pronounced in the first quarter, analysts said, and was in line with a slowdown in another key wage indicator, hourly earnings. With inflation dormant, the trend highlighted a conundrum that has economists and policy makers at the Federal Reserve scratching their heads: Why has the combination of steady growth and low unemployment not forced a run-up in wages and then prices as history and traditional economic models suggest it should?
"It does not appear to be a fluke," said Stuart G. Hoffman, an economist at the PNC Corporation in Pittsburgh. "No matter how you slice it or dice it, the story is the same, that total compensation and wages in particular have actually been rising more slowly in the face of a declining unemployment rate over the last several quarters."
The report suggested that there were no inflationary pressures building in the economy and that compensation costs were unlikely to squeeze corporate profits. Bond prices and blue-chip stocks rallied, pushing the Dow Jones industrial average up 32.93 points, to 10,878.38, its third consecutive record close.
The four-tenths of a percent increase in the employment cost index in the first quarter followed a seven-tenths of a percent rise in the fourth quarter of last year and a nine-tenths of a percent jump in each of the two previous quarters.
The figures showed a 3 percent increase in wage and benefit costs for private industry in this year's first quarter compared with the same period last year. That was down from a 3.5 percent year-to-year comparison in last year's fourth quarter and a 3.8 percent increase in the third quarter.
Wage growth in private industry was up 3.3 percent in the first quarter compared with the period a year earlier.
Overall compensation costs were also held down by a decline in the growth of benefit costs. Benefit costs were up just three-tenths of a percent in the first quarter, half the rate of the previous quarter, belying predictions that resurgent medical costs would lead to increased health care expenses for businesses.
To a large extent, economists said, the explanation for the slowdown in compensation appears to rest with the realization among employees that inflation is no longer eating away at the purchasing power of their paychecks. Consumer prices last year rose only 1.5 percent, meaning that even a 3 percent wage increase still puts them ahead of the game.
"We're seeing nominal wages being pulled back down by lower inflation expectations," said James F. O'Sullivan, an economist at the Morgan Guaranty Trust Company. "Both workers and companies were surprised by how low inflation was last year, due in part to lower oil prices."
With wages in private industry having grown 3.9 percent last year and inflation at 1.5 percent, workers enjoyed a real wage increase of 2.4 percent last year, the best in two decades, Mr. O'Sullivan said.
At the same time, companies are making extra efforts to hold down their costs because intense global competition has left them with little ability to raise prices to maintain their profit margins. When they do agree to wage increases, they often demand flexibility from workers in helping the company be more efficient and productive.
Increases in the last several years in the rate of productivity growth have helped companies to absorb higher compensation costs without putting undue pressure on either prices or profits. Economists are debating whether the absence of inflation results from temporary factors like low oil prices, cheap imports and weak economies in much of the rest of the world, or some fundamental improvement in the economy's capacity to grow and create new jobs without igniting inflation.
At the Wisconsin Central Transportation Corporation, a small rail line serving the upper Midwest, 300 locomotive engineers recently approved a union contract that gives them a 3 percent pay increase this year and 2 percent next year. The pay increase was coupled with an agreement to formalize flexible staffing rules that allow the rail line, which is trying to build a business on tracks abandoned as unprofitable by bigger carriers, to respond to the needs of its customers.
"The wage increases we're talking about here are above the rate of inflation," said Ann G. Thoma, a spokeswoman for the railroad. The local union representative, Ken Davis, said he was "very pleased with the result."
When the United Steelworkers of America agreed to a deal with the RMI Titanium Company in Niles, Ohio, this month, it concentrated on winning improvements in its pension package and in job security. The pay increase was 50 cents an hour for five years, or a little over 3 percent a year for a worker earning $14.50 an hour. RMI is a unit of USX.
"It's a modest wage increase that keeps us up with inflation," said Ray Raschilla, the unit chairman of the union's Local 2155-7. "We had a lot of issues that needed to be addressed quickly, and to be honest it would have been too expensive to hit every area."
Wage increases are being held down, especially in manufacturing, by a persistent fear among workers about losing their jobs despite the strong economy, said Calvin A. Campbell Jr., chairman and chief executive of Goodman Equipment, a maker of mining machinery in Bedford Park, Ill. Unemployment may be low but the pace of layoffs in many industries is high, he said.
"Obviously sometimes you have to find someone with a particular talent, and you may see some wage pressure there," said Mr. Campbell, who is chairman of the National Association of Manufacturers. "But that's in the minority. Growth is slowing and a lot of companies will be cutting back or not expanding. Fewer workers will have a tendency or desire to jump jobs."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place;
Investors Show More Interest In REIT Stocks

BYLINE: By RICHARD A. OPPEL Jr. 

 BODY:
Until now, Warren E. Buffett's best-known real estate gamble was on a bank.
In 1990, his Berkshire Hathaway bought a big stake in Wells Fargo amid a California real estate slump. The bank did not break out of the real estate quagmire for two years, and Mr. Buffett later acknowledged that he had not foreseen the severity of the problem even though his investment ultimately paid off handsomely. 
 Now, the Omaha billionaire is betting on real estate again. And investors are left to wonder if he has correctly spotted the bottom -- this time in real estate stocks -- or if he is early again.
Mr. Buffett has spent more than $50 million, much of it in personal funds, on shares of four REIT's, with three purchases disclosed this month. REIT's, the publicly traded companies that own shopping centers, apartment complexes, office buildings and other properties, have been in the doldrums even as rents and real estate values strengthen across much of the United States.
But they are coming to life. News of Mr. Buffett's purchases, and a broad rebound in cyclical stocks that tend to soar with the economy, has pushed REIT indexes about 10 percent higher in the last month, the first good news for real estate shareholders since the sector peaked in 1997.
Despite the sudden shift, real estate stocks look cheap by some measures. For example, the stocks in the Standard & Poor's big company index are trading at about 34 times their earnings. For the last dozen years or so, that multiple has been about 1.5 times the average REIT's funds from operations, an industry measure similar to cash flow. The stock multiple is now more than three times the real estate average.
Investors pulled money out of real estate mutual funds every week for four months, but reversed course in the week ended April 21, pouring about $80 million into these funds, according to AMG Data Services, an Arcata, Calif., mutual fund researcher.
Part of the attraction is the relatively high yields -- on average, at least two percentage points more than on Treasury notes. Wall Street analysts anticipate double-digit profit growth at many real estate trusts this year. This means yields will remain strong, since REIT's pay out 95 percent of their taxable income to shareholders every year to avoid taxes.
REIT's could still get hurt if the economy stumbles. But few analysts expect problems anytime soon. Corporate earnings continue to be surprisingly strong, and many economists have pronounced the fallout from the financial crises in Asia, Russia and Brazil to have passed. Meanwhile, commodity prices have stabilized -- a bullish sign for real estate and other assets that perform well with higher inflation.
The current environment is similar to that at the bottoms of the last six REIT cycles, says Bob Steers, co-manager of the Cohen & Steers Realty Shares fund. Over the last three decades, he says, the average bear market for real estate stocks lasted 14 months and share prices declined by about one-quarter. Before the April rally, the stocks had been in a slump for a little longer and had declined a bit more.
"When the group is ready to move, it's ready to move because it has been oversold," Mr. Steers said..
Among the big real estate funds without high minimum investment requirements, top performers for the three years ended in March include Longleaf Partners Realty, with a 10.7 percent annualized return; Cohen & Steers Realty Shares, 9.3 percent; Fidelity Real Estate Investment Portfolio, 8.4 percent, and CGM Realty, 8.3 percent. All figures are from Morningstar Inc., the fund tracking company in Chicago.
Even after the recent rally, analysts say shares in REIT's are mostly trading for less than the total that their properties would sell for piecemeal. As a result, some real estate trusts are diverting cash that would normally be used to buy properties to buy back their stock. A few have even sold assets and used the proceeds for stock buybacks. Archstone Communities, a REIT in Englewood, Colo., which owns apartment buildings, sold property and repurchased $84.4 million in stock in February and March.
Since the beginning of last year, REIT's have announced that they will buy back $2.9 billion of their stock, compared with just $861 million in announced buybacks during the previous eight years combined, according to Thomson Financial Securities Data in Newark. CRA Real Estate Securities, a money manager in Radnor, Pa., counts 36 REIT's that have announced buybacks since last summer, and those have so far repurchased, on average, about 44 percent of the shares authorized.
Some managers are doing more than partial buybacks. An investor group led by the company's chairman recently won a battle to acquire Berkshire Realty outright for $12.25 a share, or a 26 percent premium to the stock's price before the buyout offer. And a group including the chief executive of Sunstone Hotel Investors, a hotel operator based in San Clemente, Calif., has offered to acquire the company for $9.50 to $10 a share, about 35 percent more than what the stock sold for earlier this year.
Meanwhile, Mr. Buffett recently disclosed that he had increased his stake in MGI Properties, a Boston real estate trust, to 10.3 percent, as the company nears liquidation. MGI's management decided last year that it should sell everything and distribute the cash to shareholders because of its low stock market valuation. The sale is now expected to generate $29 to $30 a share, almost one-third more than MGI shares fetched last summer, when the plan was disclosed. Shares closed yesterday unchanged at $27.5625.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Nasdaq Slump Casts a Pall Over the Broader Market

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
Nasdaq stocks slumped for the third consecutive session yesterday as investors took profits in high-technology shares, casting a further pall over the market in general.
While some money was redeployed into issues sensitive to the business cycle, helping push the Dow Jones industrial average up 32.93 points, or three-tenths of a percent, to 10,878.38, its fifth record in seven trading sessions, traders and analysts seemed increasingly cautious. 
 "If technology continues to erode, I don't think there's much to bail the market out," said John R. Peluso, head of listed equity trading at Lehman Brothers.
The Nasdaq composite index fell 21.93 points, or nine-tenths of a percent, to 2,528.44. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 8.08 points, or six-tenths of a percent, to 1,342.83.
Contributing to the sober mood was the fact that stocks responded scarcely at all to a Government report that employers have been exceptionally successful in holding down wage and benefit increases, a development that also bodes well for the Federal Reserve's efforts to keep inflation at bay.
Interest rates retreated along a wide front but the stock market seemed oblivious. "You had an incredibly bullish employment cost index which in most other times would have shot the market up a couple hundred points," said Thomas M. Galvin, chief strategist at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette.
Moreover, Mr. Galvin found that a favorite short-term indicator showed the market to be "overbought" because the percentage of New York Stock Exchange issues trading above their 10-week moving average climbed above 70 percent at this week's calculation. Last week it was 64 percent.
"Stocks to me are beginning to look tired," he said, predicting prices will stay in a tight range for the next six to eight weeks until the trend of second-quarter corporate profits becomes clearer.
Even so, financial stocks rallied. J. P. Morgan rose 2 3/16 , to 138; Citigroup increased 2, to 76 5/16 , and American Express climbed 2 5/16 , to 135 3/8, accounting for the bulk of the Dow's gain. At one stage the blue-chip gauge, which was paced by Exxon's 3-point surge, to 84 1/2, had been up by nearly 83 points.
Some cyclical stocks moved higher, with Alcoa reaching a new high of 65 5/8 before closing up 2 11/16 , to 64 11/16 . But some other stocks sensitive to the business cycle, like International Paper and DuPont, gave back some recent gains amid predictions that the recent popularity of this group would wane. International Paper fell 1 1/8, to 56 1/4; DuPont slid 3/4, to 72 3/4.
"The rotation into cyclicals is going to prove to be short-lived," said Philip J. Orlando, chief investment officer at Value Line Asset Management. He doubted they could sustain earnings growth in a disinflationary climate.
Among high-technology shares, Amazon.com skidded 25 1/4, to 168 1/4, after losing 12 3/8 on Wednesday. The biggest Internet retailer warned of wider losses as it spends heavily on promotion and new services. America Online eased another 1 5/8, to 141 3/8, as investors took profits after its strong profit report.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Treasury Prices Rise
 (By Bloomberg News)
Bond prices rallied after a Government report showed the smallest quarterly rise in labor costs on record, suggesting inflation will not accelerate soon.
The price of the benchmark 30-year bond rose 25/32 , to 96 1/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 5.52 percent from 5.58 percent. Yields on two-year notes fell to 4.95 percent from 5.01 percent.
Traders bought bonds after a report showed the employment cost index -- considered one of the best measures of wage and benefit costs -- rose four-tenths of a percent in the first quarter, the smallest increase since the Government began tracking the figure in 1982.
The report signaled that economic growth and a tight labor market are not creating a big rise in job costs that could lead to higher prices on goods.
Other investors are loading up on higher-yielding corporate debt as the vigor of the economy brightens the outlook for company profits and credit quality.
The Sprint Corporation led borrowers that sold about $5 billion of debt yesterday. The nation's No. 3 long-distance phone company sold $3.5 billion of bonds, increasing its sale by 17 percent because of strong demand.

HEADLINE: U.S. Approves Deal for Taiwan To Buy Radar to Monitor China

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
The United States has decided to sell an early-warning radar system to Taiwan that will allow it to monitor the launch of Chinese ballistic missiles or manned bombers, Clinton Administration officials said today.
The decision has already drawn protests from Beijing and was opposed by a group of mid-level Administration officials who argued that it would worsen recent tensions between Washington and Beijing. 
 The Administration approved the sale at the recommendation of senior policy makers from the White House, the State Department and the Pentagon, who said that China's deployment of large numbers of short-range missiles along its coastline posed a serious military threat to Taiwan, officials said.
The Defense Department, in a report to Congress earlier this year, said: "Taiwan's most significant vulnerability is its limited capacity to defend against the growing arsenal of Chinese ballistic missiles."
The radar system, which could be worth hundreds of millions of dollars in business to American defense contractors, will give Taiwan several minutes' warning of the launch of ballistic missiles on the mainland.
"I'm pleased that the Administration has finally recognized that it is appropriate to help the Taiwanese protect themselves," said Representative Benjamin A. Gilman, who heads the House International Affairs Committee. "If China is pointing missiles at Taiwan, I would expect Taiwan to try to defend itself."
Earlier this month, Mr. Gilman, a New York Republican, threatened to introduce legislation to permit the sale if the Clinton Administration refused to approve it.
He said in an interview that the Chinese had no reason to be concerned about the sale of the radar "because they're the ones putting the missiles along the coastline in the first place."
The spokesman for the Chinese Embassy in Washington, Yu Shuning, said that the embassy had already issued a formal protest to the Clinton Administration over the decision. The deal was first reported earlier this week by news organizations in Taiwan.
"We have made serious representations with the U.S. side," Mr. Yu said. "We say that any arms sales to Taiwan by any country in the world constitutes an infringement on Chinese sovereignty, an interference in our internal affairs."
Beijing considers Taiwan a breakaway province and fears that early warning systems could become part of an anti-missile defense system proposed by Washington that could shelter Taiwan.
Administration officials said opposition to the sale of the radar was voiced by mid-level officers at the White House and State Department who said that it would needlessly provoke China at a time when tensions are already high over trade disagreements as well as allegations of Chinese nuclear espionage in the United States.
But they were overruled. A senior Administration official, speaking on condition of anonymity, said that top policy makers at the White House, the State Department and the Pentagon all agreed that the sale should be approved.
"I think there was a fairly good meeting of the minds on this," the official said. "We have told the Taiwanese that we will meet their legitimate defensive needs. And a passive early-warning system is clearly a legitimate defensive need."
Administration officials said there was still no agreement with Taiwan over exactly what type of radar system would be approved, which companies would build it, and how much it would cost.
"This was an agreement in principle," the senior official said. "There wasn't a decision on a specific system." The contractors thought likely to be asked to bid on the contract include Lockheed Martin, Raytheon and Thomson-CSF of France.
Taiwan's Government said in a report in February that it had determined through intelligence sources that China had deployed more than 100 ballistic missiles that could be fired across the Taiwan Strait at the island. The Taiwanese have cited the intelligence in arguing the need for development of a missile-defense system.
The agreement on the radar system was reached during talks with senior Taiwan military officers who were in Washington this week on an annual arms-buying tour.
Under the 1979 Taiwan Relations Act, which was passed by Congress after the United States broke off formal diplomatic relations with Taiwan and established ties to Beijing, the United States agreed to provide the island with the weapons to defend itself from attack by Beijing.
The Taiwan Government had no comment today on the radar sale. A spokesman for the Taipei Economic and Cultural Representative Office in Washington, which serves as a de facto embassy for Taiwan here, said that the Clinton Administration had requested that it not issue any statement on the agreement.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
Spies and Character

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
How come they can do it right here in America and how come our Government does not take action when the crimes are discovered?
The woman next to me at dinner was asking the question all Americans should ask about China's continuous and successful nuclear espionage against the U.S. -- how come? 
 So far only one or two people have been named as suspected spies, who got hold of thousands of pages of secret American information on nuclear weapons -- one of the great espionage coups of our time.
But it is almost comical -- any belief that one or two men could find out exactly where the information was, what data to search for and in what priority, how to extract it from lab computers without getting caught, and the names and access codes of the companies in America that are fronts for the Chinese Government and Chinese Army.
Historians will be writing books about Beijing's counterparts to the Soviet spy rings in America, and the Cambridge treason crowd in Britain.
The Clinton Administration, which for years ignored early tips on the espionage, now stalls on releasing information gathered by a committee headed by Representative Christopher Cox of California. But I expect pretty soon the investigation teams of The Times and other papers will break the story of the spy rings plural and their contacts plural. Read the excellent article in the May issue of The American Spectator by Kenneth R. Timmerman, one of the experts read by other experts.
But still -- how come these made-in-China spies? The answers are essential not only to understanding the espionage case but the character -- the precise word -- of President Clinton's problems about other critical realities, from Lewinsky to Kosovo.
For almost all its time in office the Administration has preached and practiced a policy toward the Chinese Government that he had denounced before he was elected the first time but soon adopted, widened and made more dangerous to American interests and ideals.
The policy is that the only way to get China to behave itself, and not do nasty things like engaging in nuclear espionage and torturing political and religious prisoners, is to build up U.S.-China trade. Like his American business mentors and our foreign allies, the President calls it the policy of engagement.
It is, it is -- engagement with China's Communist Politburo, not with the Chinese people.
The Administration uses an old trick to fight American people and organizations who still want what he supported before assorted C.E.O.'s and political donors got to him -- the use of economic leverage to squeeze human rights concessions out of the Politburo. Mr. Clinton falsely accuses them of the fatal weakness of his own policy -- playing only one note on his saxophone.
When the Chinese take the trade money and tell the world to get lost about human rights, or go in for nuclear espionage in America, the President has a choice. He can remain a prisoner of Beijing by insisting he is right. Or, he can break out of his cage by admitting error and returning to the mixture of realism and idealism he had promised Americans, to get the votes of those who cared.
But somehow I doubt he will ever do that. I think he really believes that evasion, falsehood, stonewalling, listening to the music of the cash register and not of the soul, stalling -- about Monica or about espionage -- are the instruments of government and the path of self-preservation. I suppose they are, for him.
They worked to save him from impeachment. They were useful in helping Americans forget the promise he once spoke into my own little ears -- not to send troops to Bosnia.
They are not quite working to save him from world realization that neither he nor the other NATO brains ever thought that Slobodan Milosevic would be so crass as to use NATO bombing as the right moment to drive out the Albanians, turning the war into a disaster for Albanians, NATO, Americans and Serbs who positioned themselves under the bombs.
He will not change his character, nor the fact that character of a President determines character of Presidential policy. He has not yet said that espionage by his Chinese captors is good for America. But he has a year and a half of Presidential time left to figure how to break the news. 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: N.J. Prosecutor Is Accused Of 'Profligate' Overspending

BYLINE: By RONALD SMOTHERS 

DATELINE: NEWARK, April 29

 BODY:
Since taking office a year ago, the Essex County Prosecutor, Patricia A. Hurt, has increased staff salaries by more than $500,000 a year and purchased a huge $248,541 customized mobile command post, and is now asking the county for more money to cover extra expenses, top county officials said this week.
James W. Treffinger, the Essex County Executive, termed the spending "wasteful and profligate" and said that Ms. Hurt's office had already exceeded its current $23.4 million annual budget. 
 The charges of excessive spending come on the heels of widespread criticism of the way in which Ms. Hurt's office handled the investigation into the shooting of an Orange, N.J., policewoman on April 8. The Prosecutor's office arrested three men during the course of the investigation, including one who was held for six days and then cleared of charges and another who died in custody.
Mr. Treffinger, a Republican who once appointed Ms. Hurt as his top deputy, said of the money request that "there should be an investigation of such wasteful spending."
"Her contention is that it is needed to fight crime," he said, "but as many instances of profligate spending demonstrate, the money has been misused."
Purchase requisitions and other analyses over the last year reveal that Ms. Hurt has overspent her budget with the increase in staff salaries and the purchase of the command post: a recreational vehicle fitted out with radio equipment, fax machines, television, microwave oven and coffee maker. No other prosecutor's office in the state has such a vehicle, law enforcement officials said.
In addition, the invoices show that Ms. Hurt ordered new office appointments when she took over in January 1998, including a $120 leather wastebasket, a $496 leather blotter, and a $1,958.45 refrigerator with a cherry finish in Victorian style. In a building that has central air conditioning, she also ordered an $800 air conditioner on wheels.
Purchase orders obtained by The New York Times from county officials indicate also that in ordering her official vehicle, a Chevrolet Tahoe, she requested options that raised the cost nearly $7,000 over the base $26,500 price recommended in state contracts with vehicle vendors.
The office then caused an additonal $1,340 to be spent to remove some of the options initially requested. There was no indication on the purchase orders as to why the optional equipment was removed.
In addition, according to personnel records, Ms. Hurt hired Vincent C. Corrado, a longtime county employee without a law degree, as a $90,000-a-year chief of staff, a position not found in any other prosecutor's offices in the state, Mr. Treffinger said.
Repeated attempts to interview Ms. Hurt about the spending were unsuccessful. Her spokesman, Ray Weiss, told The Times that she would not grant an interview unless she received questions in writing, in advance.
Ms. Hurt has been in an extended budget battle with Mr. Treffinger and the Essex County Board of Freeholders since a short time after taking office early last year. At that time, she asked for additional money and received $1.4 million more than the initial allocation for the office. In her budget request for next year, Mr. Treffinger said, Ms. Hurt sought $4 million more than her current budget. Mr. Treffinger has refused the additional money and instead is directing her to stay within the $22 million budget that was in force the year before she took over.
"Last year I had several conversations with her to urge her to institute efficiencies and economies, and she took great offense to my suggestions," said Mr. Treffinger.
On three occasions, he said, the prosecutor sought the Attorney General's permission to institute a lawsuit to wring more money from the county budget, which was $506 million for the 1998-99 fiscal year, which ends in June.
Under New Jersey state law and court precedent, prosecutors are allowed to bring such lawsuits on the theory that their executive branch and law enforcement operations cannot be arbitrarily limited by county legislative bodies or county executives. But on all of those occasions, Ms. Hurt's requests to go into court were denied, said Mr. Treffinger, who added that he learned of the outcome from Ms. Hurt and others. The Attorney General's office was unable to confirm this allegation today.
The charges of excessive spending marked the sharpest political rift so far between Mr. Treffinger and Ms. Hurt, whom he had recommended to Gov. Christine Todd Whitman for the Essex County Prosecutor's post. By most accounts the relationship has been fraying for several months, not only over budgetary issues but also over the growing sense of disquiet about Ms. Hurt's management of the state's busiest prosecutor's office.

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: CONGRESS;
House G.O.P. Adds Billions for Military In Balkans Package

BYLINE: By TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
House Republicans moved today to add billions in military spending to the package the Administration had requested for NATO's air war, one day after denying the President political support to fight the war.
The Speaker, J. Dennis Hastert of Illinois, said "the Congress is deeply divided" over the fighting in the Balkans, a rare understatement during the skirmishing and sniping that erupted in Congress today over the political and economic costs of the attacks. 
 Tonight the House Appropriations Committee approved nearly $13 billion in emergency spending, $7 billion more than the White House sought to pay for military and relief costs in the Balkans. The committee chairman said the extra money was intended to address longstanding military needs and to "let the troops know we really support them."
The measure was labeled as "relating to the conflict in Kosovo." But President Clinton's budget director said it included billions for projects with "no relation to Kosovo."
Republicans pushing for the added spending, some of whom now speak of the conflict as "Clinton's war," said they saw no contradiction in voting to reject a measure supporting the fighting while voting for money to finance it.
They called the extra billions a small down payment toward beefing up the post-cold-war Pentagon. Some angrily accused Mr. Clinton and the Democrats of bleeding the American arsenal and leading the nation into a military morass. "These are the same chicken hawks who vote against defense all the time," Representative Tom DeLay of Texas, the majority whip, said in an interview. "Not only is their President getting us in a mess, but he's undermining our military."
Democrats bitterly accused Republicans of sabotaging foreign policy after Wednesday night's unexpected 213-to-213 House vote defeating a nonbinding resolution to express support for the bombing campaign. Democratic leaders, who had attached great symbolic importance to the measure, said the vote could encourage President Slobodan Milosevic to think the United States and its allies were weakening.
In a show of unity today, Secretary of Defense William S. Cohen and Gen. Hugh H. Shelton, Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, appeared at the regular Pentagon briefing to criticize the House vote and emphasize NATO "solidarity."
"I think a vote signaling less support on the air campaign is counterproductive," Mr. Cohen said. "But we will continue to wage this effort and we will continue to take this case to the American people, and we will continue to go after Milosevic until such time as we achieve our goals."
The minority leader, Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, said the vote marked "a low moment in American foreign policy" and "a betrayal of everything America stands for." To follow that vote with a move to add billions to military spending was hypocrisy, he said.
"It is mind-boggling that some of the same members that voted against the operation will today vote to double the spending," he said.
In the House Appropriations Committee, Republicans and a handful of Democrats who usually support increases in military spending approved a $12.9 billion emergency bill. It more than doubled the $5.1 billion Mr. Clinton sought to pay for military operations in the Balkans through Sept. 30. His request also included money to pay for the patrolling and bombing of Iraq.
In an interview Jack Lew, Mr. Clinton's budget director, said House Republicans were "going too far."
"They're exaggerating the costs of the Kosovo operation and they're funding things that meet no one's definition of an emergency," he said.
The House spending bill added nearly half a billion dollars in bombs and missiles the Pentagon did not request. It would also finance more than $1 billion in military construction projects, mostly in Europe, and $1.8 billion in pay increases for military personnel.
The law says emergency spending must be restricted to "unanticipated" and "immediate" needs. The chairman of the Appropriations Committee, Representative C. W. Bill Young of Florida, said the bill met pressing military demands, would cover hidden costs of the Kosovo conflict and would show that Congress "stands behind our troops."
"The Administration's record in anticipating the costs of military deployments is pretty sad," he said. "We were supposed to be in the Balkans for a year, at a cost of $1.5 billion. Five years later, it's $9 billion."
The bill could be voted on by the full House next week, he said. A Senate vote would come the following week.
If such a bill arrives on the President's desk, he does not have to take it or leave it, Mr. Young said. He can decide that any given item within the bill is not an emergency and withhold his approval for that project. The money would go unspent.
"It all depends on what your definition of 'emergency' is," Mr. Young said. "That's a phrase that should be familiar to the White House."
The Senate is also struggling over two bills that would finance the conflict and urge the President to achieve victory over Yugoslavia.
The Senate Appropriations Committee has drafted a $12 billion spending bill for the war. But at least six senators, all fiscal conservatives, say they are loath to vote for a bill far in excess of the President's request, especially since the money would come from excess Social Security revenues, which they have sworn to protect.
And the Senate Foreign Relations Committee today canceled a meeting to consider a resolution offered by a bipartisan group led by John R. McCain, an Arizona Republican, and Joseph R. Biden Jr., a Delaware Democrat, that would authorize the President to use "all necessary means" to win the war -- an authority Mr. Clinton has not sought.
Mr. McCain, a presidential candidate, is invoking a provision of the 1973 War Powers act that could guarantee a vote on the Senate floor next week. But the Senate majority leader, Trent Lott of Mississippi, is seeking to delay that vote, his aides said. He is also exploring alternatives, like the measure passed by the House on Wednesday, blocking the President from undertaking a ground war without a go-ahead from Congress.
Nancy Ives, an aide to Senator McCain, said he would wait for two weeks, as long as he is guaranteed a vote on the use of force in the war.

INTERNATIONAL A3-17
 
Bombing Unites Serb Army As It Debilitates Economy
The NATO bombing that was intended to cripple and demoralize Slobodan Milosevic's military machine has instead invigorated the Yugoslav Army and has helped him heal his long-poisoned relationship with the officer corps, according to American and NATO officials. But the raids are destroying the country's civilian industrial capacity and public works, including its highway, rail and communications networks. A1 

House Republicans moved to add billions in military spending to the bill for NATO's air war, the day after denying President Clinton the political support to fight it. A1
 
NATO expressed regret that a missile had strayed into the outskirts of Bulgaria's capital, Sofia; no one was reported killed. The Bulgarian Parliament is preparing to vote on whether to give permission to NATO to fly over the country for operations against Yugoslavia. A12
 
A Russian-led peace initiative for Kosovo appeared to make little headway, with Moscow and the West divided over the composition of any international force in Kosovo. A12

NATIONAL A18-28
 
Mars Magnetism Suggests A Geology Like Earth's
An ancient magnetic pattern in the surface of Mars appears to show that the planet in an early epoch churned with internal heat and other powerful forces, remarkably like the geology of Earth today, scientists said. The finding added to a body of evidence suggesting that Mars and Earth started out much alike, then evolved into very different worlds. A1
 
Parasites, Not Pollution
At least some of the frog deformities that symbolize fears of an ever more poisoned environment are caused not by toxic substances but by parasitic worms, scientists said. A22
 
Home Test for Liver Virus
The F.D.A. has approved the first device for home testing for the dangerous liver virus hepatitis C, which affects about 4 million Americans. A24
 
'Dolphin-Safe' Rule Changed
The Commerce Department is broadening the definition of "dolphin-safe" tuna to include fish caught by a method banned in 1990. The decision drew outrage from many environmental groups. A18
 
Link to School Gun Explored
Eric Harris and Dylan Klebold got the high-powered, semiautomatic pistol used in the Columbine High School massacre with the help of a fellow pizza parlor employee, law enforcement officials in Littleton, Colo., said. The worker, who officials believe acted as an intermediary, has not yet been charged. The Denver Post said the owner of the gun said a former employee had sold it for him at a gun show. A26
 
The idea that juveniles and adult criminals buy their weapons from legitimate sources is a finding developed only in the past three years and contradicts what was the widely accepted view, that they steal their guns. A26
 
The last of the victims' funerals was held, for Isaiah Shoels, a 4-foot, 11-inch football player and the only black victim. A26
 
A 14-year-old boy was charged with murder in Canada, a pipe bomb was found in an Oklahoma school, five Brooklyn youths were charged with conspiracy, a New Jersey school district ordered closings because of E-mail threats, and districts across the nation were on heightened alert. Nine days after the Littleton shootings, there were new reports of threats, of schools evacuated, of police officers patrolling hallways, of young people being arrested or suspended on suspicion of plotting to blow up their schools or to kill or maim classmate they considered enemies. But actual violence and real bombs have been rare, law-enforcement officials and educators say, and what has been stalking the schools is an inordinate fear of death. A1

Payroll Costs Barely Budge
Wage and benefit increases slowed markedly in the first three months of the year, despite declining unemployment and a shortage of workers in some industries. The Labor Department said the employment cost index, the Government's broadest gauge of compensation, rose just four-tenths of one percent in the first quarter, the smallest increase since the index was created in 1982. HEADLINE: A Learning Experience For a Prominent Visitor

BYLINE: By VIVIAN S. TOY 

 BODY:
Cheyenne Phillips, a kindergartner at Public School 107 in Flushing, Queens, rushed through her lunch yesterday, chewing almost nonstop, in anticipation of a matinee performance of "Peter Pan." She had never been to a Broadway show before. "But I saw a commercial for it on TV," she quickly offered.
Her benefactor was a man in a gray suit, Michael Goldstein, who paid for tickets for the school's disabled children as well as for her mobile prone stander -- a contraption with big wheels that allows her to move around while standing upright. Cheyenne cannot walk or stand because of spina bifida. 
 While it is primarily a neighborhood school, about 140 of its 1,100 children come from throughout Queens and have physical or learning disabilities. Mr. Goldstein, the chairman of Toys "R" Us, hooked up with P.S. 107 three years ago when he was asked to serve as Principal for a Day, in a program that links celebrities, politicians and business leaders with public schools across the city to promote public education.
Yesterday, 1,050 "principals," ranging from the actors Frances McDormand and Billy Baldwin to Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and Hillary Rodham Clinton, spent the day in one of 880 New York City schools.
Officials at Public Education Needs Civic Involvement in Learning, or Pencil, the organization that has run Principal for a Day since 1994, said that many of the people who have acted as principals have developed ongoing relationships with their schools. Some, like Mr. Goldstein, have written checks. Others, like Norma Kamali, the designer, and Mickey Drexler, the chief executive officer of the Gap, have created student internships.
Many do nothing more than return for commencement at the end of the year. And there is an occasional gaffe, like the time Donald J. Trump offered to buy students free Nike sneakers, prompting one to ask why he was giving shoes when he could afford scholarships.
"While we encourage people to give, we certainly don't expect anything," said Lisa Belzberg, president of Pencil, who estimated that principals for a day have donated tens of millions of dollars. "Our notion is that the system is enhanced by contact with the private sector and vice versa. And if all it means is you look at a kid on the street differently the next day, then we've done our job."
If that is the case, she can consider Mr. Goldstein a huge success.
Mr. Goldstein is a product of the New York public schools himself: he is a graduate of Jamaica High School, and his two daughters, now adults, attended public schools in Queens and in Rye in Westchester County. One is now a guidance counselor in the New York City school system.
He has flipped from being a careful skeptic three years ago to a believer in the system. "I expected the worst," he said. "I thought there would be chaos and teachers who were working but not giving their all, and instead I saw just the opposite."
So in addition to helping develop the school's program for disabled children, Mr. Goldstein proselytizes his corporate buddies on the need to contribute to public schools. "It'll take money to get the class sizes down," he said, "and with the economy doing so well, this is the time to do it."
Mr. Goldstein has donated about $35,000 -- a combination of his own money and funds from the Toys "R" Us foundation, the Children's Benefit Fund -- toward equipment and programs for the disabled children at P.S. 107.
The children in Gale Criss's fourth-grade class bounced with energy yesterday when he walked into the room and started to talk toys. Dozens of hands shot up when he asked about favorite toys. "Ooh, Pokemon!" "Star Wars!" "Nintendo!" Mr. Goldstein listened attentively as the children heatedly debated the merits of Nintendo 64 versus Sony's Playstation.
He smiled sheepishly when some children asked for an autograph as he headed out the door. "They don't need my autograph," he said. "I just enjoy being with them."
And he's not ashamed to take careful note of the children's opinions on toys. "We spend millions on market research," he said. "But they're the ones who really know."

HEADLINE: Repsol Pursues Buyout of Argentine Oil Concern

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
In a deal that would create the world's eighth-largest oil company, Repsol S.A., Spain's largest oil company, said yesterday that it was offering $13.4 billion in cash to buy the 85 percent of YPF, the Argentine oil company, it does not already own.
Repsol, which paid $2 billion in January for 15 percent of YPF, said it would pay $44.78 a share in cash for the rest of YPF, a price that Repsol said was a 25.4 percent premium over yesterday's closing price for YPF shares on the Buenos Aires stock exchange. 
 Whether that price will be high enough for the bid to succeed was questioned by several oil analysts yesterday afternoon as rumors about the deal, which had been circulating for weeks, drew attention on Wall Street a few hours ahead of the announcement by Repsol. The rising price of oil -- $18.53 a barrel yesterday in New York, up from $11.78 just 10 weeks ago -- should make YPF shares rise in value, these analysts said.
The combined company would have half the gasoline-station market in Argentina and would be a major force in Peru, Ecuador and Brazil, Alfonso Cortina, Repsol's chief executive, said last night in a telephone call from his office in Madrid.
"We are going to create an integrated, world-class oil company," Mr. Cortina said, with enough cash flow that the company will be able to pay back the added debt it will take on to make the purchase by the end of 2000.
Mr. Cortina said that "we can commit to our shareholders to increase our earnings per share roughly 20 percent from 1998 to 2002" and that return on assets should rise from 11 percent now to 15 percent by 2002.
The new company will be highly leveraged at first, with 30 percent equity and 70 percent debt, most of it an unsecured bridge loan at floating interest rates that makes the company vulnerable to a sudden upturn in interest rates or a sharp drop in the price of oil. By the end of 2000, however, the company's debt should be between 48 percent and 50 percent of the combined company's capital structure, Mr. Cortina said. To pare down debt, he said, about $2.5 billion worth of YPF assets will be sold and a new issue of stock, intended to raise $4 billion to $6 billion, is being arranged with Goldman, Sachs, Merrill Lynch and Salomon Smith Barney as the lead underwriters.
YPF, whose formal name is Yacimientos Petroliferos Fiscales, was founded in 1922 as the first state-owned oil company outside the Soviet Union. For seven decades it was known as the only oil company in the world that lost money as Argentine dictators drained it of both products and cash to subsidize other state-owned enterprises and to prop up unrelated YPF businesses, from hospitals to movie theaters.
YPF was privatized between 1990 and 1993. Its payroll of 50,000 workers was cut to 10,000, and unprofitable operations were sold. YPF is already the largest private oil company in Latin America and the largest private company of any kind in Argentina.
Mr. Cortina acknowledged that there was strong opposition in the management of YPF to Repsol taking charge, but he said he was confident that he could win over the key personnel.

HEADLINE: METRO NEWS BRIEFS: NEW YORK;
Unions to Protest Cuts, Vouchers and Workfare

 BODY:
New York City union leaders announced yesterday that they would hold a rally on May 12 to protest the budget policies of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and Gov. George E. Pataki.
At a news conference on the City Hall steps, the leaders said the rally would protest Mr. Giuliani's workfare program and his plan to use vouchers to send students to private schools, as well as Mr. Pataki's plans to reduce spending on education and health care. They said the rally, to be held outside City Hall during the evening rush, would attract tens of thousands of union members, including hundreds bused in from upstate. 
 "It is time for Mayor Giuliani to stop slashing the budget and reinvest city surpluses in services and programs that will benefit both our economy and working families," said Brian M. McLaughlin, president of the New York City Central Labor Council.
Dennis Rivera, president of 1199, the National Health and Human Services Employees Union, said, "We will be marching to say no to Giuliani and Pataki's attempt to cut funding for schools, health care and jobs."
